Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Ninian Martin Stephen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of
the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight Commander of the Most Excellent Order of the British Empire, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of
the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: FIFTH PERIOD)
Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 140 of 1982)An Act to amend the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 consequent upon the enactment of the Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982.
Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 70 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Act 1963.
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 77 of 1982)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Air Navigation Legislation (Validation and Interpretation) Act 1982 (Act No. 95 of 1982)An Act to make provision in relation to the exercise of certain powers and the performance of certain functions under regulations and instruments under the Air Navigation Act 1920 and for related purposes.
Appropriation Act (No. I) 1982-83 (Act No. 1160£]982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1982-83 (Act No. 117 of 1982)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83 (Act No. 118 of 1982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service, in relation to the Parliamentary Departments, of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Asian Development Fund Act 1982 (Act No. lSI of 1982)An Act to authorize a further contribution by Australia to the Asian Development Bank for the purposes of the Asian Development Fund.
Australian Bicentennial Road Development Trust Fund Act 1982 (Act No. 133 of 1982)An Act to establish a Trust Fund for the purpose of the grant of financial assistance to the States and to the Northern Territory for expenditure on upgrading Australian roads in celebration of the Bicentenary in 1988, and for related purposes.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 126 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 125 of 1982)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 124 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981.
Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 127 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Bank Account Debits Tax Act 1982 (Act No. 141 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks.
Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982 (Act No. 142 of 1982)An Act relating to the collection of a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Berry Fruits) Act 1982 (Act No. 132 of 1982)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain berry fruits.
Bounty (Ships) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 145 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Bounty (Ships) Act 1980.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 154 of 1982)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. ISS of 1982)- An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964.
Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 71 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Act 1959.
Conciliation and Arbitration (Management of Organizations) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 143 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.
Cotton Levy Act 1982 (Act No. 147 of 1982)An Act to impose a levy upon certain cotton produced in Australia.
Cotton Research Act 1982 (Act No. 146 of 1982)An Act to provide for the collection of levy imposed by the Cotton Levy Act 1982 and to establish a Cotton Research Trust Account.
Customs and Excise Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 81 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Excise Act 1901.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Customs Securities (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 137 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 in relation to certain Customs securities, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff Act 1982 (Act No. 113 of 1982)- An Act relating to duties of Customs.
Customs Tariff (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1982 (Act No. 115 of 1982)An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Customs Tariff Act 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 114 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 consequent upon the enactment of the Customs TariffAct 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 136 of 1982)An Act to repeal section 140fthe Customs Tariff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment (Countervailing Duties) Act 1982 (Act No. 68 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 in relation to countervailing duties.
Defence Force (Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1982 (Act No. 153 of 1982)An Act to amend certain Acts, and to enact transitional provisions, consequent upon the enactment of the Defence Force Discipline Act 1982, and for other purposes.
Defence Force Discipline Act 1982 (Act No. 1520f 1982)An Act relating to the discipline of the Defence Force and for related purposes.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 109 of 1982)- An Actto amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.1) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. II00f 1982)- An Act to amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.2) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 108 of 1982)- An Act relating to taxes on diesel fuel.
Dried Fruits Export Charges Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. l440f 1982)- An Act to amend the Dried Fruits Export Charges Act 1924.
Drought Assistance (Primary Producers) Act 1982 (Act No. 99 of 1982)An Act to make provison for the grant of financial assistance to primary producers who are affected by drought.
Export Market Development Grants Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 157 of 1982)An Act to amend the Export Market Development Grants Act 1974, and for related purposes.
Fertilizers (Subsidy) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 69 of 1982)An Act to amend section 3 of the Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Act 1966 and to amend the Phosphate Fertilizers Bounty Act 1963.
Health Legislation Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 1120f 1982)An Act to amend the Health Insurance Act 1973, the National Health Act 1953 and the Health Insurance Commission Act 1973, and for related purposes.
Income Tax (Companies, Corporate Unit Trusts and Superannuation Funds) Act 1982 (Act No. 102 of 1982)- An Act to impose a tax upon incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Individuals) Act 1982 (Act No. 101 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax upon incomes, other than incomes of companies, of corporate unit trusts and of superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Rates) Act 1982 (Act No. 105 of 1982)An Act to declare the rates of income tax payable upon incomes other than incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 103 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Act 1979.
Income Tax (Rates) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 1040f 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Rates) AC11976.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.4) 1982 (Act No. 76 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.5) 1982 (Act No. 106 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.6) 1982 (Act No. 123 of 1982)- An Act relating to income tax.
Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 97 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Act 1980.
Live-stock Export Charge Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 83 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-slock Export Charge ACI 1977.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Live-stock Slaughter Levy Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 82 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-stock Slaughter Levy Act 1964.
Loan ACt(No. 2) 1982 (Act No. 107 of 1982)An Act to authorize the borrowing and expending of moneys for defence purposes.
National Crimes Commission Act 1982 (Act No. 138 of 1982)An Act to establish a National Crimes Commission to investigate criminal activities, in particular organized criminal activities, with a view to the prosecution of offenders.
Northern Territory (Self-Government) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 130 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Northern Territory (Self-Government) Act 1978.
Pay-roll Tax (Territories) Assessment Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 128 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Pay-roll Tax (Territories) Assessment Act 1971.
Public Service Acts Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. III of 1982)- An Act relating to the Australian Public Service.
Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 78 of 1982)- An Act relating to certain remuneration and allowances.
Repatriation Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 100 of 1982)- An Act relating to repatriation and related matters.
Royal Commissions Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 139 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Royal Commissions Act 1902.
Salaries and Wages Pause Act 1982 (Act No. 134 of 1982)An Act to suspend for 12 months the powers of certain authorities in relation to the remuneration of certain persons, and for certain other purposes.
Sales Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 840f 1982)- An Act to amend the Sales Tax Act (No. I) 1930.
Sales Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 85 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Sales Tax Act (No.2) 1930.

MINISTERIAL ARRANGEMENTS
Mr ANTHONY (RichmondActing Prime Minister)-I inform the House that the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) left Australia on 18 November to attend discussions on agriculture and trade matters in the Middle East, Europe and the United States. The Minister for Transport and Construction (Mr Hunt) is acting as Minister for Primary Industry until Mr Nixon returns.
WAGES PAUSE
Ministerial Statement
Mr ANTHONY (Richmond-Acting Prime Minister)-by leave-The Commonwealth Government called yesterday's special Premiers Conference to achieve concerted action by all governments in Australia to address the deteriorating employment situation. The Commonwealth put to the Premiers and the Chief Minister a set of proposals around which it hoped to achieve agreement, the central thrust of which was to put in place the widest and most effective pause possible in wages for the next twelve months throughout the Australian economy, as a means of saving jobs. Full agreement was not reached on the proposals put by the Commonwealth. That fact, however, should not obscure the great measure of agreement which was achieved, and its significance. The Conference produced an unprecedented agreement among eight governments to effect a pause in wages in the public sector and through concerted action before tribunals and by other means to achieve a similar pause in the private sector. I shall enumerate the points of agreement in a moment.
The Commonwealth was seeking agreement on a twelve months pause in wages. The Premiers of Queensland, Western Australia and Tasmania and the Chief Minister of the Northern Territory will be fully supporting the Commonwealth Government in this respect. The Premiers of New South Wales, Victoria and South Australia will be seeking a six months pause in wages. I do not rule out that, depending on the state of the Australian economy during the next year, the Premiers of the latter three States may well extend the action that they are taking to twelve months. That is for the

future. however. What must not be overlooked is that all eight governments have agreed on effective action to secure a pause in wages throughout the economy of at least six months duration. That is the unprecedented central aspect of the agreement reached yesterday.
The fact that the governments represented at the meeting here in Canberra yesterday reached such an historic agreement is a reflection of the seriousness of the economic situation we are now facing. I think we have all been surprised at the suddenness with which the international recession finally impacted on Australia. It may have been late in coming to Australia, but there is no doubting now that our domestic economic performance is being affected by reduced demand for our exports, rock-bottom commodity prices which not only are affecting the profitability of our resources sector but also have contributed to the drying up of new resource investment plans, and increased import competition as overseas suppliers search for market opportunities wherever they can find them. All of this, of course, is being exacerbated by the worst drought in memory. In those circumstances it will become increasingly a matter of absolute survival for our business communities to maintain and improve their competitive position.
Unfortunately, as we all know only too well, domestic wage developments have been working in the opposite direction. Over the year to the September quarter average weekly ordinary time earnings rose by about 17 per cent, and this does not take into account reductions in standard hours. Real wages rose by about 5 per cent, while productivity was virtually unchanged. With domestic demand weak, the world economy in recession and an increasingly difficult world trading environment, many Australian businesses have not been able to pass on these increases in wage costs. As a result, profits have fallen by about 13 per cent over the last year and retrenchments have multiplied. In short, excessive wage increases have worsened our economic problems, weakened our competitiveness, contributed directly to unemployment and weakened demand. They have meant an increase in the real incomes of those in employment, while their less fortunate fellows have lost their jobs.
It was against that background that all those present at the Conference yesterday approached the discussions in a constructive and co-operative

3060 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Wages Pause

spirit. Each had his own perspective on the problems, but each was seeking constructively to find a consensus on urgent and effective measures to deal with the worsening employment situation. Specifically, this is what was agreed:
The Commonwealth Government will legislate for a 12-months pause in wages and in reductions in standard working hours in its own employment. There should be no catch-up at the end of the wages pause to recoup wages which might have been forgone in the course of the pause.
The Queensland, Western Australian, Tasmanian and Northern Territory governments will effect a similar 12-months pause in wages and in reductions in standard working hours in their own employment, by legislation as necessary.
The New South Wales, Victorian and South Australian governments will effect a sixmonths pause in wages and in reductions in standard working hours in their own employment.
To achieve the widest and most effective pause in wages throughout the private sector, the Commonwealth, Queensland, Western Australian, Northern Territory and Tasmanian governments will approach the arbitral tribunals seeking a 12-months pause in award wages and in standard working hours in the private sector.
The New South Wales, Victorian and South Australian governments are expected to intervene alongside the other five governments by supporting a six-months pause. The Com-
monwealth Government will be seeking the support of the peak councils for a 12-months pause in wages. The Government will be meeting the Confederation of Australian Industry later today and has sought an urgent meeting with the Australian Council of Trade Unions.
The wages pause is to take effect as soon as possible, with exceptions restricted to the processing of applications for those few groups not yet in receipt of a wage increase in the most recent community wage round.
Legislation to give effect to the wages pause will be introduced as soon as possible.
Resumption of wage increases after the end of the pause would depend on restoration of capacity to pay, which would be determined by appropriate Commonwealth and State tribunals.
All governments will urge a pause in overaward payments.
Professional incomes funded from the Commonwealth Budget will be included in the

pause by legislation where necessary. The States are expected to take similar action, where professional incomes are funded from their Budgets.
All governments will seek commitments from professional bodies for a pause in professional fees.
All governments will urge maximum restraint in other non-wage incomes.
Subject to all legislation to effect the wages pause and achieve the necessary savings being passed, the savings to the Commonwealth Budget achieved through the wages pause, expected to be some $300m, would be expended on employment-related projects of the Commonwealth's nomination throughout Australia, projects of high labour content and high Australian content, capable of being implemented quickly. Special consideration will be given to projects in areas of high unemployment within States. The Commonwealth will be consulting with all the States and the Northern Territory to identify such projects.
To the extent that State governments participate in the wages pause there would be some savings to their Budgets which they might use in similar activities. Alternatively, they would be relieved of the need to increase taxes or charges or to reduce services.
Semi-government and local authorities are to use their savings from a wages pause in additional employment-related activities or, as appropriate, to defer increases in charges or reduce borrowings.
The Commonwealth Government will be taking a range of other measures to help Australian businesses to weather the present economic difficulties, and to maintain their employment. These measures include a Buy Australian campaign, reintroduction of preference for Australian goods in Government purchasing, together with increased availability of finance through the Commonwealth Development Bank and the Australian Industry Development Corporation and acceleration of payments to businesses under various existing assistance schemes. We are also acting to ensure that Australian businesses have effective recourse to anti-dumping action against unfairly priced imports.
The Government has also considered the proposals being advanced to divert some of the funds spent on unemployment benefits to enable the unemployed to work on projects of value to local communities. Apart from questions of cost and effectiveness, such proposals may simply replace

Wages Pause

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3061

jobs. They therefore require careful consideration. Because of the seriousness of the unemploy-
ment problem, the Commonwealth has indicated to the Premiers that it will introduce a community projects program for a trial period of 12 months. Letters will be sent to State governments shortly, outlining specific guidelines for the trial and inviting their nomination of projects.
The Commonwealth would provide grants through the States for local bodies to undertake specific additional projects using part time labour recruited from the longer term unemployed. These grants would cover employment at casual award rates for a period necessary to earn an equivalent of benefit plus $35 a week. Each project could run for up to 12 months and employ up to 50 people. As a guide the Commonwealth would be willing to provide funds equal to the benefit rates involved plus an amount of $2m to cover the $35 a week supplement. This would enable trial projects involving about 1,000 people.
The Minister for Social Security, Senator Chaney, will elaborate on this initiative.
The measures I have just outlined constitute a vigorous response to the serious employment problems facing the Australian community. They are not a total cure and we still have a difficult job ahead of us. But the unprecedented nature of the agreement struck among the eight governments represented at yesterday's meeting shows how seriously the problems are viewed by governments. The mood among ordinary Australians throughout the community is equally realistic. There is a strong sense throughout the country that those who have jobs should now make some sacrifice to help their fellows who do not have jobs or whose jobs are at risk. It is now up to all of us to join in this consensus and give our strongest support to making the program work.
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition)-by leave-The Opposition welcomes a statement on this topic from the Government. One must confess that last night it seemed that nothing but confusion prevailed in all quarters involved in discussions on this matter. Even within the Cabinet there seemed to be conflict and confusion as to exactly what the Government was intending with its proposed wages freeze. One was not helped a great deal this morning by the newspaper coverage of the conclusions from the Premiers Conference of yesterday. Therefore, we welcome some sort of statement on this matter from the Government. I must say, however, that many issues are raised in the statement which need much further fleshing out before we will be clear on exactly what is involved. In those circumstances the Opposition

does not find itself in a position in which it can respond in a definitive way to this proposal. Accordingly, it prefers to reserve its response in detail until the debate which will follow the introduction of the legislation which relates to this matter.
However, one can say some things immediately
about this proposal. I believe that the first thing I should say on behalf of the Opposition is that we reaffirm our objection in principle to the proposition that a wages freeze is a solution to the economic problems of this country. There are a number of reasons for this. Firstly, it is unfair. If one considers the proposal that the Government has presented to the Parliament-indeed, to the nation-one notices that there is exclusive concentration on the wages sector of the economy but absolutely no attention at all given to the prices sector. I do not believe that introducing a prices freeze concurrently would work either. It did not work in 1977 when the Government tried it. It would not work now. But we acknowledge that moderation on both prices and incomes is essential. However, there is a different way to approach this matter and I will come to that in a few
minutes.
I refer to the rampant unfairness of a proposal which concentrates exclusively on wages and ignores prices. I also draw attention to that part of the statement of the Acting Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) where he refers to non-wage income. His proposal is to urge restraint. The point about the non-wage income sector of the economy is that this is where all the rip-offs have been going on, where all the tax-free speculative gains have been plundered and where all the tax avoidance and evasion has been taking place. Therein lies the genesis of much of the resentment in the community today by ordinary decent Australian households. They see a small, influential, privileged and very wealthy sector of the community getting more influential, more privileged and more wealthy by evading its responsibilities and they see little done. Wage earners are excluded from this; their families and households are denied this sort of opportunity. One would presume that they would not want it; they would want a more morally obliged society, fulfilling its commitments. But they see the unfairness of these double standards. Today the Acting Prime Minister in the Parliament has rubbed salt into the wounds, proposing fierce draconian restraints on the living standards of ordinary Australian households by legislative action but urging restraint on a section of the economy which has been a haven to some people who have been quite outrageous in their moral irresponsibility and in avoiding their commitments to this society.

3062 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Wages Pause

In relation to professional incomes the Acting Prime Minister said that the Government will seek commitments from those people who, very largely, are able to set their fees. It is nonsense to project market force theory on the fixing of prices for goods and services into the professional arena, especially in relation to medical services. These people are able to adjust their rates according to the volume of the market they have. If their mar-
ket goes down because there are more doctors their prices go up, and it completely confutes the principles of market force economics. All the Government proposes here-this is in line with all the co-operation that is extended from the States--is to seek commitments. I do not think many people in the community believe that there will be wholesale or even large scale commitment to restraint in that area.
What about speculative gains? There is absolutely no attention to this matter. They remain tax free, denying people the benefits that should be coming back through proper revenue processes being fed into the system to provide better services and, most of all, more job opportunities in the community. It is little wonder that out there in the community there is a cynicism about the whole process of government because these double standards prevail. An influential and privileged few are able to rip off uninhibitedly, while for the vast mass of ordinary Australian households there is nothing but prohibition, embargo and obstruction. Let me cite some of the figures. If the Government is concerned about the movement in wages, as the Opposition is-I will come to that in a minute; there is no denying it-it must realise that wages have two functions to fulfil in the economy. One provides a living standard for families and the other a price input for employers, manufacturers and the suppliers of goods and services. We have to weigh up these two influences provided by wages in the economy. The Opposition is concerned at movements in wages in recent times. I repeat that I will come to that.
At the moment I want to stick to the unfairness of what the Government is doing. Certainly, wages and salaries increased by more than 16 per cent in the 12 months to the September quarter this year, but non-wage income increased by well over 17 per cent. If the Government is worried about wage movement why is it not worried about non-wage movement? Why is it not doing something effective there? Rather vaguely the Government refers to taking steps to control incomes of professional people where those incomes are provided for in some unspecified way through Budget mechanisms. That is one of the reasons we want

to see the legislation. What does it portray in practice? Will it mean that members of the medical profession in this country, who this year will get more than $1,OOOm in medical benefits from Australian taxpayers, will, by some magical process, have salary or income restraints imposed on them? If so, what are the specifics of this measure? What steps do the States undertake to ensure that that is done, because my understanding is that the Federal Government cannot effectively do so under the Constitution. Can anyone here contemplate, even for the novelty value, for
more than a minute that the Premier of Queensland would move in that direction? That is just one example.
Chemists will receive more than $520m this year through pharmaceutical benefits. What sorts of controls is the Government proposing there? I am not recommending any there or anywhere else
at this point. I am merely raising questions to illustrate how totally deficient and unsatisfactory this statement is in terms of allowing a more considered debate to proceed at this point in relation to the wages freeze proposal. What about income from commissions and from money lending? With
interest rates high, many people at present are making quite hefty incomes through money lending as individual entrepreneurs, apart from organisations. What about self-employed contractors and sub-contractors entering this area to get into the black economy, in many cases? What this will do, in fact, is guarantee an explosion in the black economy. The Government will have no control over that. Already $15,000m a year of income li-
able to tax remains tax free because it is in the black economy.
It is also unfair because it is regressive. This I find the most disquieting aspect of the proposal. There is, through its effect, an invisible tax to be applied to income earners. It is regressive in its impact. Let me illustrate that. What will happen over the 12-month period that the Government is proposing is that, because inflation will go up effectively by about 10 per cent, real wages in their purchasing power will come down by about 10 per cent. In money terms that has the same effect as an increase in income tax-a rather fierce fiscal measure designed to contract the economy. When one takes that sort of income and spending power out of the economy, one contracts consumer activity, reduces economic activity and aggravates the recession that we have. There will be more unemployment. That is the effect. It is an invisible tax.
Let us look at the regressiveness, the unfairness, of this proposal. Someone who is on 75 per cent of average weekly earnings-that is, $240 or $250 a

Wages Pause

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3063

week, a very modest amount of income-will, in fact, through this measure if it is fully effective, experience an increase in his or her income taxthis sort of conceptual form of invisible income tax that I am putting forward-of 52 per cent. But someone on three times average weekly earnings--that is, nearly $1,000 a week--will face an increase of only 24 per cent.
I illustrate the unfairness of this proposal with these examples. The fettler out on the Nullarbor Plain working for the Australian National Rail-
ways is expected proportionately to make a greater sacrifice through this system, to pay for whatever exercise the Government is keen to embark on, than the chief design engineer or someone of that ilk who has all of the pleasantries of working in an air conditioned office and having much more stimulating, uplifting and creative work in the same organisation. It means that the nurse working in the Tasmanian hospital on a similarly low income will have to make a greater sacrifice for what the Government has in mind than the head of the Department of Health responsible for the administration of the health services. The postman doing the rounds in the suburbs, in all of the inclement weather that he experiences, will make a greater proportional sacrifice than the people who are running Australia Post. In Telecom Australia, the linesman will
have to make a greater sacrifice than the chief executives in the organisation. That is grossly unfair. It is a blatant abuse of the innocence of the Australian community.
I point to those examples and ask honourable members in this Parliament, who have all the privileges that parliamentarians have and the very generous salaries that parliamentarians have, to stop for a second to consider the situation of not just ordinary workers but the families they support on that sort of modest income. I ask honourable members to contemplate all of the liabilities that they have, the cost of which will continue to grow as their incomes shrink in real terms. I find that situation outrageous in its discrimination. It is a clear reflection of class prejudice on the part of the Government. I ask again: What does the Government expect to achieve in terms of an impact on inflation when it freezes public sector wage increases, for example, for a Fourth Division clerk in the Department of Social Security? His wage increases do not feed in any apparent way into the consumer price index. We have no quantification of the level to which inflation will be reduced as a consequence of these proposals.
I state now that I am absolutely convinced that the Government has not thought its way through this proposal properly. That is why there was still

conflict between Ministers, to the embarrassment of Premiers at the Premiers Conference, as to what the Government was trying to achieve. I say that the plan will not work. The Government is fortunate that the public is prepared to give it a try.
I mention the poll in this morning's Bulletin. To the extent that they are a reliable reflection, I do not recommend them all. I must say that some of the polls have been very good lately---in the Bulletin. Let me make this point. If one looks at that poll in relation to the Government's proposal, one finds that 52 per cent of the public is prepared to approve a l2-month freeze. In a rather superficial way, the poll shows in a perplexing way that the public however overwhelmingly does not believe-that is, 57 per cent-that it will have any effect in reducing unemployment. A much greater proportion does not believe that it will reduce inflation than believe it will. I think there is only one way to interpret that finding, that is, that the Australian public has put the Government on notice. The public is prepared to give the proposal a try because it is desperate as a result of the mess the economy is in. But the public is making it pretty clear to the Government that it has doubts as to whether the proposal will work, and the Government had better make sure that it does.
Let us look at what the Sydney Morning Herald had to say about this proposal, in case honourable members are uncharitable enough to think that it is merely a political point of view that is being put here. What I am putting forward is a carefully, technically assessed view of the deficiencies of this proposal. Today's Sydney Morning Herald states:
If any further proof was needed that the wage freeze is more about politics than economics--more about appearance than reality-now we have it . . . What started as gimmickry has now become farce.
The newspaper goes on to point out the very serious deficiencies in this proposal. I remind honourable members that it is similar to the proposal which was tried in 1977 and jettisoned. It is, as we see it, in all its elements a stunt. Because of the economic conditions of the moment the rate of increase in wages is going down. It has been going down for several quarters. The proposal is an empty gesture for the east coast States. Neither New South Wales and Victoria nor Queensland would be contemplating any noticeable wage adjustment settlements in the course of the next six months. It is all over for New South Wales and Victoria in that respect. On Monday Queensland made sure that it did not get caught in the trap. An Industrial Court decision there provided for a

3064 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Wages Pause

5.9 per cent increase in State awards. The Premier of that State declared this morning that, although he has committed himself to a 12-month freeze, at the end of six months he will review his position. Whatever way we look at the proposition, it seems to be on somewhat shaky foundations. In that sense we find ourselves unable to explore confidently and assert thereafter what our position is in relation to the proposition until we see the legislation before the House.
The Premiers, in their various ways, do not seem to reflect the enthusiasm of the Government. The Premier of Victoria described the proposal as a dog's breakfast. It was something entered into because of the stand and deliver tactics of the Government. No other options were provided. The Premier of New South Wales likened it to trying to treat a cancer patient with aspirin. The Premier of South Australia went away and said that he had agreed to nothing as far as he was concerned.
Another deficiency in this arrangement is that, although it is aimed at freezing award rates, it does nothing effectively about over-award rates. Therein lies another cause of concern for us. Although the Government says that it will urge restraint in that area, this will mean very simply that where those industrial organisations which represent people in particular industries have clout they will be able to extract wage increases while others will miss out. The inevitable consequence of that will be a redistribution and reallocation of the proportions of wages within the total wage sector between groups which draw on that sector. It will mean that those less well off will find over time a diversion of real spending power from them to the more powerful groups within the economy. As one deeply committed to equity in our system, acknowledging nonetheless the case for differentials on the basis of skill, nature of work and so on, I find this a very unattractive proposition.
Let us look at the elements of what the Government finally says it is trying to pull together in all this. It reckons it will create 10,000 jobs in the course of a year. In that year unemployment will be between 550,000 and 600,000 at its peak. To what extent will the Government assert now, with conviction if it has so much commitment to this policy, that unemployment will be reduced by these proposals? Ten thousand is a piddling number in terms of the level of unemployment and the collapse in the economy which is currently under way. The amount the Government is talking of allocating to the States through the processes which will be related to this policy is $300m. It says the total savings on the wages bill

of State and Federal instrumentalities, semi-government authorities and so on, will be $700m. Let us focus on that $700m. There will be lost revenue because that will not be provided in wages. That $700m represents a loss of $250m to the Commonwealth Government in tax revenue. The Government will provide $300m for jobs. There will be a net addition to the economy of only $50m as a consequence of this proposal. That, I believe, exposes the hollowness, in economic terms, of what the Government is proposing to do. When one contrasts the sorts of jobs which would have been in existence, sustained by spending from a public sector wage package before this proposal, against the jobs which will be destroyed by this change-the jobs which will be created-my hunch is that there will be very little net effect. If there is any net effect it will be marginal. I suspect that there will be none at all.
I challenge the Government to provide us with the calculations to establish beyond any doubt that there are substantial benefits for the community, so substantial that in terms of the jobs they generate in addition to those existing at the moment they will help to overcome unemployment and generate economic growth so that the policy itself in quantifiable terms will bring down inflation and overall the economy will be in a better condition and avoid the contraction which would otherwise take place with this sort of policy.
Finally, I have mentioned that we acknowledge that there is a case for wage moderation. It would be useless to deny it. The case for wage moderation is unassailable. We have been saying that regularly. In 1982-83 profits in national accounts terms fell to 12 per cent of national product. According to the Department of the Treasury in a more normal situation they should be 17 per cent. Wages had gone up to 67.6 per cent as against a more normal situation of 63 per cent. There are several things to be said about that. Some of that contraction of profits, of course, can be accounted for due to the contractionary state of the economy, but not all of it. Not all the movement in wages can be explained in that sense either. It is quite clear that in the last two years there has been a quite marked real increase in wages allowing for productivity as well as the cost of living. It would be foolish to try to deny that this has had a destabilising effect on the economy. It would stand in the way of sensible and more successful economic management.
I ought to put on the record parenthetically, before I move on, that many enterprises in this country are enjoying extraordinary levels of profitability. In the debate that will follow I will be

Wages Pause

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATlVES 3065

enumerating scores of them. These rates of profitability must almost be records. In that sense we see again the glaring unfairness of the policies of the Government. In that sense there should be in place policies in relation to wage moderation which are also related to prices, profits, non-wage incomes and people who are living off speculative gains tax free. These policies should refer to the social wage and a range of related matters. They certainly should refer to reform in the income tax system. They should preside over or monitor movements in these various sectors of the economy so that they are held in restraint within guidelines which have been agreed upon through a system of consensual collaboration. No prices and incomes policy will work in any other way. These are matters to which we want to relate in more detail in the debate on the legislation when it comes to the Parliament next week.
The final point I want to make on this matter is simply this: Wages freezes and prices freezes have not worked elsewhere. They failed in this country in 1977. They are in their nature more a stunt than anything else. They will not be a resolution of the major economic problems confronting this country. The worst aspect of them is that the community will be encouraged to expect much more than will be achieved if indeed anything is to be achieved. When they fail to achieve those grand goals which have been outlined the community will be very seriously disaffected, disabused and angry because of it. More than that, these sorts of things make no provision for moderating the movements in those various sectors of the economy I mentioned a few minutes ago in the event of economic recovery. In the last seven years, on each occasion when government policies have generated additional economic growth, this country has seen with a lagged effect an upsurge in wage demand.
Assuming that the Government were to be successful with this sort of policy in some sort of way-I have made clear my views on that-there would be a period at the end of it when there would be an explosion in demand for wages. So the whole policy would be self-defeating. It would mean that real economic recovery of the sort that we must work for to bring down unemployment and get the economy moving again would be unachievable as the present Government's approach now stands. In those circumstances we argue-we will be arguing in more detail next week when the debate takes place in Parliament-that the more appropriate measure in this country at the present time is a prices and incomes policy embodying a comprehensive series of proposals which is seen to

be fair, which has consensual support in the community and which will successfully regulate and moderate the various forces at work in the economy.

QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
BUDGET DEFICIT
Mr WILLIS-I refer the Treasurer to his reported admission yesterday that the Budget deficit this year is likely to be about $4000m, or some $2300m more than estimated at Budget time. Will the Treasurer inform the House what components of the Budget are responsible for the

3066 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Questions without Notice

deficit blowout to this all-time record high level? Can the Tresurer assure the House that this latest revision to the deficit forecast will be his final figure, or does he envisage the possibility that the deficit will blowout even further than he has so far admittecl? Finally, what increase in the deficit would occur as the result of an effective freeze on wages? Has that increase been taken into account in the $4 billion deficit estimate?
Mr HOWARD~ I did say at the Premiers Conference yesterday that the deficit figure for this financial year could approach $4,000m. I am able to inform the honourable gentleman that the current estimate from the Treasury and the Department of Finance of the Budget deficit for this financial year is about $3,750m. That is made up of an estimated increase in outlays of $1,343m over the Budget estimate and lower receipts to the tune of about $708m. I can oblige the honourable gentleman by giving him the main elements. Unemployment benefits are estimated at this stage to add $603m to the Budget forecast. That represents an increase in forecast unemployment beneficiaries from 395,000 to 525,000. As I have explained earlier, that is significantly due to there being a larger component of beneficiaries. Drought assistance is estimated at this stage to be $356m. Natural disaster relief is estimated at $52m extra, and that represents a further response to approaches from the States as a result of the drought. It is also estimated at this stage that there will be an additional $93m to cover public debt charges because it is necessary to finance through the sale of additional government securities, which is the non-inflationary way of financing the deficit. It is therefore necessary to sell more government bonds. They are the major elements of the outlays side of the Budget.
It is anticipated that gross pay-as-you-earn collections will be about $323m less than estimated at the time of the Budget. We have already revised downwards from 12 per cent to II t per cent the average weekly earnings estimate contained in the Budget. If the pause is fully effective it is my advice from Treasury that there will be a further reduction to about lOt per cent in the AWE outcome for 1982-83. We have already chalked up, through increases in the pipeline, about 8 per cent or 9 per cent of the AWE forecast contained in the Budget.
Employment is now anticipated to decline by about one per cent rather than the constant estimate made at the time of the Budget. As to per-" sonal tax collections in the provisional area, largely due to the drought, we anticipate a larger number of farmers than expected will self assess and this will result in a further $123m decrease. It

is anticipated that collections under the recoupment legislation will be in the order of $1 OOm this year although that figure is subject to more than the usual margin of difficulty. Because of the atypical nature of the legislation that figure may prove to be on the pessimistic side. Because of the generally lower level of economic activity than we forecast at the time of the Budget there are effects in the indirect tax area. Because of reduced consumer purchases sales tax is expected to be down by $84m, excise duties down by about $1 70m and customs duty on imports down by about $100m. On the other side it is anticipated that collections from the crude oil levy will be about $240m greater than the collections forecast at the time of the Budget.
I say to the honourable gentleman that if $4,000m were the outcome, that would represent about 2.5 per cent of gross domestic product. Therefore the honourable gentleman is not correct in describing the figure as an all-time high. In 1975-76 the deficit represented 4.9 per cent of gross domestic product. I want to say to the House that I draw no comfort from that particular comparison. As far as I and the Government are concerned the fact that our fiscal position is now weaker than anticipated at the time of the Budget, because of the prolonged effect of the drought and because of a much weaker economy due almost entirely to the effect of the overseas recession, it will be necessary for us to place a much greater weight on monetary policy and it will be necessary to sell more government securities in order to finance that deficit. As a consequence monetary policy will need to carry a much greater weight than previously expected. That underlines the real value, in economic terms, of the pause that was obtained yesterday.
Dr Klugman-The Treasurer seemed to forget the loss in revenue due to any successful wages freeze.
Mr HOWARD~ I will supplement my answer. I indicated to the honourable gentleman that if the pause were totally effective in 1982-83 the best estimate at present of the effect of that on AWE would be to take it down to about lOt per cent, which would have the effect of another $200m to $300m in revenue receipts.
Mr Scott~Mr Speaker, I raise a point of order. I ask the Treasurer to table the document from which he was reading.
Mr SPEAKER~I ask the Treasurer whether he was quoting from a document.
Mr HOWARD-I was reading from it. The document has some other valuable points that I

Questions without Notice

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3067

did not use. There is no problem at all in tabling the document.
INTERNATIONAL ECONOMICS CONFERENCE
Mr CHAPMAN-I ask the Treasurer whether he is aware of comments made yesterday by the United States Treasury Secretary, Mr Regan, calling for an international conference to be convened similar to the 1944 Bretton Woods conference. What would be the Government's attitude to such a conference?
Mr Young-If it is a trip overseas they will go. They will all go. They will all go and claim expenses.
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The House will come to order. I indicate to the honourable member for Port Adelaide that we all welcome him back and welcome his return to health. I also indicate to him that he has already interjected 10 times today and that he is coming back into a foreign environment if he continues that.
Mr HOWARDMy attention has been drawn to remarks attributed to the American Treasury Secretary Mr Regan reportedly calling for a new international economic conference similar to the one held at Bretton Woods towards the end of World War II. I have had some inquiries made about the comments and it would appear that the reports are a little overblown against the comments that were actually made by Mr Regan. He indicated that he would want to talk to the finance Ministers of the major industrial countries about this issue. He criticised some of what he described as the ad hoc financing practices that had gone on in recent months, although he also said in responding to a question that he did not believe that the world was yet ready for a new conference of that kind.
I say to the honourable gentleman that, of course, if any conferences of that kind were convened, naturally Australia would be interested in participating and contributing. I take the opportunity of saying that those who imagine that the world's economic problems-certainly the world's monetary management problems-can be solved by large international economic conferences and that in some way those large economic conferences are an alternative to sound domestic economic policies will in the end be proved wrong. Ultimately the strength, in an international monetary sense, of the world economy can be achieved only through the total effect of strong domestic economies. That would generally be our approach at those meetings.

PHARMACEUTICAL BENEFIT PRESCRIPTIONS
Mr LLOYD-The Minister for Health will be aware of the strong opposition of chemists to the new statutory declaration requirement for concessional pharmaceutical benefit prescriptions. Is it necessary to have this requirement for every prescription? Why is the responsibility for this declaration being placed on the chemist rather than on the prescribing doctor?
Mr CARLTON-I advised the House in answer to a question a few weeks ago that with

the introduction of the new three-tier pharmaceutical benefits scheme I was taking the opportunity to tighten up a very loose administrative system in line with my general concern about protecting the taxpayers' dollar. That scheme gives additional benefit to over one million Australians on low incomes or in receipt of sickness benefits and to elderly people who have pensions but who do not have pensioner health benefit cards. Instead of the prescriber-that is, the doctor-writing a number on the prescription form, it will be necessary for the person claiming the benefit to write the number from his or her pension card, or whichever card it is, on the back of the prescription form. It is not a requirement of the chemist. The chemist does not take the legal responsibility for this although, of course, if the form is not properly completed the benefit is not payable from taxpayers' funds. It does not have to happen with all prescriptions, of course. It happens only with those prescriptions where there is a special benefit of $4 to a pensioner with a PHB card or $2 to a holder of a health care card, a sickness beneficiary, or as I said earlier, a pensioner without a PHB card.
The actual form to be introduced on I January is to my mind rather too complicated. I have agreed with the chemists that it looks too complicated. I am trying to find a better form consistent with advice from the Attorney-General's Department that the declaration is legally binding on the claimant. I think that is an important requirement. I have written to every registered pharmacist in Australia explaining what I am doing and explaining my concern about the complicated nature of the form. I have asked my Department, in conjunction with form design experts-I am picking out ones to whom officers of the Department can talk-together with representatives of the Attorney-General's Department, to work out a much simpler form and to have the proposals with me this week so that I can go back to the Pharmacy Guild of Australia with those proposals.
I want to make two things quite clear. One is that there is no going back on the requirement for the declaration by the recipient. That is something that I cannot go back on. I am under very strong pressure from this House to tighten up on the systems in a number of areas of government benefits-in the medical area and elsewhere. I agree with the general consensus of this House that these systems are too loose. Bit by bit I am going to tighten them up. It is just unfortunate that the chemists have to be involved in the first bit of that tightening up. I am asking the chemists to bear with me on this. I am not going back on

Questions without Notice

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3069

that requirement. Secondly, I am very concerned indeed about attempts by certain people to frighten pensioners over this whole matter. A statement put out by Senator Grimes attempted to do this and some pharmacists-not the guildare trying to frighten pensioners on this matter.
Mrs Darling-That is not right.
Mr CARLTON-I hope that the honourable member for Lilley will understand my objectives in this matter. Contrary to statements she has made in public, I have no intention whatever of trying to make things difficult for pensioners.
Mrs Darling-Mr Speaker, I wish to make a personal explanation.
Mr SPEAKER-I will call the honourable lady at an appropriate time.
Mr CARLTON-I want to make it quite clear that I think that the pressures that have been put on me in this House, by honourable members from both sides, to tighten up these various systems are ones to which I must respond. I believe that I have this responsibility to the taxpayer; I believe that the systems in the whole health care area need tightening up; I believe that some of the professionals in the area have developed over the years a somewhat lax view about the taxpayers' dollar. That is something about which I have to be concerned. At the same time. I am concerned about the pensioners, but I will not go back on the requirement that the person who gets the benefit must make it clear that he or she is claiming that benefit. That, of course, is a legally enforceable matter if there is fraud.

It is a fact that the Taxation Office has been engaged over the past few months in ensuring-I am sure that this policy will meet with the approval of the Opposition, which is very strong on effective action against sales tax avoidance arrangements-that sales tax is not avoided by the unfair use of service contract arrangements whereby part of the real price of goods is in fact appropriated to a service arrangement that does not carry the sales tax. If the honourable gentleman is referring to that, I say to the honourable gentleman and to the House that the Government has not been involved in any way in taxation by stealth. What the Government and the taxation officers have been involved in on that matter is protecting the revenue-something that the Opposition has been very hot and strong about over the past few months.

I indicated to him that a certain report had not been received. I have checked with my office. The truth is that I have received a report on that matter from the industry working party. PHARMACEUTICAL BENEFIT PRESCRIPTIONS
Dr BLEWETT-I ask the Minister for Health: What evidence has he of fraud and malpractice in the pharmaceutical benefit area to justify the imposition of new bureaucratic provisions on the aged and the disadvantaged? Will he also explain to the House why it is not possible for the doctor who writes the prescription and is also aware of the status of the beneficiary concerned to provide the protection that the Minister so desires?
Mr CARLTON-In this case I am not waiting for another Public Accounts Committee inquiry to tell me the extent of the fraud in this arell. I am merely looking at the actual systems themselves. When I find that nobody is held responsible for a claim there is a prima facie case that people are able to indulge in fraud. That is one of the loose parts of the system I intend to tighten up. Let me explain to the House, as I explained the other day, that at present the doctor, the prescriber, is asked-he is not required to do so-to write the pension number on the prescription form. There is no legal or constitutional power on the part of the Commonwealth to require a doctor to write down that number. But we can require the person who is the beneficiary, that is the person who receives the $2 or $4 benefit, to sign the form. As I said earlier, I am trying to make that requirement as simple as I can.
We have to understand that pensioners in the ordinary course of events give proof of entitlement day in and day out for all sorts of things. They do not always have to sign a form. Sometimes it is a matter of showing a pension card when getting on a bus, or for having cheaper entry to a picture theatre, or when getting a perm in a private hairdresser's establishment or in other areas. There are various ways and means in which pensioners indicate entitlement for a benefit. I think people are exaggerating in advance of the event what difficulties might be encountered in

this matter. Not all pensioners are in a state of great infirmity. Many health care beneficiaries and various other people-in fact three million Australians-come under these kinds of provisions. All I am asking is that they sign something to show that they are entitled to an additional benefit of $2 or $4 every time they receive it. The total cost of pharmaceutical benefits is about $470m, about half of which is made up of concessional benefits. I do not have to sit around and wait for a Public Accounts Committee inquiry into fraud in pharmaceutical benefits before I take steps to tighten up the system.

I have a letter in my possession and a telex from the Pharmacy Guild (Queensland Branch) which shows that for months before this form came in information and advice was given to the Minister suggesting that the form was too complex but he insisted on going ahead with it.
After the chemists' representative came to see me, I held a meeting with consumer groups, the pensioners league and the Pharmaceutical Society and put the form before them to get feedback and information from them as to whether something should be changed. That feedback came from the three different groups. They requested me to launch a campaign to have this form withdrawn. I in no way scared any pensioners. I have gone around to groups. I showed them the form. They wish it to be simplified-and, in fact, withdrawn. I feel that it was a complete misrepresentation on behalf of the Minister and reflects his traumatic experience in trying to put forward a form over a number of months which people within the profession, as well as pensioners and consumers, do not wish to have.
