CONDITIONS OF ENTRY FOR NUCLEAR POWERED WARSHIPS
Ministerial Statement
Mr SINCLAIR (New England--Minister for Defence)--by leaveThe House will be aware that nuclear powered warships of the United States Navy regularly visit Australian ports. Since the Government decided in 1976 to allow such visits about 50 port calis have been made by a variety of United States Navy nuclear powered surface ships and submarines to Western Australia, Victoria, Tasmania, Queensland and the Northern Territory. These port visits-like visits made by conventionally powered United States Navy warships-are valued by both the Australian and United States governments and are an important practical expression of naval co-operation in peacetime between close allies. This year's ANZUS Council communique recorded that the Council members:
confirmed the high priority each partner placed upon a regular and comprehensive programme of naval visits to each other's ports, as well as to friendly ports in the Asia/Pacific region generally. They recognized the importance of access by United States naval ships to the ports of its Treaty partners as a critical factor in its efforts to maintain strategic deterrence and in order to carry out its responsibilities under the terms of the Treaty.
In this regard, I would draw to the attention of the House the fact that the United States Navy now

operates about 135 nuclear powered warships, and that more than 40 per cent of the major combatant units of the United States Navy are nuclear powered. All the nuclear powered warships visits that have taken place in the last six years have been arranged in accordance with the conditions of entry laid down by the Australian Government in 1976. These conditions of entry were incorporated in the document titled 'Environmental Considerations of Visits of Nuclear Powered Warships to Australia' which was tabled in this place by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) on 4 June 1976. I now wish to inform the House that the Government has decided, following a review of the conditions of entry carried out by the Department of Defence in co-operation with other departments and the Australian Atomic Energy Commission, that a number of changes should be made to those conditions.
Unlike the conditions of entry laid down in 1976, the new conditions of entry are intended to apply to warships having reactors larger than 100 megawatts as well as to warships with reactors smaller than 100 megawatts. This change follows further assessment of information available on these vessels and the selection of berths such that any health hazards associated with the visits will be no greater than those accepted currently for warships with smaller reactors. The new conditions of entry will therefore enable visits to approved berths in Australia by the Nimitz class of aircraft carriers, subject only to appropriate arrangements being made to remove the vessel to a remote location in the unlikely event of an accident involving the nuclear propulsion plant. The other change of substance to the general conditions of entry concerns emergency towing arrangements. The 1976 conditions of entry stipulated that a tug must be available for emergency towing duties whenever a nuclear powered warship visited an Australian port. United States nuclear powered surface ships, however, have two or more reactors and could move under their own power even in the unlikely event of damage to one of those reactors. Except when visits are made by nuclear powered submarines, which have only one reactor, the requirement to remove a warship to a safe location may therefore be met by the warship itself rather than by separate towing vessels.
The new conditions of entry will apply to visits by nuclear powered warships of the United States Navy and-should visits be requested by it-to nuclear powered warships of the Royal Navy. They are as follows:
A. visits will be for purposes such as crew rest and recreation and not for fuel handling or

Entry for Nuclear Powered Warships

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3079

repair to reactor plant necessitating breach of reactor containment;
B. visits will be subject to satisfactory arrangements concerning liability and indemnity and to provision of adequate assurances relating to the operation and safety of the warships while they are in Australian waters;
C. movement of vessels must take place during daylight hours under conditions where visibility is not less than three quarters of a mile;
D. navigational controls on other shipping will be applied during the time that nuclear powered ships are entering or leaving ports;
E. there must be a capability to remove the vessel either under its owr. power or under
tow, to a designated safe anchorage or to a designated distance to sea, within the time frame specified for the particular berth or anchorage and in any case within 24 hours, if an accident should occur; and F. an operating safety organisation, competent to carry out a suitable radiation monitoring program and able to initiate actions and provide services necessary to safeguard the public in the event of a release of radioactivity following an accident, must exist for
the port being visited.
I would emphasise to the House that the new conditions of entry are completeiy consistent with the very high standards of safety that have always been applied during visits by nuclear powered warships. It might be noted that, among the detailed precautions that are taken, these warships may use only those berths which have been individually assessed and cleared by the Australian Atomic Energy Commission as suitable for such use. Moreover, competent Australian personnel monitor carefully radiation levels in the vicinity of these warships throughout each visit, and a separate environmental monitoring program is conducted in co-operation with State authorities in each of the ports that are visited.
The results of these monitoring programs are published annually by the Department of Home Affairs and Environment. No emissions of radioactivity have been detected by these programs from any of the nuclear powered warships that have visited Australia. Application of the new conditions of entry for nuclear powered warships will facilitate future visits to Australia by our United States ally's warships and at the same time will ensure that all the necessary precautions are taken to protect fully the interests of the Australian public in these visits. I commend them to the House. I present the following paper:

Conditions of Entry for Nuclear Powered Warships---
Ministerial Statement. gDecember 1982.
Motion (by Sir James Killen) proposed:
That the House take note of the paper.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (3.36)-The Minister for Defence (Mr Sinclair) has brought in a new set of conditions under which nuclear powered vessels may enter Australian ports. He has not sought in any way to advise the House why it was necessary to change the existing arrangements, nor has he indicated who will pay the costs and whether the State governments, which in the main are responsible for State emergency services and other activities, have been consulted about these new arrangements. He has also not indicated why it was necessary to take out the detailed requirements for the various organisations involved in monitoring the safety of areas to which visits by nuclear powered vessels take place. From a reading of the 1976 agreement it is fairly obvious that a number of the requirements set down then, including those for public safety and the evacuation of members of the public in the event of a nuclear accident, have been totally neglected over the intervening six years. The safety zone requirements, if they have been implemented, have been the best kept secret from the population of those areas, probably a better kept secret than some of our defence plans.
The Minister indicated that, because of multiple reactors, tug stand-bys will no longer be required for surface ships. I draw the Minister's attention to the fact that the existence of multiple real:tors does not guarantee that one of those reactors can be used in circumstances of disability. The reactors of all nuclear warships share coolant systems, and when the problem is a radiation leak in the coolant system, neither reactor can be used. Therefore, the requirement for tug stand-by exists just as much for a multiple reactor warship as it does for a single reactor warship. The fact is that multiple reactors can be rendered inactive, even though neither of the reactors is out of operation, by disabilities in the coolant system shared by the reactors. The new statement removes specific references to the acceptance of liabilities and to the United States Statement of Assurances. This has been totally dropped from the guidelines and has been replaced by a rather broad and sweeping general statement which mayor may not mean something. The statement gives no detail.
There are a number of other matters of concern. I mentioned previously the safety of local communities and the bodies which exist for the monitoring of nuclear visits. The new statement sets out no criteria, no conditions and no minimum requirements for such an operation. They

3080 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Inquiry into Drug Trafficking

were clearly spelt out in the previous statement, including safety requirements for the public. They have been removed. We now have faith, hope and trust. Given the example of the last six years when the provisions of the previous statement were totally ignored, we can fairly safely assume that those safety requirements which are not specifically spelt out but which are left to trust will be ignored by the Government. The Government is interested only in facilitating visits and is not interested in the security or safety of the Australian community, otherwise it would not have felt the need to remove those specific requirements which had not been a hindrance in the past and which should not be a hindrance now.
There are a number of other matters of concern. I mentioned previously that the reimbursement of monitoring costs is not mentioned. If the Minister cares to look at the Notice Paper, he will find on it a question from my colleague the honourable member for Swan (Mr Beazley) relating to a dispute existing with the Western Australian Government about large sums of money in respect of monitoring facilities which have never been paid. The new statement seems to me to be an indication that the Government wishes to have minimum specified requirements and is prepared to take risks with other people's lives. That is not acceptable to the Opposition.
Debate (on motion by Mr Falconer) adjourned.
ROYAL COMMISSION OF INQUIRY INTO DRUG TRAFFICKING
INTERIM REPORT No.2-PASSPORTS
Ministerial Statement
Mr STREET (Corangamite-Minister for Foreign Affairs)-by leave-On 27 May this year, Senator Dame Margaret Guilfoyle (Minister for Finance) tabled in the Senate interim report No. 2-Passports-of the Royal Commission of Inquiry into Drug Trafficking. The Government has considered the report and I should like to outline to the House the Government's response to the report and the recommendations contained in it. The Government welcomes the report because we accept that false passports and false identities are tools of trade for drug traffickers and other international criminals; we share the concern expressed by a number of people including the Williams Royal Commission of Inquiry into Drugs and the Stewart Royal Commission of Inquiry into Drug Trafficking about passport abuse; we acknowledge that the Government has a responsibility to do what it can to deter international criminal activities; and we

are determined to take all possible steps to eliminate criminal activities, including passport abuse.
The Government's concern at these problems is evident from its action in appointing the Williams and Stewart royal commissions and the Costigan Royal Commission on the Activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union. The Government's attitude is also evident from the changes which have been introduced into the passport issue and control system by the Government and my Department since 1976. In that period my Department has taken the following initiatives: In 1976 passport records previously held in State offices were centralised-this allowed a ready check of current passport holders. In 1978 records previously held on card indexes were placed on computer. Since 1978 a number of improvements have been made to the computerbased information system. In 1979 significant amendments were made to the 1938 Passports Act. These amendments tightened procedures for the issue of passports, detailed the circumstances under which a passport could be either denied or withdrawn, and increased substantially the penalties for passport abuse. These were the first major changes to the Passports Act since the Department of External Affairs introduced the first Passports Act into Parliament in 1920. Since 1980 all passport-issuing offices in Australia have had on-line access to the central computer to prevent the issue of a second passport to a person of the same name and date of birth, and it allows an immediate check against those who are on various alert lists supplied to the Department of Foreign Affairs by authorities such as law enforcement agencies. In 1981 the lamination of the personal particulars page of passports was introduced to prevent the alteration of photograph or information about the holder. Earlier this year my Department gave approval for further improvements to be made to the passport in order to enhance its security. In January this year I announced the introduction of a system of checks against the identity of applicants. It should be noted that the improvements which I have just outlined were the first major changes made to the passport system in over 40 years.
The Stewart Royal Commission report on passports raised a number of factors relating to the passport issue. In considering the Stewart report, the recommendations of the Williams Royal Commission were also given further attention. A number of other factors which would improve passport security were taken into account at the same time. In the five months since the report was tabled it has been the subject of intense study. My

Inquiry into Drug Trafficking

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3081

Department established a working party to examine the recommendations and their implications. A series of interdepartmental committees were established to discuss the various recommendations and other suggestions for improving the passport system. A survey was taken of the travel industry to assess the effect of the recommendations on that industry. Discussions were held with travel industry representatives. Discussions were also held by officers of my Department with State and Territory authorities. Passport applications submitted in 1981-82 were surveyed in order to obtain basic information on which the Government could then consider the recommendations.
The main thrust of the Stewart Royal Commission report on passports relates to the problems of checking the identity of applicants. This is not a problem which is unique to the passports system. Those who watched the television program which set out to underline the weaknesses in the passport issue system will have noted that the program underlined the ease with which television staff obtained false birth certificates, driver's licences, gun licences, building society accounts, cheque accounts from banks and Bankcard credit facilities and enrolled on the electoral roll.
Establishing identity is vital in a number of important areas other than passports which are also of concern to Commonwealth and State governments and the private sector. Citizenship, checking illegal immigrants and banking are a few. It is also the key to receipt of a number of financial benefits from the Government. In Australia and those countries with similar political and judicial backgrounds such as the United Kingdom, the United States of America, Canada and New Zealand, there is absolutely no infallible method of establishing identity through a single identification system. The most effective method of establishing identity would, of course, be the establishment of a national identity system based on fingerprinting. The Government has considered this but is not prepared to institute such a system in relation to passports because of the implications that this would have on our traditional way of life. There would also be major administrative difficulties and additional costs. In the context of the passport issue and control system there is a limit to the inconvenience government can impose on honest Australians to stop fraudulent practices by a few-over half a million Australians obtain passports each year.
As I have indicated, this problem is shared by other like countries and it is not practicable to introduce the system applied in some European countries which have a number of features which

distinguish them from Australia. They are not migrant receiving countries, few are federal and they have a tradition of registering their citizens and monitoring the changes of address, marital status and so on throughout their lives. The Government, therefore, has examined alternative ways of tightening identity checks in respect of passport issue and control. The Government will retain the present multi-level checks on identity which will be further improved as a result of the Government's consideration of this report.
A number of recommendations in the report were already part of the passport issue system and others have since been implemented. The Government's response to each of the recommendations is outlined in the schedule which I now table. The more significant of the 40 recommendations in the report are those which are addressed to the need to require applicants to appear personally before a passport officer who is adequately trained and responsible to check identities. The report proposes the provision of more passport offices and the appointment of commissioners for passports to deal with applicants residing more than 100 kilometres from a passport office. While the Government accepts the need for personal interviews and agrees that this will require an appropriate infrastructure to cope with personal interviews, it does not propose to implement these changes in the form recommended by the report, for reasons which I will now explain.
The survey of passport applications which I mentioned earlier shows that there is insufficient work load to justify the establishment of regional offices on the scale proposed by the report. The Government also feels that there are a number of problems of control, security and servicing with the appointment of commissioners for passports and has some doubts that it would contribute to overcoming the problem of identifying applicants. The Government has therefore decided that, rather than establish regional offices and appoint commissioners for passports, official post offices will be used as agencies for passport offices. Senior postal clerks of about the same classification as interviewing staff in passport offices will, after thorough training, be authorised to accept applications for intending travellers, check the documentation against application details, certify the photographs and forward the passport application to passport offices for processing, at which stage further checks will be carried out before a passport is issued by the passport office. I emphasise that passports will not be issued by post offices. It is intended that passports, when issued,

3082 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Inquiry into Drug Trafficking

will be sent by certified mail to applicants' residential addresses or be available for collection at passport offices by the applicant. Further details of these arrangements will be announced in the near future but it should be noted that full implementation of these arrangements will be phased in over an 18-month period.
The Government will maintain the present security requirements for a certificate regarding applicant, but following recommendations from the Williams Royal Commission and after consultation with State and Territory authorities, the categories of persons eligible to provide the certificates regarding applicants' identity will be restricted even further. In future the categories will be limited to the following: Members of ParliamentFederal and State; aldermen and councillors of municipal and shire councils; town clerks and secretaries; medical practitioners and dentists; judges, magistrates, clerks of petty sessions and clerks of court; school teachers of five years service; postmasters; police officers of the rank of sergeant and above and officers in charge of stations; officers of the armed services; ministers of religion designated as marriage celebrants; and Federal and State public servants of at least 10 years' permanent service. It will be some six months before this change is fully implemented. The security checks in our offices, which I announced in January of this year, will continue to be carried out by passport offices when the passport application is being processed.
A series of recommendations in the Royal Commission's report is designed to give passport officers access to adequate documentation and information to check identities and to improve the reliability of information held in passport offices. Passport offices already have access to electoral roll information and further examination is being made on the possibility of access to other information which could verify the identity of applicants. It is not possible or practicable to go to the extent envisaged in the report. Much of the information is held by the States and there would be a need for the States to computerise and integrate such information and make it available to the Commonwealth. Even if it were possible to do this, it is most doubtful that for passport purposes alone such effort would be warranted. Nevertheless, a number of the security measures designed to improve the range and integrity of information available will be discussed further with State and Territory authorities.
A number of other recommendations canvassed issues which raise important fundamental principles and issues. These include restricting the entitlement of certain individuals to passports and

restricting eligibility for a passport on conviction or suspicion of offences. These recommendations are to be referred to the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) for consideration by the appropriate State and Territory authorities. The Government acknowledges that there has been inadequate consultation and co-ordination on passport related matters. A consultative committee to consider all aspects of passport security will be established. Its membership will comprise representatives of appropriate Commonwealth authorities. My Department will also maintain liaison with the State and Territory authorities and will discuss various aspects of passport security with those authorities on a regular basis.
In common law it is, of course, quite easy for people to change their names and the Royal Commission's report has pointed to the effect that this can have on controlling the number of passports held by one person. After discussions with State and Territory authorities and further consideration, I have decided that in future all passport applicants will be required to provide a full birth certificate showing the name of the person at birth and the full names of the mother and father. This information will then be recorded on the passport computer file and checks made to ensure that, whatever names a person may have acquired, a record will be made of the passport issue against the name at birth. The requirement for a full birth certificate will be implemented with the introdudion in May next year of the new application forms.
It is proposed to introduce passport printers into passport offices in the next financial year. These printers will not only improve the quality and production techniques in the passport offices but also enhance the security in the passport offices themselves and lead the way to introduction of machine readable script in passports. This latter move is designed to speed the handling of passengers through points of entry in Australia. In January this year I announced that the Government had decided to consider the establishment of an independent consultancy review to examine the security aspects of the passport issue and control system. This was deferred pending receipt of the Stewart Royal Commission's report. The Government has decided to give further consideration to the establishment of this review when the new measures, which I have outlined, have been in operation for a period.
Most of the changes which I have outlined will be implemented within the next six months but others require significant administrative preparation and will need to be phased in over a longer

Inquiry into Drug Trafficking

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3083

period. The decision to use Post Offices as lodgment centres, for example, will be phased in over an 18-month period to enable the new system to be explained to the public, administrative procedures to be established, training of postal clerks to be completed and the travel industry to adjust. The action which I have outlined reflects a general acceptance of the thrust of the recommendations of the Stewart Royal Commission report on passports, though some of the detail for the implementation of those recommendations differs from what was proposed by the Royal Commission for reasons already given.
As I indicated earlier in this statement, the Government welcomes the Stewart Royal Commission's report on passports and the contribution it has made to tightening the passport issue and control system. This, however, is not the end of the matter. There is not, nor can there be, a perfect infallible system. The Government, through my Department, will keep the passport issue and control procedures under constant review and further changes will no doubt be made in the future. The efforts to deter and prevent criminal elements in our society from undermining the basis of this society must be constant and unremitting. We are determined to do what we can in what is regrettably an unending battle. Therefore, though there may be some inconvenience to those many thousands of honest Australians who apply each year for passports, I am confident that they will understand the reasons why these measures are necessary. I present the following paper:

and visa control, particularly relating to people coming to this country on a short term basis. It is recognised, of course, that the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs is the appropriate department to deal with the matter on the basis of the reunion of long term migrants and their families. But the whole nub of what the Minister said, and indeed of what the Royal Commissioner said, is the difficulty of establishing identity. I note that many problems are associated with identity because identity can certainly be manufactured.
The Stewart Royal Commission stated a number of matters to which I should like to advert in order to show the nature of the problem. Page one of the interim report deals with a person named Clark who was well known for criminal activity-in fact murder and heroin charges. The inquiry clearly indicated that there was an abuse of the passport system. Clark obtained a number of passports; he was not entitled to any. He had at least five, none of which was in the name of Clark. If we look at the problem we will see that the abuse was enormous. At the time of his arrest Clark had passports issued in the names of Kriss, Newton, Gorrie, Sinclaire and Perkins. Clark was a New Zealand citizen anyway. This shows that it is very easy for the criminal mind to find ways of getting around what were deemed to be adequate identification procedures. Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford Smith)-by leave-The Opposition welcomes the statement by the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) in relation to the problems associated with passport abuse. I note that the Minister referred to the recommendations of interim report No.2 of the Stewart Royal Commission of Inquiry into Drug Trafficking. I also note that the Minister did not refer to recommendation No. 38 which dealt with the proposal that the issue of passports be transferred from the Department of Foreign Affairs to the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs. I do not want to dwell on that matter, but I am of the opinion that the Department of Foreign Affairs is anxious to preserve the capacity and the authority to issue passports, and there is some evidence to suggest that that should be so. There is no doubt there has been a lot of laxity and abuse. The Department of Foreign Affairs has a role to play in respect of passport

The report goes on to describe a person who was found with a great deal of heroin, was charged and remanded and eventually did not appear. When the police carried out investigations they discovered that that person had been issued with a passport in another name altogether-in fact in the name of a Kevin William Harris who died at the age of 13 months. That shows the sort of arrangement that can enter the criminal mind. This person, who got away with that passport in the name of an infant, went through the exercise of producing a certificate of identity. The certificate of identity, as explained by the Royal Commissioner, was given by Mr William Victor Cook, J. P. A justice of the peace would be entitled to do that. When Mr Wilfred Vincent Cook was interviewed he said that he had never heard of William Victor Cook. In other words, the identification of the justice of the peace was also forged. It was not hard for the criminal mind to do that because all

3084 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Inquiry into Drug Trafficking

he had to do was look up the telephone directory and notice that Mr Cook had 'J.P.' after his name.

We now come to the value of birth certificates. Those of us who have been involved from time to time in litigation and evidence know that a birth certificate is prima facie evidence of birth, but the certificate could be a complete forgery. If we look through the report of the Royal Commission we will also note that the official birth certificate paper has in fact been stolen. The criminal has access to everything, so if he can get the official imprimatur of State documents he will readily type in a lot of particulars. He will readily produce all sorts of evidence. That is perhaps a matter we cannot always prevent.

I shall now comment in relation to all the difficulties. I note that the Minister is now saying that he wants a full birth certificate to be produced, a matter with which I fully agree. We come now to the question of identity and the natural revulsion by people in a democracy-and this is what the Royal Commissioner said-having to have some identification; this can infringe the right to privacy. He asked quite fairly to get a balance between criminal activity and the fact that one gets a benefit from one's country, and a passport is a benefit. So we must have a balance. Whilst one can maintain privacy, one cannot do that to the extent that one allows criminal conduct to flourish, as has happened.

I am not at all critical of what the Minister has said. In fact, I say that it is a worthwhile contri-

But one has to be careful that the criminal mind does not work also to forge that identity.

The issue is that the Minister says that a 'full birth certificate' can be produced; and that is
right. But one has to be able to establish the identity of the person holding that birth certificate and I think this is the problem.

Mr LIONEL BOWEN-Exactly. Those who have been involved in earning an honest living in law have had to go through the exercise from time to time of convincing people that we can actually get this sort of evidence. The evidence would be something like this: A person would have to produce a full birth certificate. Attached to it would be an affidavit or a declaration by a member of the family or somebody close to the person concerned saying: 'Yes, you are the person referred to in the certificate' and that they have known the person for so many years. That person would have to sign the declaration. That would be one method. That does not get over the other fact that that person might also be a party to a forgery.
For example, the Commonwealth Bank does thi~ in respect of what are called probate applicatIOns. I remember them well. If one wants to withdraw the proceeds from an account of a deceased person, for the bank's purposes one has to have a full death certificate identified by a declaration before a justice of the peace or somebody the person may wish to nominate-usually a justice of the peace-saying that the person mentioned on the death certificate is one and the same as the person holding the bank account. The declaration would state that the person making the declaration knew the deceased person for a number of years and that that was the person who held the account. So, somebody would be qualified to identify that person.
One also would need to have somebody in the category identified by the Minister-he is now saying that a school teacher or a person of that nature is quite entitled to do this--identifying the person who was providing the original identification. So, one would need to have a double check. The bank went further and said in its terms that it wanted the signature of that identifying person identified by another bank.
The Minister may wish to look at precedent. Perhaps the Department can see plenty of precedents. Whilst it might be a little difficult, it is not too hard, acknowledging that the full birth certificate does not necessarily give the protection form forgery sought, to have identification by somebody who knew the person mentioned in the

I raise one final matter. I understand that a number of passports are lost when people are overseas. I understand also-this is not said in criticism-that, naturally, when an Australian has lost his passport he immediately goes to our embassy and asks for another one. He may need to get the passport as a matter of urgency. That system has a weakness. The passport has to be issued again on the basis of some form of identification. The identify of the person is checked by confirming that, in fact, a passport was issued. That practice does not really establish the identity. I think that in future the Minister might have to look at the need, when there is a claim that a passport has been lost, to put the person who has lost it to the difficulty of waiting to allow the embassy to contact Australia immediately in order to get a photostat of all the evidence that was furnished at the time when the original passport was granted so that the embassy may issue a new passport. At present the embassy is not able to do that when a matter of urgency is involved.
Also, a passport which is reissued overseas on the basis of the original passport having been lost is issued for a five-year period whereas the lost passport might have had only about three or four months to run. In other words, the reissue overseas is granting a brand new passport for a further

period without there being an opportunity to check the identity in detail. I think that ought to be a matter of some comment at some stage in relation to whether what I have suggested can be done.
Generally speaking, I think that what the Minister is about is what we all want to see-an ability to prevent crime. Obviously passports have been abused and will continue to be abused because we cannot readily stop all the criminal tendencies. However, looking at the simplicity of the methods that have been used in the past to gain passports illegally, I think certainly it can be seen that better systems have been prevailing for some time. I am concerned about the declaration aspect and the need for identification of the birth certificate by somebody who can be readily found. In turn, the identity of the person giving the identification also has to be established. I suggest that some of these people whom the Minister is now suggesting as appropriate should also exercise the function in relation to people obtaining a passport. Generally speaking, the Opposition welcomes the efforts being made by the Government.
SOUTH WEST TASMANIA
Ministerial Statement
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment)-by leave-I wish to inform the House of the Government's intentions regarding South West Tasmania. The Government reaffirms its wish that the Western Tasmania Wilderness National Parks be inscribed on the World Heritage List along with the other nomination of Lord Howe Island. If these nominations are accepted by the World Heritage Committee, these areas will join Kakadu National Park, the Great Barrier Reef and the Willandra Lakes Region on the World Heritage List.
Honourable members will be aware that the World Heritage Committee is meeting in Paris commencing 13 December. Professor Ralph Slatyer, the former Australian Ambassador to the United Nations Educational Scientific and Cultural Organisation and the outgoing Chairman of the World Heritage Committee, will lead the Australian delegation. Professor Slatyer will be assisted by Dr D. F. McMichael, Secretary, Department of Home Affairs and Environment, Mr Max Bourke, Director of the Australian Heritage Commission and Mr Ernst Willheim of the Attorney-General's Department. Additionally, the Government in response to the request by the Premier of Tasmania, Mr Gray, has issued an invitation for the Tasmanian Government to be

3086 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

South West Tasmania

represented in the delegation subject to the conditions which normally apply to State representatives on Australian Government delegations. For the information of honourable members, it is the practice at meetings of the World Heritage Committee for the nominating country to respond only to questions put by the Committee. Notwithstanding this limitation on participation in the discussions on the nomination, I hope the invitation to Tasmania will be accepted.
The Government has provided to the Bureau of the World Heritage Committee the additional information sought on the nomination of the Western Tasmania Wilderness National Parks for inscription on the World Heritage List. I gave Professor Slatyer this morning, just before his departure for Paris. the Government's response which he will deliver to the Bureau prior to the meeting of the World Heritage Committee. In responding to the request the Government noted that the Bureau has recommended the inscription on the World Heritage List of the Western Tasmania Wilderness National Parks. I should like to confirm to the House the Government's firm belief in the international importance of this area and its outstanding biological and cultural qualities which have been recognised by its inclusion on the Register of the National Estate. I make special reference to the archaeological sites, the huon pine habitat, the temperate rainforest and the other unique features of this area.
The Commonwealth Government has cooperated with the Tasmanian Government over a number of years in documenting the natural resources of the area as a basis for future planning management. The Commonwealth looks to continuip.g this co-operative approach. Towards this end lne Government stands by its commitment to provide financial and other assistance in the establishment and management of a national park of world significance which includes a substantial wilderness component. Nonetheless, the Government has to face facts. The Premier of Tasmania has stated firmly, ap.d with the backing of legislation, that the proposed dam is to proceed. Accordingly, the Government has indicated to the World Heritage Committee that the Tasmanian Government is constructing a hydro-electric power scheme in the nominated area and that the Commonwealth has been and is discussing the scheme with the Tasmanian Government with a view to minimising the damage that the dam might cause.
The Government does not intend to intervene in the construction of t.he dam. The Government, in its discussion with the Tasmanian Government, will make it clear that it will back its concern for

these features with money, manpower and technical resources. I am pleased to inform the House that the Tasmanian Government has agreed that the Commonwealth will be consulted in the development of the management plan for the area. The mechanism for consultation is to be the subject of discussions with the Tasmanian Government. Whilst Fraser Cave and other caves of archaeological significance will be flooded by the dam, let me assure the House that every endeavour will be made to preserve this important part of our heritage.
The House will understand that the position taken by the Government will be welcomed by parts of the community and opposed by others. The expressions of concern have been clear and widespread. Nevertheless, the Government has to strike a balance between competing intentions. Confrontation between the Commonwealth and the States and between individuals in the community is not the road to prosperity, development or conservation of our heritage. The Government believes, on balance, that its decision is the right one and seeks from the community support in its continuing discussions with the Tasmanian Government.
Finally, I join the Premier of Tasmania in exhorting those that oppose the construction of the dam not to interfere physically with construction works. I abhor violence, as I am sure do all in this House. Nothing can be gained from violent confrontation. I present the following paper:

Mr WEST-We have the Minister saying that he understands the archaeological significance of the area that is going to be flooded but he assures us all that every endeavour will be made to preserve the cave. I refer honourable members to the conclusion of the report of the Senate Select Committee on South West Tasmania that was brought down in the Senate some weeks ago. It states:
11.15 The Committee heard evidence from Australian archaeologists and received copies of letters forwarded to the Prime Minister from eminent international archaeologists on the significance of the archaeological discoveries in an area near the Franklin River which would be inundated by the proposed Gordon-below-Franklin scheme.
11.16 The Committee accepts the views of these experts that the archaeological discoveries near the Franklin River are of such international importance that they should not be inundated.
The reason the Committee said that is that these archaeologists told the Committee that Fraser Cave shows evidence of being the southernmost point on this planet ever reached by prehistoric man. The Aborigines were there in Fraser Cave 20,000 years ago-that is about 15,000 years before Cheops built the Great Pyramid. Fraser Cave is one of the great archaeological sites on this planet. Yet this mean-minded, mean-spirited Government, without any idea of what this site might mean to the world cultural and natural heritage, is willing to go along with a petty, parochial government like the Government of Tasmania that says to the Minister and the Government: 'You're only the Commonwealth. Keep your nose out of our affairs. We know better than you. We know that we need this dam for an economic purpose'.
Let me give the lie then, having established the environmental case, to the economic case. There is no economic case whatsoever for the dam. It will cost up to $1 billion to produce 178 megawatts of power. That is what it will be because the

Pieman River Scheme has already blown out to almost $800m. It started off in the early 1970s at $120m; now it is $800m. The HEC already consumes 30 per cent of the State's general purpose capital funds, 60 per cent of its total loan funds, 94 per cent of major semi-governmental borrowing and 95 per cent of the infrastructure borrowing program that has now been terminated. Now it has to borrow on national and international markets at ruling interest rates.
The recession is hitting Tasmania. We know that. But Tasmania is going to create power for industries that it does not have because hydro industrialisation has demonstrably failed. Tasmania has not attracted a new industry of any power consuming consequences for 10 years. Comalco Aluminium (Bell Bay) Pty Ltd, Electrolytic Zinc of Australasia Ltd, Australian Paper Manufacturers Ltd and Tasmanian Electro Metallurgical Co. Pty Ltd have joined all those companies around Australia which are laying off labour. The Department of National Development and Energy has said that it will not need any new power in Tasmania until 2000 AD. Between August 1981 and August 1982 there was a 5.5 per cent fall in power consumption in Tasmania. The Senate Select Committee report said that the HEC had not convinced that Committee that it needed to start a major project like that now of such enormous cost and for which there is no demonstrable need for the power. The Committee recommended a moratorium of at least three years. The Committee knew that Tasmania could then go to either a scheme on the King River or the Arthur River to produce a combined 140 megawatts of power or, alternatively, a thermal power station.
