GOVERNMENT'S BUDGET STRATEGY
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The collapse of the Government's Budget strategy.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (4,45)-Seven weeks ago, on 20 October, the Opposition brought before this House a matter of public importance alleging that the Government had falsified the Budget by understating certain expenditure and overstating taxation receipts, and so coming up with an artificially low deficit which the Government wanted for the purposes of a possible early election. When we put that to the Treasurer (Mr Howard), he vehemently denied those charges, He said that the figures had been presented in good faith, that they were the best estimates available and that the increased deficit, which he had admitted to at that time, was only small beer compared with the total level of government outlays; and so really what was all this carryon about.
What do we see now, just seven weeks from that time? We see that the Treasurer has now admitted that the deficit is likely to be about $4,000m which is some $2,300m more than the original Budget estimate of about $1.7 billion, which figure, of course, is an extraordinary increase over the original Budget deficit estimate. In

Question Time today the Treasurer said that his actual estimate was around $3,750m. But he seemed to suggest that there was likely to be a further extension of that estimate at least to the area of $4,000m. I think I can use that figure, particularly as that is the figure he also gave to the Premiers. What we have had, therefore, is an increase in the Budget deficit of 140 per cent in the course of the few months that have passed since the deficit was mentioned in this House. This is a quite stupendous blowout which will take the deficit to the highest level in money terms than it has ever been in our history. It may even go higher than the $4,000m.
At the same time as the Budget's financial parameters are going haywire the economy is clearly going downhill at an alarming rate. The economic situation now is far worse than was estimated by the Government in its Budget forecast. So on both fronts-on the financial front in regard to the Budget's financial estimates and on the economic front-the Budget is literally falling apart. The Budget strategy that was proclaimed in August is now totally irrelevant. Who could have any faith in a Budget which has a deficit blowing out at a rate of $135m a week, which is the rate at which this deficit is now blowing out. That is a quite extraordinary rate of blowout. This deficit is now blowing out at a rate of something like $19m a day. That figure of $4,000m is not the bottom line. The Treasurer, when asked by me in Question Time today to give a guarantee that it would not be any higher than that, did not do so. Clearly, it is quite possible that when Parliament resumes in the new year the Treasurer will say that the figure is now something well in excess of $4,000m. Certainly it will be if the economy continues to slide at its present rate.
Why is this enormous blowout occurring? In our view there are two basic reasons. It has resulted partly from the Government's deception in relation to the Budget--its deliberate falsification of the Budget which it dollied up for electoral purposes. I will quickly allude to several elements of that falsification. Firstly, there were the unemployment benefit payments. The Government estimated its level of unemployment benefit payments in the Budget on a basis of an average 395,000 registered unemployed. In fact, the actual numbers on the unemployment benefit at the time of the Budget were already 10,000 above the figure for the month of July, the first month of that financial year. So it is quite clear that the unemployment benefit figure was patently false. Of course it is now admitted to be so by the fact that the Treasurer is admitting an increase of some $600m or 38 per cent on the Budget

Government Budget Strategy

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3091

estimate-an extraordinary increase in that amount. He now says that the number of unemployment benefit recipients is estimated to be something like 525,000-130,000 more than he estimated in the Budget only a little over three months ago. Interestingly, that figure is also 193,000 more than the figure for the last financial year. So it is quite clear that this figure was patently and totally false and must have been known by the Government to be false when it brought down this Budget.
Similarly, in respect of employment, the Government's estimate of no decline in the total number of wage and salary earners was nonsense. In the last financial year when we had a 2.3 per cent growth of non-farm product we had no growth in employment. This year, with a Budget forecast of no growth in gross non-farm product-that is, no growth in the production of non-farm goods and services-the Government has estimated that there will be the same employment position as last year; that is, just no growth. Clearly, if we have approximately 2t per cent growth in one year of goods and services associated with no growth in employment and then we have a year of no growth in output we will have a much worse employment situation. The Government knew that. But it still put forward its Budget forecast in regard to tax receipts on the basis of no change in the employment figure. Of course it was nonsense! It has now admitted that it will be down by at least one per cent. I confidently forecast that it will fall by considerably more than one per cent.
The Government estimated that $255m would be gained from recoupment tax. Knowing that it had great trouble and would face pressure from opponents of the legislation within its own ranks both inside and outside this Parliament it believed that it may well have to make concessions. It eventually made those concessions. As a result, instead of getting $255m it will get $1 OOm, which is $155m less than it said it would get in the course of this financial year mainly because of its backdowns in respect of the legislation.
Then there was the drought. Two weeks after the Budget was brought down in this House the Government announced a package in respect of the drought which would add $356m to Budget outlays. It cannot possibly argue that, at the time of the Budget, it did not know that there was a drought going on in this country and that it had been going on in a very severe form for months if not years. It was quite clear that the Government must have had in mind a drought package. It kept it out of the Budget figures by delaying the announcement of that drought relief until two weeks

after the Budget. That means that the outlays will be some $350m above the estimated level in the Budget for that reason alone. Indeed, there have been suggestions recently that that amount of $350m will be considerably higher. But in the figures given to us today by the Treasurer we see that he is sticking to his $356m figure. Even at that level it is a significant addition to outlays which should have been in the Budget figures and it should not just be put there as part of that whole process of falsification of that deficit.
This spectacular blowout of the deficit which is occurring is partly a reflection of the falsity of the original Budget estimates. Indeed, there are many other areas to which I could point but because of time restrictions and other reasons I will not go into them. However, this blowout of the deficit is also partly a reflection of the collapse of the Australian economy and the consequent increase in expenditure on unemployment benefit and the loss of tax revenue which that collapse entails. The estimates given by the Treasurer at Question Time today are a clear indication of this. Pay-asyou-earn tax is down $323m on the Budget estimates because of the reduced employment and some reduction in wage growth. Indirect tax is down $355m because of the recessed economy. People spend less on goods and services and so pay less in the way of indirect taxes. Unemployment benefits are up not just because of the original falsifications but also because of the much worse state of the Australian economy and the much greater levels of unemployment.
All this reflects falling employment, falling sales, falling production and growing unemployment which marks this economy at present. In every key respect the Budget estimates about the state of the Australian economy in 1982-83 are now exposed as utterly unrealistic. In every case the position is worse than the Budget estimates would have had us believe.
I will just quickly run through some of those key economic forecasts in the Budget. Let us see how out of touch they are with the current state of the economy as we know it. The Budget estimate was for a further decline in home building. In fact in the September quarter we saw that the growth of home building approvals fell spectacularly--it was 34 per cent less in the 12 months to September 1982 than in the 12 months to September 1981. That represents some further decline. It was estimated that the growth in real private consumption expenditure would slow to around 1t per cent in 1982-83. We now have declining retail sales in real terms. After one adjusts for inflation retail sales declined in the September quarter. The latest figures for motor vehicle registrations also

3092 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Government Budget Strategy

show a decline in real terms in the September quarter. So the key areas of personal consumption are falling, not just declining, in their rate of growth.
It was forecast that real private business fixed investment would show some decline. In fact, we find in the September quarter it was 5 per cent lower than it was a year before. The Treasury, in its 'Round-up of Economic Statistics', stated:
In view of the continuing deterioration in the tone of the economy since the July / August survey period it now appears that the fall in real business fixed capital expenditure will be greater than suggested at Budget time.
The fall in the expenditure sure is greater. We were told that the consumer price index would rise by 10.75 per cent. The latest figure is 12.3 per cent in the 12 months to September. That figure is double the level of our trading partners, which is around 6.6 per cent. It was said that there would be some decline in rural farm production. In fact, we find that the net value of rural production during this year, as estimated by the Bureau of Agricultural Economics, will be about half that of last year and the lowest level since the mid-1950s. Nothing explicit was mentioned in the Budget on factory production but there was mention of nonfarm product showing no fall. In fact, we find that in the year to September factory production has fallen by 10 per cent to below the level in 1976-77. The recent massive lay-offs in manufacturing show that output is falling even further.
We were told that there is unlikely to be any growth in employment. As I have already mentioned, there has already been a decline in employment. The Government has now, through the Treasurer, agreed it will be at least a one per cent decline. It was said that there will be a marked increase in recorded unemployment. There certainly has been a marked increase in recorded unemployment. The figure which we now have for the number of unemployed benefit recipients is 193,000 more than the figure we had last year, which is a spectacularly enormous increase.
Seasonally adjusted employment figures have grown by 113,500 during the four months from July to October. Quite clearly, there has been an enormous increase in unemployment. We are looking at a figure of about 700,000 unemployed people in the months of January and February. If the increase in unemployment is only the same in actual numbers between October and February this year as it was last year, there will be 653,000 unemployed people in February. Of course, it is going to be more than that with all the lay-offs. So we are looking at least at something of the order of 700,000 unemployed people, which will represent over 10 per cent unemployment by

February. That is the unemployment situation which was described in the Budget as a 'marked increase' in unemployment. It is an horrendous increase in unemployment. Quite clearly, it is far worse than the Budget would have had us believe.
The Budget estimate forecasts were for nonfarm products to remain broadly unchanged in 1982-83 and with some decline in overall gross domestic products. The current situation is that in the September quarter gross non-farm product has already fallen by 0.3 per cent and GPD by 0.6 per cent. Given what has happened in the rest of the economy since that time, the fall has become greater since. So there will be a much worse situation in respect of national accounts than was indicated in the Budget. On the other hand, the balance of payments situation has not deteriorated. We have been affected by the international recession but, in fact, in the September quarter a major addition to growth came from exports. So the Government's suggestion that we have been suddenly damaged by the international recession does not fit with what was happening to the Australian economy because, in the September quarter, the exports kept things going.
The fact is, therefore, that the Budget strategy has completely and utterly collapsed. Its forecasts in regard to financial parameters and the state of the economy are totally at odds with what is happening in the economy. The Budget should, therefore, be scrapped and a new Budget brought before this Parliament which addresses the real state of the nation and which seeks to treat the real cause of our economic problems at present, which is the collapse of demand resulting partly from the drought and poor export markets but, much more importantly, from contractionary budgetary and monetary policies. The Government's tearing off with a wages freeze as the answer to our problems ignores the basic fact that our economic problems are primarily caused by this collapse of demand. It is budgetary and monetary policy which addresses that question. The budgetary and monetary strategy has to be changed to ensure there is an increase in demand in order that we can get some economic growth back in this country. The Treasurer should now bring forward a new Budget which has those elements in it to give the economy a chance to recover and not continue in a vast depression, which is its current state.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Jarman)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (5.0)-1 am indebted to the honourable member

Government Budget Strategy

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3093

for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) for his concluding remarks. He gave the House some idea of the alternative Budget strategy which he believes should be adopted. In those concluding remarks, he said that domestic demand in Australia was collapsing because of a contractionary Budget strategy. I can only conclude from that statement that the honourable member for Gellibrand would be arguing for a substantially higher Budget deficit than the one towards which we are now heading. He argues that existing strategy is far too contractionary and that we have to do something about that strategy in order to pump up demand in the Australian economy.
The honourable gentleman's speech was remarkable for one other feature. He made only one reference to the international influences on the Australian economy. If I may say so with respect, that was a slightly incorrect reference. It is true that our export performance in the September quarter has been better than we thought, but overwhelmingly the reason why our balance of payments was stronger in the September quarter has been the heavy purchasing of securities in Australia by overseas investment institutions, particularly from Japan; in fact, it has been the flow of capital into those investments in Australia more than a more buoyant export performance that has achieved that result. I would have hoped that the honourable member's definition or analysis would be more correct than I believe in fact it was. Apart from that reference he made no other reference to the international circumstances which are now affecting the Australian economy. I will come to that matter in just a moment.
It is necessary-if we are to understand our present dilemmas and find a way out of themthat we first understand why we are in the situation which is now facing the Australian economy. Before I do that, let me go back to the introductory remarks of the honourable member for Gellibrand and simply say, as I have said to him on earlier occasions, that I reject utterly, completely and without any qualification his claim that there was any deliberate falsification of figures by the Government in this Budget.
Mr Young-There had to be.
Mr HOWARD-The honourable member for Port Adelaide and any other honourable member can interject until he or she is blue in the face. But that will not alter the completely unambiguous and unequivocal nature of the dellial that I give on behalf of the Government on that matter. The fact remains that the Australian economy has worsened very markedly since the Budget was brought down. It is true that the estimates of

growth and activity on which the Budget was put together have turned out to be much more conservative in terms of the extent to which they estimated the worsening of the Australian economy.
It is also worth reflecting that at the time when the Budget was brought down some economic commentators in Australia said that the Treasury forecasts contained in the Budget were, in fact far too pessimistic. But what has happened is that economic activity has slowed down to a much greater extent than was then calculated. The drought has worsened. The international recession has continued and has not abated as some thought it might earlier on this year. Wage increases have continued to eat into our profitability. How remarkable it is that the alternative Treasurer in this country can make a speech for a quarter of an hour about our economic circumstances at present, when we are facing the highest unemployment in this country for forty years, and say absolutely nothing about the disastrous slump in the profitability of Australian companies which has happened over the last few months, and say absolutely nothing about the underlying reality that, over the last 18 months, profitability of Australian industry has been shot to pieces. As a consequence, the capacity of Australian industry to employ people has been crippled by the wage increases that have gone on over that period.
It is no accident that for almost 20 years we had a profit share of the national cake in this country of about 17 per cent or 18 per cent a year. While that remained, the unemployment level in this country was so low that it was the envy of the industrialised world. It is no accident that when the profit share-that is, the wherewithal for people to be employed-started to dissipate the unemployment level in this country begin to rise.
It is no accident that there was a small improvement in the unemployment situation in 1980 when there was some recovery in the profitability of Australian business to about 15 per cent for a short period. But, affected as it was by the onset of wage demands beyond the capacity of the economy to afford, that profit share has now fallen back to the lowest level it has been at since 1974. The fall in that profit share has produced, of course, the high unemployment which is our common concern at present.
The honourable member for Gellibrand made a speech about economic policy and, remarkably, said nothing about those circumstances. It is truly remarkable that the honourable member for Gellibrand can make a speech and not even mention them. Even his Labor colleagues at a State

3094 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Government Budget Strategy

level are not quite as myopic as that. Even they are prepared to acknowledge-~perhaps not to the extent that we and other people around the country have argued--that there is a link between profitability and employment, there is a link between the capacity of firms to make a living and therefore employ people. It is truly remarkable that the honourable member for Gellibrand can make a speech of the kind that he has made today and say absolutely nothing about those realities.
Let me return to the question of the Budget deficit. It does not please me in the slightest that the Budget deficit is now, according to the latest estimate, about $3,700m to $3,800m. I used the expression yesterday in this chamber at the Premiers Conference 'approaching $4,000m'. I have already indicated to the House the principal elements on the expenditure and revenue sides
which have made up the additions. There is, of course, the increase in unemployment benefit. I have given to the House before and I shall repeat the reasons for that significantly higher estimate. The calculations that were made at the time of the Budget and presented to the Government at the time of the Budget were based upon assumptions as to the ratio of unemployment benefit recipients to the total number of unemployed, which had reflected the experience of a period of some 18 months or two years.
What has happened since then and what has rendered those estimates deficient since the time of the Budget has been that there have been two eventualities. Firstly, there has been a sharper rise in the total number of unemployed people over and above what was estimated at the time of the Budget. Secondly, within that rise there has been an even sharper rise in the ratio of the number of people drawing benefits to the total number of people out of work. That has reflected the higher incidence of main breadwinners in families being thrown out of work. The experiences at centres such as Newcastle and Wollongong in New South Wales and Geelong and Whyalla in other parts of Australia provide some very unhappy examples. That is the explanation as to why the unemployment benefit figure has had to be revised. It is not some kind of three-card trick by the Government. To suggest that is not only to impugn the political members of the Government, which is part and parcel of the parliamentary process, whether it has foundation or not, but also to implicate apolitical Public Service advisers as though they were part of some giant overall conspiracy to confuse the country. That accounts for some $603m.
We also have drought assistance of $356m, natural disaster relief of $52m and, because of the projected increase in the deficit, the provision of a

further $93m to service the additional borrowings that will be necessary to finance that higher deficit. There has also been a revision downwards in the estimated collections of revenue. Because of a higher level of unemployment and because of slower growth there has been a revision downwards in the projected collections of pay-as-youearn tax of some $323m. We also expect to collect, largely but not entirely because of the drought-there has been a heavy fall in many professional and non-salary incomes earned by people other than farmers-about $123m less in the provisional tax area.
I have already mentioned the revision downwards in the recoupment tax area. That is a very difficult figure to estimate with any precision. The revised estimate may well turn out to be on the conservative side. I do not know and the commissioner of Taxation has, quite properly, warned me that it is a very difficult figure to estimate. There will be, because of slower economic activity and reduced consumer spending. a shortfall in sales tax collections because fewer things are being bought. Some $84m has been allowed for that. Excise duty is down $170m, which is accounted for in part at least by a fall in the purchases of traditional excise items in the alcohol
and tobacco areas. Customs duty collections will be down by $100m. On the other side, there will be some increases in the collection of the crude oil
levy.
I think it also ought to be said that the Government is extremely unhappy about and derives no pleasure at all from the fact that we will have a higher deficit this year. The honourable member for Gellibrand said that it is the biggest deficit in history. Of course it is the higher deficit in money terms but we all know that that is a fairly inadequate and phoney basis of comparison. The real size and dimension of a deficit is its proportion of gross domestic product. Of course, in money terms a deficit now will be immensely bigger than what it was 10 years ago. It is 2.5 percent of GDP. In 1975-76 the deficit was 4.9 per cent of GDP. It approaches 5 per cent in the United States, 5+ per cent in Japan and 6t per cent in Canada.
The honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke), who has been in the news lately, said just after the Budget was brought down that it was inadequate and that he wanted one that was the average for the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development. If the honourable member for Wills had his way we would have a deficit of $6,000m. Let not the honourable member for Gellibrand come into this House and say that what we are doing is inadequate. His colleague the honourable member for Wills wants

Government Budget Strategy

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3095

the deficit to be $6,000m. I would be interested to know whether the honourable member for Gellibrand reckons that that would be a responsible figure, or whether it would be one that we ought to walk away from.
Whilst I draw no comfort at all from those sorts of comparisonsI do not want to be in that league-these factors ought to be born in mind. It also ought to be remembered that it has been the common experience of every industrialised country that in a time of recession one's revenues fall and one's outlays increase simply because there is less activity, less tax is collected and there are larger social security payments that have to be met on the expenditure side of the Budget. What is happening to the Australian economy at present is duplicating what is happening in other areas of the world economy. I do not pretend that the Australian economy is not going through ~ery difficult circumstances at the moment. That is self evident. I believe that, in concert with the State governments, we made a significant stride forward yesterday. It is regrettable that the only group at a political level in Australia that appears to want nothing at ali to do with what happened yesterday is the Federal Parliamentary Labor Party. Even its colleagues at the State level who are in government know darned well that what was done yesterday will make a valuable contribution to improving the situation.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Jarman)- Order! The Minister's time has expired.
Mr HOWE (Batman) (5.15)-This debate is about the enormous blowout that has occurred to the deficit-a deficit which the Treasurer (Mr Howard) is now speaking of as likely to be in the order of $4 billion by the end of this financial year. I do not think that the argument of the Opposition was so much that it is all that frightening to have a deficit which is 2t per cent of gross domestic product-I certainly did not hear the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) suggest that. I think the criticism that the Opposition has of the Government in respect of the deficit is that it is a deficit that was not forecast in the Budget Papers. Certainly it is a deficit arrived at almost by accident. It is a deficit which may perhaps have been arrived at as a part of a kind of political accident because the Budget this year, insofar as it was devised-it would appear to be mostly patched together-was devised in a heavily political context, as every Budget of any Fraser Government has been.
This year the political context was that of the election. The Government put together a Budget which was going to produce a number of goodies

to be delivered in the period before Christmas and within which time an election could be held. Frankly, the Government, the Treasurer and presumably senior levels of the Treasury were after a while perhaps not too concerned about much else. That is what they were dealing with. I think John Stone indicated some views he had about that. The Budget got under way but very quickly fell apart. What the Treasurer is trying to suggest to us is that it is a matter of no moment for a Budget, within three or four months, literally to fall apart. The economy, which was in a very serious situation earlier this year, since the Budget has been brought down is moving to a state of near collapse. The Treasurer would argue that it is a matter of no moment that the Budget that was devised had nothing to do with the central reality of Australian society today, which is that we are in a depression, with mass unemployment.
It would seem to me that it would need an extraordinarily successful Treasurer to persuade the Parliament or the country that he knew what he was about when he devised the Budget in August this year-a year in which we are likely to see unemployment reach at least 700,000 people. There we are talking about official figures, not about the hidden unemployed on top of that. How can he say that he anticipated nothing of the dramatic nature of these consequences? That would be quite extraordinary. I think it is not extraordinary when we look at conservative governments and conservative treasurers. In this country Treasury, governments, and Treasurers have never been concerned about economic planning. They have never been seriously concerned about seeking to manage the economy, in perhaps the way that many governments within the European context might have been. They have always been much more susceptible to political interest. What this Budget was about was political interest, namely, the neck of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser). Now the country is having to live with the consequences.
I think it is well to reinforce just how bad the situation is. I would have thought governments were about not simply addressing the statistics but addressing problems facing people within this country. What the statistics of the last 12 months and in prospect indicate is massive suffering for Australian people. The honourable member for Gellibrand referred to what had happened in relation to the housing industry, where home building approvals fell 34 per cent between September 1981 and September 1982 by a third. A fall of that scale and magnitude means that across Australia thousands of people who require housing are not getting it. If we put that fact alongside the fact

3096 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Government Budget Strategy

that no government has ever been meaner than the Fraser Government in relation to public housing we have what adds up to a housing crisis within this country. That impacts upon thousands and thousands of families right across the country.
If one looks at real private consumption expenditure growth in this country it is estimated that that could slow to around I t per cent in 1982-83. That is what the Budget suggested. The value of retail sales, seasonally adjusted for the September quarter, was only 0.8 per cent higher than for the June quarter. Since the consumer price index rose 3.5 per cent in the September quarter the real value of retail sales fell substantially. Again that is just a figure. What it amounts to is a dramatic decline in demand. Because goods and services are not selling through those retail outlets it means that the factories which produce these goods are in a situation of dramatic decline. The Treasurer may not have much feeling for this. However, I am sure honourable members have visited factories and seen people being put off and heard people saying that their factories are being used by the big retailers as warehouses and that, having been through periods of uncertainty, they are now faced with a situation of not knowing whether they will ever work again in those factories. That is not statistics. That is a result of terrible planning. It indicates the Government is not concerned about the level of demand.
The Opposition has been saying that it is crucial to keep up demand. When the Opposition says that it is not coming up with some kind of new idea because fundamental to economic policy throughout the postwar period has been the view that if we are concerned about employment levels of aggregate demand have to be maintained. If the Budget this year had been designed to do thatthat was why we had a $4 billion deficit in prospect-perhaps one might be a bit sympathetic. There was nothing in the Budget strategy to suggest that what the Government was on about was boosting demand so that people would be placed back into employment. The crisis that this country faces is a crisis for people.
The Treasurer suggests that the Opposition is not interested in profits but is interested only in employment, something quite distinct from profit. If we look at what is happening to investment in this country we can recognise quite clearly that business is not investing and that this is resulting in the lack of new jobs. There has been a dramatic deterioration in investment. I do not think anyone would suggest that there is no relationship at all between profits and investment. Nor can we simply sustain the argument that we should somehow arrange a dramatic wage cut throughout the

Australian economy. Indeed, that is what the Government is about. It is not about a pause or a freeze in wages but it is about a real wage cut. I very much doubt that as a result of that real wage cut there will be increased profits because there will also be a dramatic further decline in demand. That is what all the businesses tell me they are concerned about-not the wages paid. They are concerned about the lack of demand in the market place. Even assuming that paradoxically, as a result of reducing demand, somehow there will be some increase in profits, who is to say that that profit will be invested in a way that will get people back to work? That is nothing more than the rhetoric of the Government.
The Government is not in this exercise of so called wage freeze or wage cut, as I would call it. It has no strategy designed to create employment. The Government simply is hoping that cutting back people's wages and theoretically increasing profits will somehow increase employment. If the Government really had been seriously concerned about employment it would have met the Premiers' request for increased loan funds to boost capital works and to develop a capital works program that was genuinely designed to lead to a reconstruction of the Australian economy. However, that would involve a plan, not an ad hoc gimmick devised in a few days for the purpose of seeking to win a by-election. That is a gimmick which will explode very quickly and about which I would imagine in the next few months we will hear very little more.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr BRAITHWAITE (Dawson) (5.25)-1 enter this debate to show that the Australian Labor Party has demonstrated once again its ignorance of the economic situation and its inability to control or handle it. We can listen all day to the problems but it is the answers to those problems that we are seeking from this House and this Government. The Flinders by-election last weekend was a fair reflection of the fact that the people of Australia realise that the Labor Party has no economic policy. One of the decisions it has made since then is to increase the staff of its spokesman on Treasury matters to help him explain his policies. I think that in itself is an indictment.
It is very important for us to realise that a Budget strategy is based on the best advice at the time of its presentation, with reasonable forecasts. If the Opposition were put in the same situation as the Government it would have to look at the reasons for the Budget blowout. I suggest to the

Government Budget Strategy

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3097

Opposition and to the House that no aspect of that blowout in the Budget was at the discretion of this Government or of the Treasurer (Mr Howard). All the matters which have contributed to it--the drought, unemployment, international competition and the international economic situation-have worsened since the Budget. I would like to pick up a few comments from the Opposition. By its own admission its Budget strategy at the time of the presentation of the Government's Budget involved a deficit of some $4 billion. Other estimates have put its deficit at $5 billion or $6 billion based on the situation at that time. I ask the Opposition what its strategy would be. The answer can be seen from the response to the Budget by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) when he implied that the Labor Party would spend its way out of the problem; in other words, it would print more money and have no concern for the inflationary impact of that.
Another thing that worries me is that the Opposition now says that it is not all that frightened by a large Budget deficit. This is where I feel it is very important to draw a distinction between Labor politics and our politics. It is very important that the deficit should be strictly controlled because if it expands beyond reasonable limits all we are doing is spending in our generation what future generations will have to pay back. That is a very critical point. I would hate to see any members opposite involved in company management and trying to run a business at a profit because they would be trying to spend their way out and would not be looking for reasonable options. I mentioned the drought. We should not forget the real and deep implications of that for the people of Australia. It is with some reluctance that I draw your attention, Mr Deputy Speaker, to the fact that all members of the Opposition, except one, have retired from the chamber without staying to listen to the real facts and figures.
Mr Howard-Isn't it their matter of public importance?
Mr BRAITHWAITE-It is their matter of public importance. With the exception of one member, they are not here to participate. The drought has had a large impact on the blowing out of the Budget. The figures presented suggest that over $300m has been spent in drought relief alone, with $50m being spent on natural disaster relief associated with it. The flow-on effect is a lower consumer demand from that sector for motor vehicles and housing, which show us how great and difficult the situation is.
I also want to talk about international competitiveness. Without doubt the people of Australia

have realised at long last that we have priced ourselves out of international competition. I am very closely associated with many great industries in Queensland, including the mining, sugar, beef and dairying industries. They are all facing serious international competition in a market in which they cannot adjust their prices.
Mr Adermann-And tobacco.
Mr BRAITHWAITE-And the tobacco industry. It must be realised that we can put our economy on a proper footing only by being competitive in an international market in which we must trade to survive. No government has done more in regard to international competition than this one. At the recent General Agreement on Tariff and Trade conference the Commonwealth of Australia pressed the point that we cannot trade in the open market if heavily subsidised European Economic Community products are to compete with us. This affects our main exports, particularly our grain and sugar and many of our other primary products. An inability to trade against fair competition will have of course an effect on the blowing out of the Budget figures.
Another aspect of the change in Budget philosophy is the unemployment situation. I have said for many years that there is only one problem with Australians: We are living at a standard that the country cannot afford. At long last, with the impact and the influence of the drought, this is being realised and appreciated. The figures for the last quarter are very revealing. They point out the result of the drought by showing that gross farm product in the quarter to September fell by 5.1 per cent. I suggest that the worst of the impact of the drought was not shown in those figures; that is yet to come. Over 12 months the figure declined by one per cent. Another revealing factor in the quarter's figures was that gross non-farm product increased by 0.1 per cent. That is really no growth at all. The reason for that 0.1 per cent growth was that wages, salaries and allowances, which go to make up that figure, increased by 16.9 per cent while company profits-the surplus cash flow from companies-declined by 13.6 per cent.
In Australia we have an economic pie. Over the last 12 months that pie has undoubtedly shrunk, and the situation cannot be tolerated in which one sector of the community feels it can get in real terms the same sized slice of that pie as previously without consideration for the rest of the community. That situation has meant that business cannot cope with declining profits and cannot employ. Basically, labour is pricing itself out of the market. That is why I applaud the positive attempt being made to bring about a wages pause.

3098 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Petitions

But a wages pause in itself is not an answer to all the problems that I see at the moment. It is significant that that wages pause in one form or another has been accepted by all State governments and, I understand, by the employer associations. It remains for the unions to declare their situation through the Australian Council of Trade Unions in further talks this week. But a pause has been accepted, whether it be for six months or 12 months. That pause will enable the people of Australia to come to grips with what we are trying to say. If we need an economic philosophy at this moment, it is that each Australian should take a little less and give a little more. If that philosophy were expounded throughout Australia there would be some hope for us to correct our internal situation in spite of world economic conditions and world competition.
The pause is just a start for something I would like to have looked at further and developed. If after the pause the base on which we pay wages and salaries is not corrected we will not have achieved anything. Recently I had the opportunity to go to America to see the industrial situation there. As an honourable member said earlier today, the Americans are confounded by the situation in Australia whereby people are paid more to go on holiday than to be kept in employment. In addition to that we grant our workers four weeks' paid leave in comparison with the maximum of two weeks which is granted in America. In Australia a 17t per cent holiday loading is payable and penalty rates apply after an eight-hour working day. That is one of the other problems. Australian workers do not need first to work 40 hours whereas overseas the overtime penalty rate applies only after a person has worked his 40 hours. Certainly if industry responds to the challenge that has been set to it now, employment numbers will come up as a result of the savings by the Commonwealth and the States in their Public Service wage bills. There will also be the opportunity for business, if we allow it to be more profitable, to employ in a like fashion.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-The honourable member's time has expired. The discussion is concluded.
PETITIONS
The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Vietnam Veterans
To the Right Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:

(I) that US. Australian and allied forces used huge amounts of toxic chemicals for military purposes. such as Agents Orange. Blue and White. in Vietnam during the war there;
(2) many of these chemicals were, and are. known to be highly dangerous to human health;
(3) Australia's Vietnam Veterans and their families are suffering many severe problems which may be due to their exposure to these chemicals: and
(4) the Vietnam Veterans Association of Australia has been seeking a Royal Commission for well over two years to ensure an independent investigation of these matters.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly pray that the Parliament of Australia will:
(a) express its support for the Royal Commission; and
(b) urge the Federal Government to establish the Royal Commission immediately.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Dr Cass, Mr Charles, Mrs Child, Mr Cunningham, Mr Duffy, Dr Everingham, Mr Free, Mr Holding, Mr Howe, Dr Jenkins, Mr Leo McLeay, Mr Maher, Mr Mildren, Mr Morrison, Mr Mountford and Mr Wallis.
Petitions received.
Great Barrier Reef: Oil Exploration
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of citizens of the Commonwealth submits:
That off-shore oil exploration within the Great Barrier Reef Region constitutes a serious threat to the richest and most varied living system on earth.
Your petitioners request that your Honourable House will:
I. Prohibit oil exploration within the Great Barrier Reef Region,
2. Declare the entire Great Barrier Reef Region a marine park under the Federal Government's Great Barrier Marine Park Act 1975,
3. Provide the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority with the staff and resources for effective management of the Region.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Baume, Mr Fife, Mr Howard, Mr MacKellar and Mr Ian Robinson.
Petitions received.
Proposed Student Loans Scheme
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that the introduction of a comprehensive student loans scheme will:
I. Restrict Access to EducationPeople from poorer backgrounds will be deterred from studying by the prospect of incurring large debts. These people. if they enrol, will be forced to amass loan debts. Richer students do not face this handicap.
2. Undermine the Tertiary Education Assistance SchemeTEAS should cover the basic living costs of students. Currently the scheme only assists 38 per cent of full-time students and the maximum level of TEAS is a mere $49.67. Government statements indicate TEAS will be further downgraded if loans are introduced.

Petitions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3099

3. Disadvantage Women---Women students would be disadvantaged by a loans scheme as they have a lesser ability to repay loan debts, because on average women earn less and have a higher unemployment rate.
4. Disadvantage Mature-Age Students-Usually mature age students forgo income to study and already have large financial commitments. They could not return to study if it meant incurring substantial debts.
5. Distort Educational ChoicesMounting loan debts would force students into studying shorter courses or not continuing with post-graduate study.
6. Result in Massive Attrition Rates-Students will attempt to undertake extensive part-time work or overload courses to minimise their loans, increased failure rates will result forcing students to 'drop-out' rather than continuing study and increase their debts.
7. Cause Administrative and Financial DifficultiesOverseas experience shows high default rates will occur on student loans. Government money will be channelled away from TEAS to paying off banks. The Government will also bear the administrative burden of pursuing defaulters.
Therefore, the proposed student loans scheme will have disastrous effects on tertiary education, which we believe benefits the whole community, not just the individual student.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that:
The House of Representatives will reject the introduction of a student loans scheme.
And your petitioners as in duty bound shall ever humbly pray.
by Mr Baume, Mr Dawkins, Mr Keating and Mr Spender.
Petitions received.
Australian Postal Commission
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of certain undersigned citizens respectfully showeth:
I. That Australia as a sparsely populated continent requires the maintenance of a high quality postal services as a vital means of communication.
2. That the Australian postal service truly meets the needs of all Australians.
3. That any proposal that Australia Post's monopoly power to carry letters for reward be diminished will destroy the concept of the national postal service.
Your petitioners therefore pray that the powers of the postal commission to carry letters for reward should be strengthened to ensure that the national postal service is retained in the interests of all Australians.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Campbell and Mr Tuckey.

Telegram Services
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. We the undersigned citizens of the electorates of Cook, Bowman and Short land respectfully showeth:
That the plan by the Australian Telecommunications Commission to withdraw public telegram facilities and downgrade telegram standards by:--
I. planning to withdraw the 24 hour telegraph service at the Chief Telegraph Office in each capital city and in Canberra and Darwin,
2. closing country telegraph offices,
3. increasing telegram charges whilst downgrading the service.
4. withdrawing telegram operating facilities from a large number of suburban and country post offices,
5. generating serious delays in telegram delivery by reducing standards of service though centralisation of the network and reduction of operating and delivery staff,
will reduce an available means of communications in commercial enterprise, deny the opportunity for people of Australia to make contact with family and friends in times of emergency, and civil or natural disasters, particularly for the 30% of Australians who are not telephone subscribers.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will take measures to ensure the maintenance of an efficient, effective public telegram service to meet the needs of the Australian people socially, publicly, commercially and in times of emergencies and civil or natural disasters.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Dobie, Mr Jull and Mr Morris.

That the plan by the Australian Telecommunications Commission to withdraw public telegram facilities and downgrade telegram standards by:
I. planning to withdraw the 24 hour telegraph service at the Chief Telegraph Office in each capital city and in Canberra and Darwin,
2. closing country telegraph offices,
3. increasing telegram charges whilst downgrading the service,
4. withdrawing telegram operating facilities from a large number of suburban and country post offices,
5. generating serious delays in telegram delivery by reducing standards of service through centralisation of the network and reduction of operating and delivery staff,
will reduce an available means of communications in commercial enterprise, deny the opportunity for people of Australia to make contact with family and friends in times of emergency, and civil or natural disasters, particularly for the 30% of Australians who are not telephone subscribers.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will take measures to ensure the maintenance of an efficient, effective public telegram service to meet the needs of the Australian people socially, publicly, commercially and in times of emergencies and civil or natural disasters.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr MacKellar.

Petition received.
Defence Service Home Loans
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the members of the Women's Services of the 1939-45 War not at present considered eligible for Defence Service Home Loans should qualify as "Australian Soldiers-' under the Defence Service Homes Act. They volunteered for service with the Naval, Military and Air Forces, serving wherever they were sent in whatever capacity they were required and yet are refused Defence Service Home Loans.
They took the oath to resist His Majesty's enemies which meant to fight His Majesty's enemies, and although not used in actual combat in war zones, they were available for that use and had a sufficiently grave need arisen would have been used, to serve with distinction as have women of other lands.
In accordance with the Acts and Regulations of the period, the women of the Military Forces had enlisted for service anywhere, some of the Naval women to sail in any waters, others with service on warships optional, while the women of the Airforce could have been sent to Papua New Guinea or other territories under the control or care of Australia.
Ex-servicemen of the Royal Australian Navy, the Australian Imperial Force and the Royal Australian Air Force who volunteered in the 1939-45 War but were not sent to War Zones are granted home loans. Not to grant eligibility to all Ex-servicewomen of 1939-45 War is unjust and unfair.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that you favourably consider amending the Defence Service Homes Act by repealing Sub-section 4 (2A) (b) (c) (d) of the Principal Act as amended by No. 137 of 1978, and grant eligibility to all members of the Women's Royal Australian Naval Service, the Australian Women's Army Service, the Women's Auxiliary Australian Air Force, the Australian Army Medical Women's Service and the Voluntary Aid Detachment of 1939-45 War.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrBaume.

Pensions
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled.
The petition of the undersigned respectfully showeth that some single supporting parents are unfairly disadvantaged in

3104 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Petitions

having chosen to care for children full-time during the formative years, believing such care was essential to their well-being; and that women without dependent children, and who are under 50 years of age are not eligible for a continuing pension. These women have devoted their whole lives to their families and do not have skills relevant to the present employment situation. Their future prospects, with increasing age and length of time out of the workforce are a deterrent to employers. Skilled workers find it difficult to obtain employment and unskilled workers find it even more difficult. Unemployment benefit was not designed for long term usage and the low rate of payment plus the work tests over a long period of time lead to a general decline in the physical and mental health, with the result that the women are being placed on sickness benefits. Sickness benefits, however, were also designed for short term usage and is unable to provide security for these women.
Your petitioners most humbly pray that the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled should:
(i) Amend the Social Services Act to reduce the age of eligibility for a Class B Widow pension from 50 to 40 years; and
(ii) Expand existing training schemes to include women taken off Supporting Parents' Benefit.
(iii) Provide rehabilitation for women with disabilities which preclude them from the paid work force.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Retirement Benefits: Private Enterprise
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of certain citizens of Australia respectfully showeth our concern that:
\. The Income Tax Assessment Act (the Act) and its administration is inadequate in providing retirement benefits for employees in private enterprise;
2. The Commissioner of Taxation (Commissioner) denies deductions necessary to index retirement pensions;
3. Deductions for the self-employed and employed are inadequate;
4. The Commissioner has exercised his discretion improperly by negating a choice the Act allows on investment in Government Securities;
5. The Commissioner's constant alterations to guidelines and lack of communication has created great uncertainty.
6. The Commissioner is acting arbitrarily, and without proper notice in restricting trustee's investments with employers;
7. The Commissioner seeks to apply new actuarial guidelines restrospectively to July 1981.
Your Petitioners humbly pray that the House of Representatives, in Parliament assembled, take action to remedy these inadequacies and in particular we pray that action be taken:
(i) To ensure that the Commissioner exercises his discretion only with proper regard to legal principles;
(ii) To ensure that the Commissioner does not delegate to auditors part of his function of administering the Act;
(iii) To enact legislation allowing proper levels of retirement benefits to be provided;
(iv) To ensure that retirement benefits are regulated by a Department with a primarily social welfare function.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Thomson.

Petition received.
Medical Services at Remote Nursing Posts
To the Honourable the speaker and the members of the House Representatives in Parliament assembled. The undersigned citizens of the state of Western Australia respectfully petition:
That the Federal Government amend the Health Insurance Act to allow medical services at nursing posts to be eligible for hospital benefits.
In the remote and sparsely populated areas of Australia, particularly Western Australia, there are many communities that cannot on an economic or population need basis, justify the provision of a complete hospital service. In these areas nursing posts have been established to provide first aid and primary nursing care services to local residents and travellers.
Since these premises do not meet the criteria laid down in Section 24 of the Health Insurance Act, they no longer attract hospital benefits from health insurance organisations for treatment received at them.
This provision of the Act places all people in remote areas at a considerable disadvantage to people living in cities or larger centres. We ask therefore that the relevant Section of the Act be immediately amended so that recipients of aid at nursing posts are eligible for health benefits. The present circumstances, although probably caused by an administrative oversight are particularly inequitable.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Campbell.

Petition received.
Tertiary Education Funding
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned students and staff of the James Cook University of North Queensland respectfully showeth that:
I. Failure to extend eligibility under the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme (TEAS) and increase the value of TEAS allowances to cover basic living costs will

(a) continue to deter thousands of Australians from entering tertiary institutions.
(b) continue to force many of Australia's tertiary students to live in a state of dire poverty.
2. Introduction of a comprehensive student loans scheme will
(a) restrict access to education because people from poorer backgrounds will be deterred from studying by the prospect of incurring large debts.
(b) disadvantage women because on average they earn less and have a higher unemployment rate and disadvantage mature age students because they generally already have large financial commitments.
(c) undermine TEAS because government statements indicate TEAS will be further downgraded if loans are introduced.
(d) distort educational choices because students will tend to choose shorter courses and will not go on with postgraduate study.
3. Introduction of fees for post graduate and second degree students will
(a) deter many tertiary students from undertaking of continuing such courses of study for purely financial reasons.
(b) result in the limiting of Australia's capacity to cope with change because fewer people will upgrade their degrees or broaden their fields of knowledge.
(c) seriously undermine Australia's Research effort.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will:
(i) introduce legislation to extend eligibility under TEAS and increase TEAS allowances to cover basic living costs.
(ii) reject the introduction of a student loans scheme.
(iii) reject the introduction of tuition fees.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Dean.
Petition received.

JOINT COMMITTEE ON FOREIGN AFFAIRS AND DEFENCE
Report
Mr SHIPTON (Higgins)-On behalf of the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence, I present the Committee's Report on the ANZUS Alliance, incorporating two dissenting reports, together with the transcript of evidence and four submissions received by the Committee.
Ordered that the report be printed.
Mr SHIPTON-by leave-On 8 September 1981, the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence passed a resolution referring to its Sub-Committee A, the Sub-committee on the Pacific Basin, the term of reference: Australian-United States relations, The Sub-Committee was chaired by the honourable member for Hume (Mr Lusher) and conducted a wide-ranging inquiry into all the major aspects of Australian-United States relations, including foreign policy, security and defence matters as well as economic and cultural relations. I thank those individuals and government departments who spent time and effort preparing written submissions and giving oral evidence before the Sub-committee.
The focus of this report is Australia's long standing security relationship with the United States, as embodied in the ANZUS treaty. This report has already been tabled in the Senate and I will not repeat the tabling statement to the House. ANZUS is an important part of a wider Australian-United States relationship which, during the last 30 years, has become increasingly more multifaceted and remains Australia's singlemost important external relationship. Chapter 1 of the report traces the history of the ANZUS pact. In chapter 2, the significance of ANZUS in the light of past events, including the Malaysian-Indonesian confrontation in the early

1960s, is discussed, as are also current Australian and American perceptions of ANZUS and its role, including the extent to which, realistically, Australia could expect assistance under the terms of the treaty should this country ever face a serious threat to its security.
The Committee considers that the mutual security provisions of the ANZUS treaty together represent a stronger assurance of military assistance in the event of an attack on Australian territory than a commitment merely to consult. At the same time, the Committee emphasises that official United States statements of its defence thinking in relation to the Asian Pacific region, as well as United States expectations of Australia's role within the framework of ANZUS, indicate that the United States view of its role in the region precludes direct military involvement except in an extreme situation. I think this position reflects the Guam doctrine. The Committee believes that the official Australian view of ANZUS acknowledges this.
In chapter 3, the Committee discusses the benefits and costs of ANZU~ There was Press comment on the report when iL was tabled in the Senate about Australian-Indonesian relations. The report stresses that no major threat to Australia's security has emerged or is likely to emerge in the short term, On page 65, a paragraph concerning Indonesia states, in part:
Indonesia is olle local power well-placed to pose a potential threat
We should amend that sentence to read 'geographically well placed' to explain the meaning of that paragraph. That paragraph reflects the evidence presented to the Sub-Committee. It discusses a purely hypothetical potential situation that could develop, It does not refer specifically to a potential direct threat from Indonesia but to the possibility that at some stage in the future a regional conflict could develop, such as the Malaysian-Indonesian confrontation in the 1960s, which in turn could affect Australia's interests. I think that has been interpreted out of context by the media. The threat has to be read in the context of the previous report of the Committee entitled Threats to Australia's Security'.
In chapter 3, the Committee concludes that the Australian-United States relationship is, and continues to be, mutually advantageous. It also points out that the United States should take seriously Australia's views on matters of mutual concern where we may have a separate national perspective. In this regard, the Committee endorses the assessment of the Department of Foreign Affairs. The United States adopts this position. In chapter

3110 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Committee on Foreign Affairs

4, the Committee evaluated what is perhaps the most significant concern in relation to ANZUS that is, the future of the alliance. The report points out that the stability of Australia's own strategic environment is linked in no small measure to the effective involvement of United States interest and policies throughout the region. The Committee believes that differences between the United States and Australia will exist from time to time but that the management of these differences is likely to be conducted in an atmosphere which favours accommodation. That is certainly the history of these matters.
The Committee's conclusion is, therefore, that the ANZUS alliance remains both useful and necessary and that A!.Istralia should remain within its framework. The report emphasises that the security links between Australia and the United States spring frem shared values, compatible institutions and objectives and continuing popular support within the Australian community for ANZUS and the wider Western alliance. I think that ANZUS and the relations with the United States are part of one relationship but the whole United States-Australian relationship, of which ANZUS is a part, is part of a wider set of relationships, and that is Australia as a committed member of the Western alliance. ANZUS arose out of the Australian-United States relationship. The relationship did not arise out of ANZUS. I think that is an important point to make. It is a flexible and changing relationship, and that is its strength.
The reference is a continuing one. The Committee will consider other aspects of the reference. One aspect I would wish to look at in future is Australia's relations with the United States Congress. The Australian Government enjoys excellent relations, based on mutual respect, with the United States President and Administration. But the United States does not have a Westminster system and the United States Government Administration is not formed from and does not arise out of the Congress; it is separate from it. The executive and legislative power are separate in that country. In our system, of course, they are not. This has led us basically to ignore the importance of the United States Congress in AustralianUnited States relations. I believe Australia has to work to improve future relations between the Australian Parliament and the United States Congress. The Congress will be receptive to the work that needs to be done. I recently spoke to our Ambassador in Washington, Sir Robert Cotton, and I think he understands the problem well. There needs to be a greater manpower effort put in on Capitol Hill by our embassy in Washington.

A mechanism needs to be developed for a permanent and regular exchange of views between members of the Australian Senate and House of Representatives and their United States counterparts. This can only be in the interests of greater understanding between our two nations. In conclusion, I thank Dr Edvi-Illes and Mr Dan Barton of the Committee's secretariat for their assistance and hard work in the preparation of the report. I commend the report to the House.
Dr KLUGMAN (Prospect)-by leaveWithout wishing to traverse the contents of the report from the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence concerning the ANZUS Alliance, I wish to add something to it. Just as important as the actual terms of the ANZUS treaty are the potential psychological factors of any changes which would have an effect, to my mind, on world peace which, currently, to a large extent is due to the balance of power between the two super powers. The greatest threat to peace would be a belief by the Soviet Union that it could win a quick war. Similarly, in the late 1930s-193839-Hitler thought we would not stand by Poland following the Munich agreement, the invasion of Czechoslovakia and the Hitler-Stalin pact. A decision by Australia to cancel ANZUS would be seen by the Soviet Union as a signal that its drive to the south, whether from Vietnam or through Iran, would not be opposed. In fact, such action by Australia and New Zealand could well persuade the United States population to become more isolationist and to allow the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics to speed up its imperialist drive which has been going on ever since the last war. If this happened it would ultimately lead to war. It is, therefore, important that our signals to the Soviet Union are clear. This also applies to other allies of the United States such as the North Atlantic Treaty Organisation countries.
In the general debate on matters of neutralism, unilateral disarmament and alliances with the United States, I shall read into Hansard part of a speech made recently by James Callaghan, the last Labour Prime Minister of the United Kingdom. According to the London Times, on 18 November 1982, in an address to the Cardiff Fabian Society, he said:
a Labour Government should retain Polaris, allow cruise, keep the American bases, and concentrate its attention on multilateral disarmament talks, comprehensive test-ban negotiations and the pursuit of non-proliferation.
The article continued:
'Why should we unilaterally give away any bargaining counters whilst negotiations are in progress?' Mr Callaghan said. 'To do so would be to hand to the Soviet Union something valuable with no prospect of anything in exchange. The Soviet Union would say "thank you" and pocket it.'

Committee on Foreign Affairs

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3111

According to the article, Mr Callaghan said that some say:
Abandon our nuclear weapons. close down our nuclear bases in this country, and send the Americans back home. If we do. goes the argument. we shall be safe from attack and. as a bonus, other countries will be so impressed that they will follow our lead.
The article goes on to state:
Such a policy was attractive. but wrong. and Mr Callaghan questioned whether Britain could retain its self-respect, given such a policy, if it continued to rely on Nato nuclear protection.
Mr Callaghan is reported as asking:
How could we escape attack in any future war, by any weapons the enemy might think necessary to reduce us?
He is quoted as saying:
Unilateral disarmament does not offer a way out. The only real safety for British people is through collective security and multilateral agreement to disarm the nuclear and conventional arsenals of both sides.
The article goes on to state:
He also said that any unilateral action, which would be certain to damage Anglo-American friendship, might eventually lead to the 'unravelling of NATO on the mainland of Europe'. If America was provoked into isolationism, the alliance would be destabilised, and it would even be possible that West Germany might be forced to seek closer links with East Germany.
He said that everyone shared the unilateralists' horror of nuclear war. But he concluded: 'The task for a Labour Government is to secure a series of comprehensive multilateral agreements to achieve our ends. That way lies safety for the British people, and for the world.'
Similarly, if we send the wrong signals to the world by changing our attitude to ANZUS--I am not suggesting that any significant proportion of the Australian population would want to do that-some of the weaker countries in our area might be prepared to do deals with the Soviet Union or the Soviet Union's satellite of Vietnam. I think it is terribly important to consider that matter of signals and the matter of making it quite clear that we stand by ANZUS. It is in our interests. It is in the interests of the United States. I think it is much more in our interests than it is in the interests of the United States. Most importantly, it is in the interests of the free world and people who want to continue to be able to make decisions as to their lifestyle freely, in the sort of democratic pattern which we are used to and which obviously is not applicable to the Soviet Union and its satellites. Therefore, it is terribly important for us to continue to emphasise our commitment to the ANZUS alliance.
Debate interrupted.
