Mr MORRISON (St George)-by leave--I must confess that I was away during the consideration by the Joint Parliamentary Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence of the report on the ANZUS Alliance. First of all I want to associate IT'yself with two items of the dissenting report. I agree with one of the statements in the introduction to the report which reads as follows:
Australia's basic objective is to fulfil its commitment to the Alliance, with due regard to our national interest and the maintenance of our national sovereignty.
I note the presence in the chamber of the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock) who is again a member of the Ministry and who will recall some of the observations I have made over the years about the North West Cape agreement and the abject derogation of Australian sovereignty in that agreement, particularly in the exchange of letters between the United States Ambassador, Mr Battle, and the then Minister for Foreign Affairs, Sir Garfield Barwick. So Australia has not always maintained this national interest and notion of sovereignty in our associations with the United States. We all remember the very demeaning slogan that was mouthed by one of our Prime Ministers about being 'All the way with LBJ'.
What I want to raise tonight is what I perceive as the tone of the report which gives an unreal expectation about the allian-ce. I believe that from the viewpoint of both sides of Parliament and of Australia this is dangerous. I quote United States Under-Secretary of State, Mr Averell Harriman who, in 1963, when the Australian Government of the day was discussing with the United States Administration the question of confrontation and what would be the impact of loss of life or confrontation between the Indonesians and Australians, said:
Defence agreements are not precise as to military movements or diplomatic activity. There is always a grey area.
I also recall the following words of President Nixon:
Our interests must shape our commitments rather than the other way around.
I point out to the House that we should not delude ourselves that ANZUS is a watertight commitment by the United States. I think it is only in the field of foreign affairs that we recognise that the United States will not assume responsibilities it

3112 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Committee on Foreign Affairs

does not see as being in its interests. The report details periods when Australia was concerned about the United States commitment. I need only to mention the confrontation involved in the Indonesians' claim to West Irian. I think we should look at those sections of the report very carefully, and should heed the experiences we went through in that period. Successive United States administrations have made it clear that possible commitments under ANZUS can arise only in the context of the central power balance. This follows from the Guam statements of President Nixon that United States allies are expected to handle in their own way regional disputes that are not associated with the central power balance. I want to raise a particular reservation in the report--- unfortunately I was not in the discussions of the Foreign Affairs and Defence Committee to raise this-which appears on page 67. The report states:
The Committee does not see any distortion of Australia's defence policy resulting from the ANZUS Alliance.
I think that if one examines Australia's role over the years one finds that Australia has been a junior partner and has supplied military aid to both the United Kingdom-Great Britain in those days--and the United States, where our force structure was in fact shaped by the alliance. We slotted in our units of the Army, Navy and Air Force to a larger command. They were reponsible for the command and also the logistics of our forces. I believe that if we are dealing with Australia as having a self-reliant defence force we have to make decisions that are outside any alliance that we may have. I think this is brought home to us in the sense that the United States expects us to do that. I fear that over the last couple of years there have been indications that the Government has moved away from this notion of self-reliance and is again taking the part of a junior partner in the alliance operations.
I am not happy about the sections of the report dealing with the Indian Ocean. We should keep in mind our interests in relation to the north-west Indian Ocean are essentially different, and can be different, from those of other countries because we are Australians. That is an area which is, in fact, closer to Europe than to Australia. I believe that in determining our own defence force structure we cannot commit ourselves outside our competence to carry out commitments. Our first functionI believe this is a function that the United States expects of us--is to be able to look after our own affairs in our area. I associate with these remarks the attitude of the present Secretary of the Department of Defence who has

pointed out that we cannot undertake commitments well outside our direct area of influence. I think this is important to consider in terms of our commitment to ANZUS.
CUSTOMS TARIFF (ANTI-DUMPING) AMENDMENT BILL (No.2) 1982
Bill presented by Mr Peacock, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr PEACOCK (Kooyong-Minister for Industry and Commerce) (8.7)--1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill amends the Customs Tariff (AntiDumping) Act 1975 to repeal section 14 of that Act. Section 14 of the Act imposes an obligation on the Minister not to take action which is inconsistent with the obligations of Australia under international agreements relating to trade. The repeal of this section is principally designed to remove for practical purposes the grounds for any application to the Australian courts raising the issue of Australia's non-compliance with international agreements, including the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade codes and any bilaterial agreements, relating to anti-dumping and countervailing duty action.
The provision of the Customs Tariff (AntiDumping) Act enable the Government to take anti-dumping and contervailing duty action against imports. Both actions are designed to offset export prices which are not established on a fair commercial basis. Australia is a signatory to the GATT, and the related codes which establish rules of conduct for anti-dumping and countervailing actions. The codes, which elaborate on the relevant provisions of the GATT, were part of the outcome of the multilateral Trade Negotiations, Tokyo Round, concluded in 1979. Australia acceded to the Subsidies and Countervailing Duties Code on 21 September 1982 and to the Anti-Dumping Code on 21 October 1982.
The codes require that signatories must take all necessary steps to ensure that their domestic legislation conforms with the provisions of the codes. The Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act was amended in 1981 so that it conforms with the codes. Anti-dumping or countervailing duties cannot be imposed unless it is determined by the relevant authority in the importing country that the goods in question have been dumped-that is, exported at less than normal prices in the country of export or subsidised and as a result an industry in the importing country has been injured or threatened with injury. The codes also limit the extent of countervailing or anti-dumping action to

Customs Tariff Amendment Bill

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3113

an amount equal to the amount of subsidy or of dumping or the extent to which injury is occurring, whichever is the less.
Where a preliminary affirmative finding of dumping or subsidisation is made by the Department of Industry and Commerce as the relevant authority and there is evidence of material injury or threat of material injury to the domestic industry, the GATT anti-dumping and subsidies and
countervailing duties codes permit the taking of provisional action in the form of securities which in Australia are taken under the provisions of section 42 of the Customs Act. The Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act has facilitated proceedings for injunctive relief in these matters before the Federal Court of Australia. The related Customs Securities (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Bill which I will introduce shortly contains a provision to remove from the scope of that Act decisions to take securities under the Customs Act as provisional anti-dumping and countervailing measures. That provision will supersede the regulations with similar effect introduced on 24 November 1982.
In the injunction proceedings before the Court exporters of goods to Australia have used the provisions of section 14 of the Customs Tariff (AntiDumping Act) as the basis for arguing that the method by which the Department proceeds to the decision to require provisional anti-dumping and provisional countervailing measures is contrary to Australia's international obligations. Whilst the Department meets the requirements of the codes in principle these arguments have nevertheless enabled interim injunctions to be obtained. As a consequence, the Department has been prevented from taking provisional securities and this has operated to the detriment of Australian industry.
The world wide economic recession has led to a
dramatic increase in the number of dumping and countervailing complaints in Australia. The Government is therefore concerned that there should be no impediment to taking quick and effective action to combat unfair trading practices which operate to the detriment of Australian industry. The repeal of section 14 of the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act, as proposed by this Bill, will not affect Australia's adherence to the relevant international obligations. The Government's view is that issues concerning Australia's obligations under the international agreements relating to anti-dumping and countervailing duty action are more appropriately considered within the context of the dispute settlement provisions of the particular agreements rather than in the Australian courts. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Hurford) adjourned.

CUSTOMS SECURITIES (ANTI-DUMPING) AMENDMENT BILL
1982
Bill presented by Mr Peacock, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr PEACOCK (Kooyong-Minister for Industry and Commerce) (8.13) - I move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is twofold. It amends the Customs Act to place beyond doubt that securities may be validly taken under section 42 of that Act for the purposes of the Customs Tariff (AntiDumping) Act. It also amends the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act to remove from the scope of that Act decisions made under section 42 of the Customs Act to require and take securities in respect of duty that may be payable under the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act. The Customs Tariff (Anti-dumping) Act is the legislation which enables the Government to take anti-dumping and countervailing duty action against imports. This action is designed to offset export prices which are not established on a fair commercial basis.
Where a preliminary affirmative finding of dumping or subsidisation is made by the Department of Industry and Commerce and there is evidence of material injury or threat of material injury to the domestic industry, provisional action in the form of securities may be taken under the provisions of section 42 of the Customs Act. The securities are held in accordance with the time limits specified in the Customs Act until, after inquiry, a final decision is made as to whether dumping or countervailing duties are warranted. If the final decision is in the affirmative the securities held are adjusted to the amount of the duty to be imposed. If the decision is that dumping or countervailing duty is not warranted the securities are returned to depositors.
As a consequence of litigation in the Federal Court the Department has been restrained from taking provisional anti-dumping and countervailing securities in respect of the importation of certain goods into Australia. It has been argued in these proceedings that the terms of section 42 of the Customs Act do not authorise the taking of security for the purpose of protecting Australian industry. The amendments in Clause 4 of the Bill provide an express link between section 42 of the Customs Act and the Customs Tariff (AntiDumping) Act. This is proposed to remove any doubt as to the validity of requiring securities

3114 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Student Assistance Bill

under the Customs Act for the purposes of the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act.
In my second reading speech relating to the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Bill (No.2) 1982, I explained how exporters of goods to Australia have been able to use the existing law to prevent the Department from taking provisional securities. The provisions of clause 6 of the Bill are designed to minimise the opportunities to prevent the taking of such securities. Under this clause decisions relating to the taking of the securities will be exempted from the scope of the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act.
Regulations with similar effect to this clause became operative on 24 November 1982. The provisions in this bill will, however, supersede those regulations and it is the Government's intention that the regulations will be repealed after this Bill receives royal assent. The Government believes that the removal from judicial review of decisions on provisional securities is essential if anti-dumping and countervailing measures are to be efffective in combating unfair international trading practices which operate to the detriment of Australian industry. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Hurford) adjourned.
STUDENT ASSISTANCE (LOANS GUARANTEE AND SUBSIDY) BILL 1982
Bill received from the Senate, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Aviation) (8.17)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to provide the legislative basis for the implementation of the Tertiary Student Loans Scheme which will be introduced early in 1983. The Bill provides for the Commonwealth to subsidise the interest charged by banks on approved loans and for the Commonwealth to give guarantees to banks that it will repay outstanding debts on approved loans within prescribed limits. The Bill also covers the eligibility conditions applying to loans.
The Tertiary Student Loans Scheme will enable full time tertiary students who are Australian citizens or permanent residents to borrow between $500 and $1,000, per annum from banks to help pay for their education and living expenses while studying. Students will be able to borrow up to $8,000 over the total period of their study.
Many full time students will be eligible for approved loans by virtue of their entitlement to

benefits under various Commonwealth student assistance schemes. These schemes are: The Commonwealth Postgraduate Awards Scheme, the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme known as TEAS, the Adult Secondary Education Assistance Scheme and the Aboriginal Study Grants Scheme. The Tertiary Student Loans Scheme will provide an additional source of support for eligible students receiving benefits under these schemes. Other full time students will qualify for loans providing they satisfy a simple means test, the details of which have already been announced in the Budget context. These details will be prescribed in regulations.
The Government has demonstrated its strong continuing support for student assistance schemes by its decision to increase substantially allowance levels and relax means tests for 1983. The loans scheme will provide another option for students. The interest rate to apply to loans will be the maximum rate permissible for advances by banks of less than $1 OO,OOO-currently 14.5 per cent per annum--reduced by 5 percentage points by a Commonwealth subsidy. The Commonwealth will provide banks with guarantees amounting to $50m per annum on approved loans but the banks will be expected to take all reasonable steps to obtain repayments from defaulting borrowers before the Commonwealth honours guarantees.
The scheme will be administered by the Commonwealth Department of Education in conjunction with the banks and tertiary institutions, and the detailed terms and conditions for approved loans will be prescribed in regulations. Funds to meet payments by the Commonwealth of interest subsidies and the repayments of any defaulted loans will be provided for through the general appropriation legislation. For this financial year the Appropriation Bill (No.2) 1982-83 provides $150,000 for these purposes. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Scholes) adjourned.
STATES GRANTS (TERTIARY EDUCATION ASSISTANCE) AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Bill returned from the Senate with amendments.
Ordered that the ameadments be taken into consideration in Committee of the Whole House forthwith.
In Committee
Consideration of Senate's amendments. Senate's amendments-
No. I - Page 14, clause 26, proposed sub-section 39A (I), lines 15 and 16, leave out " whether or not specified in Schedule 18,'.

States Grants Amendment Bill

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3115

No.2-Page 14, clause 26, proposed sub-section 39A (I), lines 18 and 19, leave out 'a college of advanced education specified in Schedule 18 and situated in the same State as the first-mentioned institution', insert 'a university or a college of advanced education situated in the same State as the first mentioned institution, and at least one of those institutions is specified in Schedule 18'.
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Aviation) (8.20)-1 move:
That the amendments be agreed to.
Honourable members will recall that on the advice of the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission the Government has required certain colleges of advanced education to enter into amalgamation arrangements aimed at bringing about larger consolidated institutions. Such institutions are better able to handle problems associated with change and can offer students a wider range of educational choice and a higher quality of educational program. Some economies of scale also exist, although such considerations were not the primary reasons behind the Government's decision to require amalgamations. Twenty-six of the 30 institutions concerned have now entered into acceptable arrangements. The Government believes that these amalgamations will be successful and that higher education will be strengthened in the long term.
There are four colleges yet to finalise acceptable arrangements. These are the Armidale, Newcastle and Milperra colleges of advanced education and the Hawthorn Institute of Education. In respect of Armidale and Newcastle it is expected that the New South Wales Government will legislate for the amalgamation of each college of advanced education with the relevant local university following the passage of the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Amendment Bill through the Commonwealth Parliament. The nature of the final amalgamation arrangements has yet to be determined in respect of Milperra and Hawthorn.
The Government has recognised that for maximum effectiveness the precise arrangements for amalgamations should reflect local circumstances and are, therefore, best determined at the local level. The States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Amendment Bill requires the Minister for Education to provide funds for the operation of institutions in 1983 only when he is satisfied that suitable amalgamation arrangements are in hand. Essentially, the amendment which I have moved enables a broader intepretation to be taken as to what constitutes a satisfactory amalgamation. In this respect it seeks to take account of the latest thinking of the State governments concerned as to how the Commonwealth's requirements may be met for the two remaining

institutions. In summary, the amendment recognises that a suitable amalgamation might be affected by an institution assuming certain of the teaching responsibilities of another institution. Such an arrangement might well meet the Commonwealth's requirements in a way which had not been anticipated earlier in the process. The amendment, therefore, ensures that education rationale remains the central concern of the amalgamation exercise.
As foreshadowed by the Minister for Education, Senator Peter Baume, during the debate in another place on the Bill, the Government's decision on a wage pause for the next 12 months will, of course, contain any increases in the cost of running tertiary institutions. Under the Government's prospective cost adjustment arrangements, the sums of money specified in the Schedules to the Bill include an allowance for wage and salary increases in 1983 in accordance with earlier decisions. Following the Premiers Conference yesterday, there will now be savings flowing from the wages pause and the Government will, therefore, restrict the rate of advances under the Bill as from the beginning of 1983 to reflect this. A similar course of action will be taken in relation to grants for both government and non-government schools. These reductions in the rate of advances will be formalised by amending legislation during 1983. In the light of the outcome of yesterday's Premiers Conference the Government is giving consideration to spending the consequent savings available to the Commonwealth, including savings with respect to this matter, on worthwhile programs which employ labour. I commend the amendments to the House.
Amendments agreed to.
Resolution reported; report adopted.

(a) That the following matters be referred to the Joint Committee on the Broadcasting of Parliamentary Proceedings for inquiry and report: (i) the televising of the proceedings of the Houses of the Parliament and their committees in the new Parliament House: and (ii) the continuous and simultaneous radio broadcasting of both Houses of the Parliament.
(b) That the Committee, for any purposes related to this inquiry, have power to send for persons, papers and records.
(2) That a message be sent to the Senate acquainting it of this resolution.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (8.29)--1 do not intend to hold up the House for very long. The matter before the House is for a reference to the Joint Committee on the Broadcasting of Parliamentary Proceedings of which consideration will take place. I believe that before members of this House or the other House commit themselves to what seems to be a good idea of televising parliamentary proceedings they should look at what the results of televising the Parliament will be, if it is on anything like the basis on which broadcasting takes place. There are areas which are televised and where parliamentary proceedings are recorded and made available to the Press for its use. Before too many members run away with the attractiveness of television in the Parliament I suggest that the three or four honourable members who are in the Parliament at the moment have a look around and see the sort of camera picture that could be picked up here.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
CONSTITUTION ALTERATION (FIXED TERM PARLIAMENTS) BILL 1982
Bill received from the Senate, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (8.30)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This is a Bill for an Act to alter the Constitution in relation to the duration of the House of Representatives, the holding of elections for the Senate and the House of Representatives and the appointment and termination of the appointment of Ministers of State. The Bill has already passed the Senate. It was the subject of an amendment in that chamber. It is a Bill that was proposed by Senator Gareth Evans, a member of the Australian Labor Party. I say at the outset that of

course this is a Bill that has been proposed in the knowledge that we are in opposition. However, we hope that the Government of the day will agree to some constitutional amendments. Even though we are in opposition we hope that the proposal the Government will agree to in the Bill, a fixed term for the House of Representatives. It is a reasonable and rational proposal to be submitted to the people for approval, as it must be if there is to be a change.
When Labor becomes the Government of Australia it will offer a much wider package of constitutional reform. Our party for some time has been urging that the people of Australia have a chance to improve the Constitution. We urge the Government to bring it out of the horse and buggy days of the last century into the modern era in which we have to face national and international problems that were in no way envisaged at the time the Constitution was drafted--the I890s. We hope to get not only the politicians involved in conventions and discussions as to the best way to improve our Constitution but also the rest of the people of Australia. They should be involved and asked as to how they see their charter being brought up to date.
For example, a new Constitution ought to have a Bill of Rights in it. In any rational democracy the people are entitled to have rights as against a government. Those rights ought to be enshrined in the Constitution. Even though this is a first step it is a very excellent step. The Senate has approved of that idea in the form of the Bill that we are now to deal with. I was reminded by Senator Gareth Evans that the Government in the Senate opposed the Bill at all its stages. Nevertheless in the Senate, in the form in which it has now been received by us, it received very substantial crossparty support. The Labor Party was joined at the third reading not only by the Australian Democrats and Senator Harradine but also by six members of the Liberal Party. Also the Bill at all its stages had strong and consistent support in newspapers and from respected people interested in constitutional reform.
The Bill has no political content in the sense that it might be of advantage or disadvantage to one side or the other. The real thrust of this legislation is to give some stability to the House of Representatives. Section 28 of the Constitution presently provides that the House of Representatives, the people's House, is entitled to remain for a period of three years. Of course, that has not happened. There has been the inevitability of having to deal with a hostile Senate. Prime Ministers, because of their own personal whims, might

Constitution Alteration Bill

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3117

wish to cut short the normal life cycle of the House of Representatives.
It is a matter of record that since the Second World War the average life of the House of Representatives has not been three years but two years four months. As one of those members left over from the Whitlam era I remind the House that when we were elected to Government in 1972 we faced continual threats of having to go back to the people simply because we had a hostile Senate. In fact we faced elections in 1974 and 1975. As Government members knew~we did not~the House of Representatives was dissolved early in 1977.The dissolution in 1977 was obviously related to the fact that the Opposition at the time was going to change its leadership or something of that nature, and it created dreadful problems for our society. It virtually destroyed the reputation of one man who held the office of Governor-General and, of course, it also affected the person who held the position of Chief Justice. Incredible damage was done not to those people as such but to the very society which we are trying to suggest to the people is strong, efficient and fair. We are very mindful of the fact that if the people elect a government according to the Constitution~for three years~that government ought to remain in office for three years and ought not to be removed by some political force, which is the normal political force of an Opposition.
The Senate was originally conceived as a House that would not be in any way aligned, motivated or affected by party politics. That was an unreal and idealistic view. To their credit, everybody has a political position. Senators are now part and parcel of the professional political arena of the Parliament. They come to party meetings, they take part in party discussions and they abide by party deliberations. It is nonsensical to think that an Opposition in the House of Representatives would not exercise its political muscle and power in the Senate when it had the numbers there because the method of election and the timing of Senate elections are different. This Bill is proposed in the savage knowledge that what happened in 1974-75 should never be repeated. But it can be repeated, because the Bill deals only with a fixed term for the House of Representatives. The term envisaged by the Bill is three years and elections would be held on a fixed date, namely, the third Saturday in November. The life of a parliament would expire in the previous October. There have to be exceptions to that. So naturally and properly the Bill provides that if a government no longer has the confidence of the House it cannot remain in office, which no government would do.

The Bill contains a provision for an election if a government loses the confidence of the House.
We cannot avoid the question of a double dissolution, nor should we. Section 57 of the Constitution provides that if there is a disagreement between the Houses, and the Government is adamant in that disagreement, there shall be an election to decide the issue. Under this Bill that can still happen. Of course, if under this proposed legislation that should happen, the Government and some senators would hold office for only the balance of the term available. In other words, if a government were elected for three years and after two years there were a double dissolution, under this proposal the terms of the Government and of the senators first elected would expire after 12 months. The Bill contains a provision for that acceleration of events and also provides a sanction whereby the Senate would not be so anxious to be difficult if it had to face up to the fact that its terms would not be for six years but would be the life of two terms of the House of Representatives.
A memorandum has been circulated setting out what Senator Evans thinks the Government should address its mind to. He makes the point that the problems are related to the utter lack of stability and predictability in the Australian electoral cycle. We knew that. As a matter of record, we were elected in December 1972 and were under threat of not getting Supply in 1973. That was the year of our very first Budget. The supplementary Supply Bills of 1974 were also under threat, so we had to have a double dissolution. The Supply of 1974 was also under threat and, of course, the Supply of 1975 was not even conceded. We recall the tremendous tension that prevailed.
I think you would almost smile now, Mr Deputy Speaker, if you looked at the proposals of the then Senate which said that it could not give that Government life in political terms because of the rising unemployment at that time. When we consider that more than 560,000 people are unemployed now, we wonder whether the Senate might send the present Government out to the people on that basis. That was the pretext that was used on the previous occasions. Another was the dreadful inflation and yet another the suggestion that the deficit was getting a little out of hand. We are now looking at a deficit of $4 billion. We understand that government has to face the people but it should not be at the whim and the cavalier suggestion of a Senate with resolutions which have no bona fides at all. We still smile at those senators and former senators who said that they were judging us on our merits. We know that they participated in the party room discussion at which

3118 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Constitution Alteration Bill

they were told: 'See whether you can get rid of that Government as soon as possible'. We know that they voted in accordance with political dictates. The concept of this legislation is to try to overcome this sort of cynicism.
I make another point. Why was an early election held in 1977? It was simply because the Australian Labor Party was likely to change its leader and the present Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) thought that it was so much easier to beat the leader we had in 1975 if he was stillrhe leader in 1977. We should have gone through until 1978. But that was the real rationale. Speeches that were made in Queensland at the time readily identified that rationale. It has brought about a cynicism as to what we are all about in this place. The people are sick and tired of the number of elections. They just cannot understand how we can be making any progress by continually having elections, particularly in the present economic climate. They are looking for some national concepts. This is where Australia is so bereft at present. It has no national spirit; it is governed, or over-governed, by at least eight governments at present. The people are out of their minds and fed up to the neck because what we would call 'some administration' is costing the earth. They say that they are dreadfully over-governed, and they are right. We can never get them from this position to that of looking at a new constitution, which I think they would fairly draft on a non-political basis, with the following concept: 'We will elect the governments and they will remain for the period we determine'. As a matter of interest, [ think public opinion would now be that a fouryear term is better than a three-year term.
We want to have a Senate. Everybody agrees that we should have a Senate as a House of legitimate review, not a House of confrontation or a House of rejection, which it has become. If we adopted a new method of ele;;ting members of the Senate, namely, on a first-past-the-post basis rather than on the basis of proportional representation, we might get more political stability in this area; but we certainly would nor get the genuine House of review or the genuine contribution that minority groups are entitled to have, which proportionai representation guarantees. We need to wake up and be alert to the fact that we have a precious ele'11ent called democracy. We can lose it if we go through the political mechanisms of the sheer, abrupt use of power or ambu~h in a Senate which is anxious always to send the House of Representatives back to the people but which would not dare to go to the people itself. That is still the unfortunate position in which we find ourselves. An effort was made in the original draft of this

Bill to provide that, in terms of Supply, section 53 would not give the Senate the power which it now has to reject or not grant supply. We should bear in mind that in that context the word 'reject' does not appear in section 53. It appeared in the original draft of the Constitution and was deleted because the forefathers, in their wisdom, said that we cannot have two financial Houses, and that finance --in other words, tax--has to come from the House of the people. That was a legitimate and valid proposition. But because it did not say that the Senate shall not reject Supply, which it could have done because it assumed that by removal of the word 'reject' the Senate would not threaten Supply, during the period 1972-75 we had to face the fact that Supply was threatened and in fact not granted.
Whilst this Bill set out to say that the Senate would not do that, the Senate would not let that provision remain in the Bill, and it has been deleted from it. So we still run the dreadful gauntlet of a hostile Senate not giving a government supply and may face continual elections, a situation which this Bill cannot correct. This matter has been discussed by various constitutional conventions. The great problem with those conventions, as I said, is that they are always attended by members of parliament, both Federal and State. Naturally, those people will fight to hold their present preserve. The people of Australia do not have any input at all. It was interesting at the Perth convention to note that we in the Labor Party were asking why we need to give the Senate the power to reject Supply. Democracy is destroyed if the Senate can continually reject Supply for a government which basically needs Supply every six months. The unfortunate situation could develop in which a government could continually be returned to the people and re-elected and the Senate may not do anything about it. That would be a nonsensical situation.
We proposed suggesting to the people of Australia, on a non-party basis, that the Senate not have those ridiculous powers. Sir Charles Court put a somewhat doubtful amendment. He said that if that were to happen there ought to be a double dissolution and that it would be a sanction on the Senate. Of course, a re-election might still give the same result because the method of election is different. Proportional representation allows for minorities in the Senate. It does not allow for that anywhere else. We could well find that the Senate would still be just as difficult and the nation would become ungovernable. If we were not to give finance to a government in the normal way it would not be able to govern.

Constitution Alteration Bill

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3119

Section 53 of the Constitution~I am speaking from memory~refers to the ordinary annual services of government. It does not refer to anything special; just annual services. It refers to the continuity of government. Why should a Senate be allowed to adopt the past practice of using all sorts of mechanisms to suggest that because the economy was not going right, because inflation and unemployment were too high, a government should not get the ordinary annual appropriation? That would mean that public servants could not be paid, no money could go to the States, every school would have to close, and hospitals could not function. In other words we would have anarchv. But that was the rationale at the time. The C~urt amendment was no solution because no State government would be able to function if this sort of position were deemed to be the way to play the political game in Australia. If upper houses, wherever they exist, have the power to refuse Supply we will get these problems.
This nation is bedevilled enough at present. I suggest, purely on the basis of sheer intelligence in support of the principle of democracy, that we ought to have some clear indication of that what we are about in this nation is protecting people's rights. When people exercise their right to elect a government we should not destroy it on the basis that perhaps they have changed their minds in the interim. It is difficult for a government always to maintain popularity during its term if it has to introduce taxation measures. The political climate can change within three years. If the Senate is always to seize on the fact that the gallup polls show that this is the right time to remove the government we will find that it will never get Supply. The government might be anxious to impose some additional tax which the Senate, out of sheer devilment, decides not to give it at that time and wants to see whether that government can win an election now because it might not be so popular.
All those judgments by the Senate would be political judgments. They would not be based on fairness, equity or democracy but on the continual struggle for power and supremacy. The fixed term parliament proposals give us some indirect solution. It is not the best solution. Senator Evans admits that. He said that the legislation is the second best solution. It does not involve talking about the past, the errors or the mistakes. It is trying to offer some reasonable alternative. We still face the overwhelming problem that the power of Supply can be destructively exercised. But the

real issue is that the basic constitutional prohibition of premature elections would make the blocking of Supply perhaps pointless and politically counter-productive. To the extent that the basic prohibition could be avoided in a particular case~for example, by the existence at the time of the blocking of some deadlocked measure for which the double dissolution trigger was already cockedthe argument is that the rule that an incoming government could only serve out the term of the predecessor would operate as an overwhelming, practical disincentive to the Senate pursuing the matter. I think that is right.
The Bill provides for a basic fixed term and it amends section 28 of the Constitution. There are further amendments to indicate clearly that the Senate terms are equivalent to two House of Representatives terms. The conditions are created for Senate elections to be always held at the same time as House of Representatives elections. There are further amendments to the Constitution to ensure that the writs for House and Senate elections are issued at the proper time. I make the point that one could have the House of Representatives passing a no confidence motion. If that happened there would have to be an election for the House of Representatives but it would be only for the balance of the term.
One can think of situations which perhaps show gaps in this Bill on which we need not dwell because we are basically talking about the positive aspects. One could find extraordinary situations in which one might not have a Prime Minister or the Prime Minister resigns and, therefore, no advice could be given to a Governor-General. But those possibilities are so remote in practical terms that we had better not waste too much time on them. The point we are anxious to make is that there will always be opportunities to remove bad governments. The best way to do it is through the people in the normal course and not by a Senate trying to judge its views. Certainly, if there were a motion of no confidence in the House it would not mean that one has to go to an election if, in fact, one could reform a government within the context of the occupants of the House of Representatives. If they could get a vote of confidence there would be no need for the unnecessary election. I think that all Senator Evans is saying in these expressions is that he has a chance to indicate clearly that we might go along the track to solving the difficulties we have experienced.
As he has said, this Bill is a serious minded and realistic attempt to grapple with the questions of fundamental constitutional importance and of

3120 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Constitution Alteration Bill

crucial importance to the future of our parliamentary system. It is a genuine attempt to find a possible middle-of-the-road solution, as Senator Evans calls it, to the major problems which have been weighing heavily on both sides. We want to ensure, in the interests of the Australian people, that democracy really means something and that it can work. We should leave it to the people to judge the question of when governments should be changed and not to the Senate. The subject matter, as Senator Evans says, is such that the temptation to score in political terms is obvious. But it is a temptation which has been resisted in the course of this debate. The only way constitutional reform on this or any other issue can be achieved is through consensus. I think that is clear.
If we can get the Australian nation to reach consensus-which I think can be done---on a new Constitution we will make enormous progress in this country. People would identify with the concept of one nation, one Australia. They do this in times of crisis. Unfortunately, the crises are usually wars. A better Australian spirit was never seen than when Australia was under attack and its very survival was at stake. Nobody worried what State he belonged to or where he was born; it was a question of Australia surviving. Our present world has shrunk but is very competitive. Distances are no guarantee of safety. There is competition for survival in other nations and in our own. If we are not able to make a go of democracy and a success of this nation, if we are too divided on the major issues, other nations will look at that as weakness. They are entitled to do so. Many of them, of course, do not practice democracy and are not likely to do so. We do not want democracy to be second best simply because words can be manipulated.
If we look at the intentions of the people who drafted the Constitution we find that they were anxious to create a strong and fair Australia in which all people had equal rights. A proper constitution is desperately needed. Knowing the conservative nature of the Government, we think that this is something that the Government might be prepared to offer the people. The only way it can do that is to pass the Bill we are now introducing. The Opposition makes the point that an opposition certainly ought to be able to bring down a government, but should only be able to do it in the House of Representatives. That is clearly the position. I make the point again that we are no longer urging the abolition of the Senate. We think it has a valuable role to play; one of an intelligent

contribution towards legislation, not one of playing the gut politics of going for the jugular vein to prevent the lifeblood of Supply.
Sir James Killen-I should know but I don't; do I take it that your Party no longer advocates the abolition of the Senate?
Mr LIONEL BOWEN~It does not. It has altered its platform.
Sir James Killen-Senator Evans, when he arrived, in his maiden speech--
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-I did not know Senator Evans was in the chamber. I am delighted to see him. I hope he does not mind my saying this, but I think our party policy also suggests that we should not have any Ministers in the Senate. The Leader of the House would not object to that. With the Evans Bill, as we call it-- I think it is entitled to have that profile because he deserves a lot of praise for the effort that has gone into it and enormous praise for even getting it through the Senate, which shows that there is some hope at times-the issue there is
Mr Ruddock-A free vote?
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-Yes, a free vote, if that will help at all. I mentioned other restraints. I will not weary the House with them. The Bill is positive in what it offers. It offers, as Senator Evans said, not the best but the second best solution. It is a good solution if in the use of the second best we can get the best, by what would be called custom or convention, in that the Senate will not continue to urge elections as it wants as opposed to what the people need. There could be no better time to discuss this matter than in the present world economic climate. It is not very healthy. The world is re-arming and is very anxious about energy resources. It is certainly going to need them. If we talk to the scientists of the world we find the world will be very short indeed of a number of necessary resources which Australia has. If Australia does not make a proper contribution to the stability of democracy it is endangering its very lifeblood. It is also perhaps guaranteeing that somebody else will take a very keen interest.
The political representation Australians have is not the best. It is not A-grade material; it is very much lower than C-grade. When one looks at the politics that have been played in the past one sees

that it has been in what one would call the kindergarten or infantile stage. It has offered nothing by way of a solution to what we are about. The Evans Bill has done something. It has altered the people of Australia-those who think and want to discuss on a non-party calm basis what ought to be done-to the fact that we need something done about this Constitution. A small and a very valuable step is to guarantee that the House of Representatives can have a fixed term. I commend the Bill to the House.

BROADCASTING AND TELEVISION AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 14 October, on motion by Mr N. A. Brown:

Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (9.2)-1 support the

That the Bill be now read a second time.

motion for new works at Queenscliff. In the last, Mr N. A. BROWN (Diamond Valley-

couple of weeks the fort at Queensliff has been Minister for Communications)-Mr Deputy

subject to centenary celebations. In recent times a Speaker, may I have your indulgence to suggest

3122 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Broadcasting and TV Amendment Bill

that the House have a general debate covering this Bill, the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Bill 1982 and the Television Stations Licence Fees Amendment Bill 1982, as they are related measures. Separate questions will, of course, be put on each of the Bills at the conclusion of the debate.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)- Is it the wish of the House to have a general debate covering these three measures? There being no objection, I will allow that course to be followed.
Mr BARRY JONES (Lalor) (9.6)-This is a cognate debate for the Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill 1982 and two consequential measures, the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Bill 1982 and the Television Stations Licence Fees Amendment Bill 1982. We applaud the legislation so far as it goes but we do not think it really goes far enough. The policy of the Australian Labor Party is to get new services into regions and, as far as it can, encourage local inputs without going too far in the direction of parochialism. We are concerned that the local organisations, stations and outlets should not be swamped by networks. It is also important that stations have a fair prospect of viability.
The intention of the Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill is to get such services into the country. Although the Opposition will not oppose the second reading of the Bill, it thinks that the legislation proposed is incomplete. We would like to have seen a broader consideration of the broadcasting system as a whole. We take the view that, if new services cannot be obtained by the existing commercial configuration, perhaps there ought to be more opportunity for public or national broadcasting. It is long overdue for the Government to consider what it will do about a second Australian Broadcasting Commission regional network, for example, and to provide further outlets for community stations especially in those areas, some of them metropolitan areas, which are not presently serviced such as Melbourne's western suburbs about which I have spoken to the Minister for Communications (Mr N. A. Brown) on several occasions. The Bill introduces supplementary licences for television and amends existing provisions so far unused for radio. It increases the maximum size of the membership of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal from five to eight.
There is a different concept in this Bill from its parent Act. There is the provision for the licensing

of services rather than the licensing of transmitters. In this legislation, a supplementary licence is one granted in the same area to an existing licensee. It is not subject to the normal ownership and control provisions, that is, it is seen as being one with the existing licence for certain purposes. The legislation does not apply to the five mainland State capitals. The question of supplementary licences does not arise for them. Under the legislation for radio, the supplementaries must be by frequency modulation transmission. For television ultra high frequency or very high frequency and radiated subscription services are permitted. Under the legislation, a total of 99 commercial FM services and 36 commercial TV services could be established.
As I said earlier, the Bill creates licences for services as distinct from the existing system of licences for transmitters. If a service area requires several transmitters-that is, the main one and one or more translaters-one licence is to cover the lot. The procedure is this: An existing licensee applies to the Minister for a single supplementary licence. A consortium of licensees serving the same area may also apply. The Minister may accept the application and forward it to the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal which may conduct an inquiry into the grant. Secondly, the Minister may reject it on planning or technical grounds. [n response the Broadcasting Tribunal will either recommend to the Minister that invitations for an independent licence be issued or grant the supplementary licence for up to a five-year period. It should be noted that the position with respect to a potential independent who may initiate action for a licence is unaffected by the Bill. However, it could be affected by the establishment of a government policy on supplementary manifest by the Bill. At the moment if a person-that is, a natural person or a legal entity --wants a licence in an area he fills in an expression of interest form with the Department, which then asks him to complete a planning pro forma. The pro forma must then pass the test of whether it is within government policy. If it is, the Minister at that point makes a decision on whether he will invite an application. This existing procedure is unaffected by the Bill.
Until now, with no settled policy on how commercial frequency modulation services are to be extended to the country, no application has passed the test of whether it is within government policy. Now that a policy has been settled it is theoretically possible for the Minister to invite applications. However, it seems likely that the Minister will never invite an application for an

Broadcasting and TV Amendment Bill

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3123

independent licence as a result of this process because to do so would be to declare that a particular independent is viable-something that otherwise would normally be a matter for determination by the Tribunal. A Minister would be in a somewhat embarrassing situation if, after holding an inquiry, the Broadcasting Tribunal knocked him back and cast doubt on his judgment because it did not think the application had viability. So presumably it is a right that would be exercised with some trepidation by a Ministerunless perhaps it was the Minister's predecessor.
Although there is nothing about it in the Bill, it is understood that when the Minister makes a statement on the scheme after the Bill's passage he will announce the date by which applications must be in from existing licensees. Otherwise he will say that he will call for independent licensees to make their applications. But this is considered a hollow threat. The reality of the situation is that all licensees will apply to forestall independence. There will be a long queue for processing. I do not know whether the mills of the ABT grind exceedingly small but they certainly grind very slowly. The fact that it is chronically short of staff and there are many--
Mr Ian Cameron-Efficient government.
Mr BARRY JONES-Not when it does not deliver the goods. That is the test of efficiency. In holding its inquiry the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal is required to determine whether an independent commercial licence would be viable and, if so, whether it would be in the public interest to invite applications. There is simply no specification of what the onus of proof of viability is and where it lies.
The Bill says that the Broadcasting Tribunal is to have regard only to the following public interest criteria: Firstly, the nature of the community to be served; secondly, the diversity of the interests of the community; and, thirdly, the nature of the broadcasting and television services of which satisfactory reception is being obtained by the community. Honourable members should note that there is no reference to any criteria such as media concentration of Press and other electronic media holdings because of that word 'only'. The ABT cannot take them into account. Honourable members should note also that there are no public interest criteria whatsoever for the granting of a supplementary licence. In other words, an existing licensee can argue against an independent applicant on two broad groundsthat is to say, non-viability and that the public interest criteria are not satisfied. By contrast, a

potential independent applicant cannot argue against a supplementary licence on public interest grounds, presumably because the Bill specifies grounds to be noted for an independent applicant but not for a supplementary licence that precludes general grounds or grounds arising elsewhere in the Act in considering a supplementary licence. It therefore seems that potential independents will not be treated equally with the incumbents. It is not known what the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal criteria of viability will be but presumably consistent criteria would have to be developed.
After eight years of operation, special renewal procedures take effect. At that stage the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal must determine whether an independent licence would be viable and, if so, whether it would be in the public interest to invite applications. If the ABT determines that an independent licence is viable, it then renews the supplementary licence for up to two years and recommends the Minister to invite applications for an independent service to begin three years later. There is then a procedure whereby the licensee can give up one of his two licences and transfer it; that is, he can sell it and only if he cannot sell it does a hearing take place for the independents. The effect of this, of course, is that the licensee is in a position where he selects his successor and gets the market value of the station.
On the face of it, if the licensee has scope under the ownership and control of provisions-that is, if he has not got a prescribed interest in the permitted maximum number of licences-he could transfer the licence to a company controlled by himself. He would then be in a position to own two independent licences in the one area. However, by virtue of clause 2 of the Bill, existing section 89A (I D) (c) (ii) of the act will apply to the transfer of a supplementary licence. This provides that the Broadcasting Tribunal shall have regard to 'the need to avoid undue concentration of influence' on the transferee, where a country licence is being transferred. This could presumably allow the Broadcasting Tribunal to refuse consent to a transfer even within the ownership and control limits. It should be noted that the Government has deliberately made a point of trying to prevent undue concentration in this case. Therefore, it may be argued that it admits the principle and it should be applied to the granting of supplementary licences in some way.
The approach adopted by the Opposition is: Firstly, we agree that a principle should be

3124 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Broadcasting and TV Amendment Bill

adopted and that some way has to be found for country areas to get new services; secondly, we believe that new services should, if possible, be independent of existing ones to provide for a greater range of choice; thirdly, we agree that if independent services could not possibly be viable, then supplementary licences should be issued; and, fourthly, we insist on avoiding undue concentration of influence across all media, which we believe is not only in the public interest but also even in the interests of advertisers. Therefore, we will propose some amendments giving guidence to the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal; for example, by directing it to adopt a liberal attitude to the question of viability.
If, for instance, a station which is automated is doing, say, the 'beautiful music' format which is common on some frequency modulation stations using syndicated news services it may well be that it could be profitable on a much lower budget than is conventionally regarded as necessary in the industry. There is very little in the way of overheads and hardly anything in labour costs. If one station in the area is local, there is also the possibility that program regulations could be adjusted to relax localism requirements for an FM commercial station. There is also the philosophical point about the degree to which governments should guarantee a market in such an industry. At the moment that is the Government's problem. The fact is that neither the Department nor the Broadcasting Tribunal has the resources, nor I think the critical attitude necessary to develop and to establish an appropriate viability level. Both will be heavily influenced by the existing industry in whose interests it will be to set the level as high as possible. The only real way to tell would be to let some independent operators try it out and then observe the results.
At the Committee stage I will move an amendment to ensure that a potential independent has the same facility to initiate an inquiry as an incumbent; secondly, an amendment to create a presumption of viability of an independent; and, thirdly, an amendment requiring the Broadcasting Tnbunal to have regard to concentration of influence in granting a supplementary licence. Obviously the very act of granting a supplementary licence may be in itself-that is a supplementary licence to an already existing licencee- . a concentrating of service. So the test would not apply to that. The granting is regarded as a last resort if there is no possibility of independent viability being established. But there remains the problem of concentration across other media. It is not a

theoretical problem. In Tasmania, for instance, the Rouse interests own all television stations, Launceston and Hobart radio and the Press. As the Bill stands there is nothing preventing them gaining an extra television station and an extra radio station in both Launceston and Hobart. That would surely, even to this libertarian Government, be excessive.
The Department's rationale is to say: 'But if one denies them supplementaries on the ground of concentration then you deny the people of Tasmania, or wherever, services. Therefore you have to give it to the existing interests. It is too bad but there it is'. This argument stands up only if there is no other way in which these commercial services can be provided. There are at least two other recourses and the procedure would go like this: Firstly, the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal would look at viability and then apply a public interest test on concentration, taking regard to media concentration generally, factors of ownership and also general geographical factors. If a case fails the concentration test-that is, where there is undue concentration such as in the Rouse case-then clearly the viability test could be more liberal insofar as it affects the incumbent. In other words, in business terms, because the incumbent owns radio, Press and television he would be less seriously affected by a forced sharing of, for example, the radio market with a newspaper, with a newcomer, than if he were simply a radio operator and nothing else because he is able to sprood his general business risk. Different viability criteria apply. His own viability would not be seriously threatened if he had a competitor in the field. Thus we could argue that the viability test be removed altogether in that case or in the general case of an incumbent who has extensive support elsewhere in the media. For example, take the case of Rupert Murdoch. Or at least there could be a heavy onus on the incumbent to prove why a newcomer would actually damage the services to the area, quite apart from the question of whether he would damage his own profits. In other words, it would be on the newcomer's head to make a go of it.
Secondly, alternatively as a competitive proposition to be put to the Tribunal, there could be aggregation of service areas for purposes of providing independent services where single service areas are found to be non-viable but where the incumbent fails the concentration test. In general there must be economies in running a networking situation over several service areas. Therefore there must be an aggregation of service areas so that the aggregation is viable. Although a lack of

Broadcasting and TV Amendment Bill

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3125

localism may be advanced as an argument against this there are undoubtedly areas which have very strong regional features such as the Central West and North Coast of New South Wales or Gippsland. Each area encompasses several local service areas and they are identifiably regions in economic and other terms, such as sport. There is of course, a long-standing case for the promotion of regional radio and television.
The third factor is that although more politically difficult it would be possible to permit the granting of a supplementary licence to an incumbent radio or television station only if that licence were divested from any concentrated pan-media grouping. Such a provision would need to be accompanied by a way of ensuring that a potential independent could initiate an inquiry. But if there was no right to initiate an inquiry the incumbent would just sit tight. There could be a provision saying that if the incumbent refused to divest, as he would of course, then an independent would be allowed to proceed with his application as a matter of course. While admitting that here would be areas where the only recourse would be supplementary licences, it is highly likely that the existing industry greatly overstates the viability issue because it is in its obvious direct interest to do so. Furthermore, given eight years of incumbency it is highly unlikely that a station would ever be divested. The industry would prevail on a Liberal-National Party Government, if such a government were in office, to change the Act if that ever looked like happening. There are plenty of precedents for that.
As I mentioned earlier in my opening remarks, the other two pieces of legislation are merely consequential. The Television Stations Licence Fees Amendment Bill and the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Bill simply provide for the licencees to be paid for supplementary licences. In conclusion I point out to the House that at the Committee stage we will be raising a number of matters by way of amendment.
Mr JULL (Bowman) (9.25)-The Broadcasting Television Amendment Bill, in terms of listeners to radio stations and viewers of television stations in country areas of Australia, is probably one of the most significant pieces of legislation to come before this Parliament in more than 20 years. It is part of the overall long term Government project of extending to those people in remote and rural areas the best available programming that can be provided through existing television and radio services. This is yet another

step by this Government to try to bring to them that range of services which obviously in the past they have so sorely missed. In fact, it is an opportunity for some 99 commercial FM radio services and 36 new television services to be considered for these rural and remote areas of Australia. That in itself is rather a significant move. I do not really think that any honourable member in this House would deny that the choice of programs available to many of these rural listeners and viewers has in the past been most restricted indeed.
One of the other interesting aspects of this Bill is the fact that it is directed, in the case of radio, primarily to the FM mode. That is important too. I suppose in many respects it was almost by mistake that Australia was introduced to FM radio which potentially is the best broadcasting medium that we can possibly provide in this country. If we take our minds back through history we will see that under the terms of the old Wireless Telegraphy Act FM radio was initially introduced into Australia. This legislation helps in providing a more professional use of FM radio and, most importantly, a professional use of FM radio in these regional and remote areas of Australia. It is an opportunity for existing country stations to test the markets and to see whether they can provide alternative programming through this particular mode.
I was interested to listen tonight to the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) and some of his arguments that he put. Honourable members will excuse me I am sure if I am wrong in saying that I think there was almost an assumption in his speech that most of the existing country radio operators were almost sitting on a bed of roses in terms of their profitability. I do not really think that is the case. In actual fact an analysis of the incomes and profit margins of many regional operators in Australia would show that their viability is very much at stake. I would also see this legislation as providing them with an opportunity to try to make their present situation more viable for very little outlay. Certainly there are opportunities within the proposed legislation for a market to be tested to see whether so many of these regional centres may in their own right be entitled to an additional independent service. Importantly too I think we have to give that opportunity to back up many of these regional operators so they can continue to provide not only their AM service but also these alternative services as well. Of course, one would hope that they would take great advantage of their opportunity to enter this particular mode-the FM mode-especially to

3126 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Broadcastmg and TV Amendment Bill

provide all that is best in terms of the technical quality in the broadcasting services. That really is what FM radio is all about. One would hope that any operator of these particular licences would move to use that medium to the very best advantage.
With the capital city stations-certainly this legislation does not apply to them-we have seen a very great acceptance of FM by listeners. A look at recent radio services around Australia would certainly indicate that the general public is adapting to such stations very quickly indeed. Surveys would indicate a very great increase in the number of listeners to FM radio services in capital cities. That has especially been so in cities such as Perth--as shown in recent surveys--and Brisbane and Sydney as well. We could see that as indicating that perhaps Australians are quicker than most people in other countries to adapt to new forms of communications and to new services that might be available. In that respect, the legislation should be welcome. It is a very real opportunity for Australians to have even greater diversity in their radio information and entertainment.
I mentioned a little earlier that this is just part of a total package of this Government to expand the whole area of communications on a much grander scale than we have known in the past. It provides an opportunity for Australians in country and remote areas to receive full services and an opportunity for people in the cities over the next few years to have on their hands almost a revolution in communications. Whilst we speak tonight of the supplementary licences for FM radio and for rural television interests, it is only a few years down the track when Australia will be benefiting from the services that the communication satellite can bring and from the even more exciting prospects of the introduction of radiated subscription television or cable television.
Although the legislation before the House tonight will not give the electronics industry such a tremendous chance to increase its participation in the manufacturing areas of the industry, I guess that it is a start. The Australian industry has caught on to FM radio, and one wonders what the true potential of the whole area of communications will be over the next five years. At a time when we are Iboking for employment and when the Australian electronics industry is going through severe periods of hardship, legislation such as this can in itself create a lot of the much needed employment in Australia. One hopes that this Government will be forward-looking enough in its very near future to make some very real

statements about the future of the new communications media, especially cable television. We have already had in this House over the last few years some debates about the communications capabilities of cable television. One would think it was almost time for the Government to start really making some recommendations about the possible introduction of cable television, perhaps even more than RSTV, for the future expansion of the electronics industry in Australia.
I would be most happy if the Government would make a commitment for the almost immediate introduction of cable television services in Australia. I say that bearing in mind that we have to make some rather hard decisions about the regulations that might govern the future of cable television in Australia. If we look at the cold, hard facts, we see that Australians have proved themselves to be amongst world leaders in accepting new technology. That was evident with the introduction of colour television into Australia. Within a matter of months of the introduction of colour television, something like 62 per cent of homes in this country were saturated with it. Australia has the greatest acceptance of any country in the world of home videotape recorders. The saturation level is something like 32 per cent already. With the early introduction of cable television services, which would cover not only entertainment but also all sorts of information services, that record would be improved upon even more.
The most exciting part of the introduction of cable television is not the services that it can bring but the employment opportunities that it can provide at a time such as this. On my estimations even the announcement of the initial plans to go ahead with cable television could start in the employment area such a revolution that, by the time it was introduced, at least 20,000 jobs would have been created in the electronics industry. The introduction of cable television here would mean a very high usage of labour. It would mean also a very high usage of capital, and at a time such as this that is probably what we need more than anything else. There is no denying that cable television would be restricted initially to the capital cities only. But that is well and good because that is where it will be viable and easily introduced and that is where there is already an expectation of the new technology which is just around the corner. If we introduce 20,000 jobs into the electronics industry, we can also look at the entertainment and information aspects of cable to see what sort of employment they will generate too.
The experience in the United States of America is that cable television services are great consumers of television programs of all types, not

Broadcasting and TV Amendment Bill

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3127

only in the entertainment area but also in the information area. Already a fledgling industry in Australia is providing some entertainment services to cable television stations in the United States. I am sure that most members of this House have already heard of the acceptance, for example, of Australian Rules football in the Los Angeles area. I guess it was a case of providing some fill-in programs but it certainly opened a whole new market for the Australian football business on the West Coast of the United States. A gentleman I know from my home city of Brisbane who has been producing a travel series for Australian television services has just sold his programs to an American cable concern. His return on the cost of that production has been about three times his outlay. Already he is making plans for the expansion of that service throughout other areas of the United States. I suppose that is an indication of the potential for Australia if we get into that mode early enough.
There is an opportunity in the general information areas and the educational areas for Australians to take advantage of this whole new technology. If we can get in early enough we can provide not only a home market but also a whole new export market. I hope that within the coming months we will look very seriously indeed at getting down to the job and making sure that we have our own regulations correct in terms of the things that should govern the introduction of cable television. I hope that Australia will get on the bandwagon and take advantage not only of the local market but also, through our own technology and the advancements that have already been made in that field, an export market of not inconsiderable proportions through Asia and beyond in the years to come.
As I said at the beginning of my address tonight, this legislation is yet another step in the everexpanding concern that this Government has for all Australians to receive the best communications services available. This will certainly mean that people in many of the country areas of Australia will have a greater diversity of services and a greater quality of services. In those respects, with the safeguards that are envisaged in this legislation, I certainly commend the Bills.
