Mr BARRY JONES (Lalor) (9.52)- The first two amendments the Opposition offers relate to clause 8 which states:
Subject to sub-section (3). the holder of a licence for a commercial broadcasting station or a consortium each member of which is the holder of such a licence may lodge with the Minister, in accordance with a form approved by the Minister, an application for the grant of a licence
And so on. We simply propose to add to new subsections 82A (I) and 82A (2) of the principal Act the words, 'or any other person'. The point is

simply that the purpose of this amendment is to put potential independent operators on the same footing as the holders of the licences to initiate an inquiry into the grant of supplementary and independent licences. As the Bill stands, only holders may initiate an inquiry. If one is a holder of a licence, an incumbent, it is extremely unlikely one would want to have an inquiry so that one could be knocked off. It is like sitting members of the Liberal Party or indeed of any other party saying, 'Look, I think I will initiate a challenge to myself for pre-selection'. It does not make an awful lot of sense. One cannot imagine that there would be too many incumbents who would be racing around trying to organise somebody to run against them. If one is to have an inquiry, if one is to have the option open, one has to have the right of application by the person who wants to challenge the status quo.
Mr Jull--But you can do that under existing legislation.
Mr BARRY JONES-Let us make that clear. The legislation is by no means-as the Minister for Communications (Mr N. A. Brown), I think, would generously concede-the easiest piece of legislation in the world to read. It is not exactly Gibbonian in its lucidity.
Mr N. A. Brown-Gibberish.
Mr BARRY JONES-Yes. It is quite patent on the face of proposed new section 82A that that right is exclusively the right of the licence holder. The licence holder is the only person named who may lodge an application with the Minister. If the Minister says that there is some alternative right, no doubt he will exercise his rights to explain what it is. But I think we have to make this absolutely clear by inserting the words 'or any other person' in proposed new sections 82A (I) and 82A (2). If the existing licensee does not really want a supplementary licence-of course, he may be doubtful about its economic value to him-he can block the process of determination whether there should be an independent or a supplementary licence by simply not applying. In other words, if he does not want the matter opened up at all, he just does nothing. There may be a marginal case for that, one way or the other.
Mr Bourchier-Then it lapses and others can apply for it.
Mr BARRY JONES-That is not at all clear in this provision. I would be interested to hear, as a piece of textual exegesis, the honourable member's views on it. We believe on the basis of

3130 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Broadcasting and TV Amendment Bill

our policy that a prime requirement is to get new services to rural areas. There should be a process whereby a determination can be brought to a head.
The arguments which may be made against an amendment to the clause are, firstly, that anyone can apply to a Minister now to invite an application. There is no formal process for this-just a request to the Minister whose power it is to call for applicants. This is true. But, since the Bill gives the right to an existing licensee to apply, it should as a matter of equity give the same right to others. Secondly, it may be argued that we are creating a new automatic right and therefore taking planning power away from the Minister. We argue that that is not so because the legislation makes a recommendation that the Minister call for applications for a licence if the Minister finds that an independent licence is viable. We could have put up an amendment making it mandatory for the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal to grant an independent licence. This would have made a parallel process to the granting of a supplementary licence. But we believe that the recommendation carries enough force. We think the opportunity should be there for further review by the Minister.
The Minister may argue that all licensees will apply anyway or that he will announce that he will call applications for independent licences in all areas if applications for supplementary licences are not received by a certain date. There is no reason why a potential independent should be held up by such a process or be made subject to the Minister's discretion when that independent's competitors-the existing licensees-have a statutory right. It is just a matter of simple equity. The incumbent has a statutory right conferred by the legislation; in effect, the independent is subject to the discretion of the Minister. He relies on ministerial whim, if one likes. We believe that that is quite inequitable. Therefore, 1 urge the Government to accept the amendments to insert in both cases the words 'or any other person'. 1seek leave to move the two amendments together.

BROADCASTING AND TELEVISION AMENDMENT BILL 1982
In Committee
Consideration resumed.
Mr N. A. BROWN (Diamond ValleyMinister for Communications) (10.1)-1 said a moment ago that the Government was not able to accept the amendments proposed by the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones). The reason why the Government is not able to accept those amendments is that they proceed, with the greatest of respect, upon a misapprehension of the nature of the supplementary licence scheme. The very basis of the supplementary licence scheme is that machinery is created by the Bill under which the licensee is able to activate the whole mechanism. He is able, as the Bill provides, to lodge with the Minister an application for the grant, in effect, of a supplementary licence. To say that somebody other than the holder of the licence can do that is, with the greatest of respect, a nonsense. It would be the exact complete negation of the whole supplementary licence scheme to provide, as the honourable member for Lalor has proposed, that someone other than the current licensee has the right to lodge the application. Even if one accepted the sort of principle that the honourable member has contended should be implemented, he would have to do a complete redrafting exercise of the present provision. That is so because the honourable member for Lalor would ask us to accept that when his amendment was added to the Bill, proposed new section 82A for example, would mean that the holder of the licence for a commercial broadcasting station, et cetera, may lodge with the Minister, et cetera, an application for the grant of a licence for a broadcasting service to someone other than himself. Clearly, the other person would not be applying for a supplementary licence. As 1 said, the proposed amendments are not consistent with the whole structure of the

Broadcasting and TV Amendment Bill

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3131

scheme. If one wanted to implement the principle contended for it would require the whole series of provisions to be redrafted.
In addition, I make the point that the proposed amendments would have the effect of compressing two inquiries into one and telescoping two independent inquiry processes involved in the supplementary licence scheme into one public inquiry. That would mean that the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal would be required to decide two things at the one time and in the one hearing. In the first place it would have to decide whether an independent licence was viable and in the public interest and whether a supplementary licence should be issued to an existing licensee. It ~ould also have to determine, if an independent lIcence were viable, which of the applicants should be offered the licence. I do not know whether anyone would be able to work out who was bouncing the ball at an inquiry of that sort but it would be, in our submission, unnecessarily ~~nf~sing. if the Tribunal were embarking upon a JOint inqUiry concerned with both of those matters at the same time. We are of the view that the issues can be properly resolved only by two separate public inquiries. Apart from involving the Tribunal in those excessively complex supplementary licence inquiries, the one process dealing with the two investigations to which I have referred would mean that prospective licensees for an independent licence would be required to prepare detailed applications for inquiries in doubtful areas on the off-chance-that is all it would betha.t the Trib~nal might determine that applicatIOns for an Independent licence should be considered. The whole thing would be utterly confusing.
I revert to the main point; that is, that we are unfortunately unable to accept the amendment because to do so would negate the whole structure of the scheme, the very basis of which is that we take the current licensee and, in effect, invite him under the scheme to apply for a supplementary licence. If he does not do so then the field is open an.d the Minister may activate the machinery to brIng the potential independent aspirants into the field. But there is no point in going on with the supplementary licence scheme if this proposed amendment were added to the Bill.
!l~ THEOPHANOUS (Burke) (10.6)-The MInister for Communications (Mr N. A. Brown), may be quite lucid but his logic is rather twisted. I cannot see why we cannot add the words, 'or any other person' after the mention of:
The holder . . . or a consortium each member of whi<;,h is the holder of such a licence may lodge with the Minister, m accordance with a form approved .

~ould thi.s not have the effect which we are tryI~g to achl~ve, namely, that if somebody in a particular regIOn or a particular area wants a second or further television licence, that is a television licence or a radio licence other than the ones that exists that person would not be able, under these provisions, to apply for that licence? The Minister made the point in his remarks that the power in the Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill is given to the person with an existing licence who wants to apply for a supplementary licence. The Minister said: 'If that person does not apply then the field is open for others'. But why should an opportunity be given in the first instance to the person who has a licence in a particular town or city? Why not simply say: 'All right, if someone feels that they can make a go of a television station or a radio station, whether or not they happen to be t~e owner of an alternative station, why not simply allow them, under the provisions set out in the Bill, to apply and to meet the conditions required so that we can treat independents and those who are already owners in the same way'. The principle of equity is important here as is the principle of concentration of media ownership. There is nothing in proposed new section 82A which is inconsistent with adding the words which have been suggested by the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones).
Amendments negatived.
Clauses agreed to.
Clause 9 (Consideration of applications by Tribunal).
The TEMPORARY CHAIRMAN (Dr Jenkins)-Before I call the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) I assume that he is seeking leave to move the amendments that have been circulated. I indicate to him that the circulated amendments (4) and (5) should more properly be one amendment because they refer to the same line of the Bill. Amendments (4) and (5) become amendment (4). Amendment (6), as circulated, becomes amendment (5).
Mr BARRY JONES (Lalor) (10.9)-by leave-I move:
(3) Clause 9, page 7, line 6. after 'licence' (second occurring) insert 'and the Tribunal has not refused to grant the licence by vIrtue of paragraph 83 (6) (gr.
(4) Clause 9, page 7,line 19, after paragraph (e) insert the followmg paragraphs:
'tea) by adding after paragraph (f) of sub-section (6) the following paragraph:
"(g) the Tribunal is satisfied that, where the licence is a supplementary licence, the granting of such licence would not result in undue concentration of influence, whether direct or indirect, on the applicant, having regard to the ownershIp and control of other media serving the area to be served by the licence.". Broadcasting and TV Amendment Bill

'reb) by inserting after sub-section (6) the following sub-section:

"(6A) In making a determination under sub-paragraph 83 (6) (e) (i), the Tribunal shall determine that a licence, other than a supplementary licence, would be reasonably likely to be commercially viable unless, on the basis of evidence supplied to the Tribunal, the Tribunal concludes otherwise.". '.

?f the amendments (3) and (4), amendment (4) IS .the key amendment. I will just say something bnefly by way of introduction of amendment (3). Amendment 3 deals with the case where the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal has not found 'undue concentration'. It says that in that case the course is to follow the Bill's procedure; that is, the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal goes on to determine whether an independent is viable and, if so, it recommends that the Minister invite an independent to apply for a licence. If not, it grants the supplementary licence. I want to say a few more words about amendment (4). Amendment (4) proposes to add in section 83 of the principal Act paragraph (g), which reads:

The tribunal is satisfied that. where the licence is a supplementary licence, the granting of such licence would not result in undue concentration of influence. whether direct or indirect. on the applicant. having regard to the ownership and control of other media serving the area to be served by the licence.

Amendment (4) says that a licence will not be

given to a licensee if he is part of a cross-media

grouping. It is meant to apply, for instance, in the

case I mentioned in the second reading debate, to

the Rouse case in Tasmania. We will not counten-

ance the situation where all radio, television and

Press in the town are owned by one person or

company. This is unhealthy for diversity. It is also

unhealthy for advertisers. We do not believe that

this amendment would deny new services to

country people. It would work as follows. Section

83 (6) of the principal Act in effect says that if the

Tribunal holds an inquiry and a set of the con-

ditions set out in section 6 paragraphs (a) to (f)

are satisfied, the licence will be granted. The sec-

tion applies to all licences and within the set para-

graph (e) deals with supplementary licences. A

new paragraph (e) is created in this Bill. Amend-

ment (4), which would insert a new paragraph

marked (ea), would set a seventh condition-that

is, paragraph (g)-which says that for a sup-

plementary effect, is to

licence an 'undue concentration' be frowned upon. Notice it is

~oint

undue concentration of ownership but undue

concentration of influence. The term is used else-

where in the Act, so we are not introducing a new

concept.

Amendment (4) (eb) provides for evidence
about commercial viability to be presented to the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal if there is to be

any challenge to the presumption of commercial viability. Amendment (5) treats the case where the Broadcasting Tribunal has found 'undue concentration '. It says that if a supplementary licence has been refused on those grounds the recommendation goes automatically to the Minister and he then invites application for an independent. In other words, it then abandons any test of viability.
It is important to emphasise that the inquiry ceases at that point. In our view it would be wrong to allow such a concentration of influence. It is contrary to the established philosophy along those lines originally set as far back as October 1935 when the first regulations against concentration of ownership in radio were made. It has had twoparty support ever since. As recently as the time of the former Minister---who is now an aviation superstar-- the Honourable A. A. Staley, this Government decided that FM was to be subject to the provisions along with AM radio, thus following the policy.
The arguments to be adduced in addition to those I have just given are, first, that if the licensee in a cross-ownership situation wants a supplementary licence badly enough he can divest himself of his other interests, when he will then be able to pass the 'undue influence' test; that is, he would be eligible or, alternatively, if he is cross-owned he ?ught to be viable enough to withstand any mdependent competition. Therefore, it is up to the mdependents to make a go of it. This is, paradoxically coming from us, a free enterprise argument if ever there was one. We are great advocates of free enterprise under certain limited circumstances. It is unlikely to be the established licensee who will be in trouble; it is much more likely to be the newcomer. If the argument that is put up here fails a Labor government would be prepared to consider in future other ways of bringing new services to the country.
I repeat what I said when I opened my remarks at the second reading stage. The flaw of this Bill is that it considers commercial services in isolation. There are other ways such as public or national broadcasting by which new services could be brought in. Unfortunately this Bill ignores these. In fact those alternative methods might well turn out to be more acceptable to many existing commercial broadcasters. I hardly need to restate the argument on the question of undue concentration. I simply say that we stand by the view that we need diversity and multiple sources of information in a society like ours. We believe that there s?ould be competition and advertising and that It IS very unhealthy for all outlets to be tied up by one group in one town of which probably

Broadcasting and TV Amendment Bill

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3133

the most outstanding examples in Australia would be Launceston and Mildura.
Mr N. A. BROWN (Diamond ValleyMinister for Communications) (10.17)-1 have no doubt whatsoever that the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) is motivated by some pure motives. The objective of competition of course is desirable but the Government is unable to accept the proposed amendments. I draw the honourable member's attention to amendment (4). At the start we could not accept this in any event because there seems to be a drafting fault which creates a double negative. Therefore it seems to achieve exactly the opposite of the effect which, I presume, is intended by the honourable member. If I read the opening words of the provision of the Broadcasting and Television Act and then add the clause proposed by the honourable member I think the fault will become apparent. Section 83 (6) of the Act provides:
The Tribunal shall not refuse to grant a licence to a person unless it has held an inquiry into the grant of the licence and
I now adopt the honourable member's words:
(g) the Tribunal is satisfied that. where the licence is a supplementary licence. the granting of such licence would not result in undue concentration of influence .
The honourable member intended to say that the licence would not be granted if there were an undue concentration of influence but in fact he has, with respect, said the opposite. As I have said section 83 (6) provides that the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal shall not refuse to grant a licence unless a number of things happen. If one of the things that happens is that there is not undue concentration of influence presumably the intention would be that the licence would not be issued. However the result would be that the honourable member would have a licence issued in that case. It is probably just a drafting mistake but it illustrates one of the several problems that we have with the legislation.
The second matter is that the reference to, and I quote from the honourable member's amendment, any 'other media' does not have any definition. There would inevitably be difficulties of interpretation which may result in applications for judicial review and endless arguments if the Tribunal gave concentration of influence as a reason for any decision made against the grant of a licence. The fact that there is no definition of any other media would seem by itself to make this an unworkable amendment in our submission. In any event this is probably the substantial objection that we have. If one imposes this criteria it is, once

again, at variance with the whole basis of the supplementary licence scheme. It is at variance because it would enable the Tribunal to refuse to grant a supplementary licence, which is what the whole exercise is about, on grounds of concentration of ownership even in those areas where the Tribunal has found that an independent licence would not be viable. Therefore, we could get to the stage where the Tribunal could find that an independent licence would not be viable and the result of that would be that the community would miss out on the service because the licence would be denied to the applicant on the grounds, presumably, of undue concentration of ownership. As I said, that could arise even where the Tribunal has found that an independent licence would not be viable to serve the area. Therefore, people would miss out on obtaining that service.
In respect of the other amendment-I am assuming that the honourable member has said what he proposes to say about it-we do not believe that it is appropriate that there should be this onus of proof provision worked into the legislation as it would result in an automatic Tribunal determination that an independent licence would be viable unless evidence to the countrary were offered. We think this is an undersirable way of putting it. In the first place, we say that the proposed amendment does not state what the standard of proof is that will be required by the Tribunal. As the applicant would attempt to supply and the Tribunal would require that evidence anyway, the amendment in our submission is unnecessary and probably unworkable.
Secondly, the proposed amendment overlooks the extensive powers and statutory obligations of the Tribunal to inform itself thoroughly on these matters. I refer the Committee to section 25 (1 ) and section 25 (2) of the Act. Section 25 (1) provides that:
The Tribunal shall, without regard to legal forms and solemnities, make a thorough investigation into all matters relevant to an inquiry under this Division. and may give all such directions and do all such things as the Tribunal considers are necessary or expedient for the expeditious and just hearing of the inquiry.
Section 25 (2) provides that:
The Tribunal is not bound by legal rules of evidence and may inform itself on any matter in such manner as it thinks tit.
Therefore, the Tribunal already has a very wide charter to look at a whole range of matters. The Government takes the view that, having gone down the path of giving extensive powers to the Tribunal and having vested the Tribunal with the responsibility of hearings and issuing licences to applicants, it is appropriate that it should be allowed to have a wide ambit, to have regard to the

3134 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Adjournment

matters to which it wishes to have regard and to conduct its investigations in its own way, without shackling it by specific provisions relating to an onus of proof which would unnecessarily restrict its activities and the proper performance of its functions. As 1 said at the outset, although I am sure the honourable member is well motivated by the principles which he has espoused the Government is not able to accept the amendments proposed.
Amendments negatived.
Clause agreed to.
Remainder of Bill-by leave-taken as a whole, and agreed to.

Multiculturalism Motion (by Mr N. A. Brown) proposed:

That the House do now adjourn.
Dr THEOPHANOUS (Burke) (10.27)-1 wish to speak about what is happening in the field of ethnic affairs and multiculturalism in this country. Let me say at the outset that 1 am very disturbed at the attempts that have been made to blame the present economic crisis on immigration and, more seriously, on the role of migrant and ethnic communities in this country. We just cannot afford at a time of serious economic problems to have divisions arising because people are blaming ethnic or migrant communities for those problems. The fact is that for nearly 40 years now we have worked to build a multicultural and integrated Australia. In working towards this goal we have had difficulties and problems. Nevertheless, we have not attempted to divide one section from another or to blame one section of the community for our economic problems. Unfortunately, however, there are certain indications that this is happening.
What is more unfortunate is that certain prominent people in the community are attacking the very concept of multiculturalism and of a multicultural society. It is good that the Minister for Communications (Mr N. A. Brown) is in the House to answer the extraordinary statement by Professor Kramer, the Chairman of the Australian Broadcasting Commission. She not only said that we can get rid of multicultural television but also virtually implied that multiculturalism had no place in our community. Statements of that kind by prominent individuals such as Professor Kramer are divisive and are calculated to attack the basis on which we have tried to build our society since the Second World War. They should be denounced. I am perturbed that that kind of claim has not been denounced by the Government.
The concept of a multicultural society was approved by this Government and by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) in particular. The Government and certain of its spokespersons are backing away from that concept. The Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Hodges) recently made an absolute fool of himself at the national language conference when he tried to tell us---

Illegal Export of Spitfire Aircraft
(Question No. 3307)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 14 October 1981:
(I) Following the conclusion of legal proceedings against certain parties concerning the illegal export from Australia of a rare Spitfire Mk VIII MV 154 will the Government pursue the return of this aircraft to Australia now that the caSe has finished.
(2) What steps are being taken to prevent the possible future illegal exporting of historical aircraft.
(3) Will the penalties for the export of historical aircraft or artifacts be increased.
(4) If the Government is not to proceed with attempts to recover the Spit tire, what are the grounds for not doing so.
Mr Peacock-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (4) The Secretary of the Attorney-Genera!'s Department has advised the Comptroller-General of Customs

that an English court would probably not enforce a claim by the Commonwealth based on the forfeiture provisions of section 229 of the Customs Act 1901 and has advised against the instituting of proceedings in the United Kingdom. The then Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs accepted that advice and I have found no reason to disagree with that decision. Proceedings to recover possession of the aircraft to which the honourable member refers will not be instituted in the United Kingdom.
(2) The Department of Industry and Commerce exercises control over such exports through the Customs (Prohibited Exports) Regulations. Offences or attempted offences against this legislation render the persons concerned subject to the penal provisions of the Customs Act including the forfeiture of the goods concerned.
It would seem that there are persons who are prepared to become involved in schemes to circumvent known Customs controls over the export of aircraft of historical interest. Assurance is given that the Department will investigate such attempts and take appropriate action wherever necessary.
(3) Yes. Legislation recently passed by Parliament, but not yet proclaimed, increases the penalties from $1,000 to $50,000 or three times the value of the goods (where the Court can determine the value), whichever is the greater.

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3139

Draw Poker Machines
(Question No. 3969)
Mr Fry asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 17 February 1982:
(I) Are machines known as draw poker machines which are unlicensed and are used for gam bling and payment of cash prizes operating in some hotels and clubs in Canberra.
(2) Has the attention of his Department and the Australian Federal Police been drawn to the use of these machines over a period of months; if so. why has effective action to control their use still not been taken.
(3) What action does he intend to take to bring these machines under the control of the ACT Gaming and Liquor Authority.
Mr HodgmanThe answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes. However, draw poker machines are not required to be licensed.
(2) Yes. Although I earlier announced my support for the ACT House of Assembly's recommendation to ban draw poker machines, I am not now convinced that such action would necessarily be in the best interests of the community. Accordingly I have referred the matter back to the House of Assembly for its earliest consideration.
(3) The involvement of the ACT Gaming and Liquor Authority will depend on the final decision made on whether draw poker machines should be permitted in the ACT.
Personal Care Subsidy
(Question No. 4519)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 29 April 1982:
(I) Is the Commonwealth personal care subsidy paid only to institutions caring for old and frail and disabled persons; if so, why are (a) individuals caring for themselves and (b) persons caring for disabled people in private accommodation not eligible for this subsidy.
(2) Are parents caring for handicapped children at home eligible to receive the handicapped children's allowance of $18.25 whilst institutions are entitled to claim a personal care subsidy of $35 for the same children; if so, why.
Mr Wilson-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The Commonwealth Personal Care Subsidy is paid only to organisations eligible under Part III of the Aged or Disabled Persons Homes Act which provide approved hostel accommodation and personal care services to their frail residents. The subsidy which was recently increased by 50 per cent to $30 a week. is intended to assist those organisations to employ sufficient staff in their hostels to assist the frail residents to live as independently as possible.
Individuals caring for themselves and people caring for aged or disabled people in private accommodation are not eligible for Personal Care Subsidy because they do not meet the requirements of the legislation.
The Commonwealth Department of Health administers the Domiciliary Nursing Care Benefit under the National Health Act. This benefit is available to people who provide full time nursing care for chronically ill relatives in their homes: these patients would otherwise require admission to a nursing home. For a person to qualify for this benefit, the patient must:

be 16 years of age or over;
have a certificate from their doctor stating that, because of infirmity or illness. disease, incapacity or disability, they have a continuing need for nursing care;
be receiving adequate care from a registered nurse.
The current rate of benefit is $42 a fortnight and at 30 June 1982 there were approximately 18,000 approved beneficiaries.
There is also an attendant care pilot scheme being developed by the Department of Social Security which was allocated $100,000 for this purpose in the 1982-83 Budget. This pilot will be administered as part of the Handicapped Persons Welfare Program Up-grading Scheme. The Attendant Care Scheme will investigate the provision of alternative modes of personal care services to severely handicapped people who live in their own homes.
(2) The maximum rate of handicapped child's allowance currently payable is $85 a month. The allowance was introduced to assist parents who provide constant care and attention to their child where the child is severely or substantially handicapped and is cared for in the family home rather than in an institution. Handicapped children's benefit is currently paid at the rate of $5 per day. It is payable to approved charitable or religious organisations in respect of each handicapped child under the age of 16 who is accommodated in the residential centre conducted by the organisation. The amount of the benefit is required to be deducted from the account sent to the child's parents.
Crisis Accommodation
(Question No. 4604)
Mr Hayden asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
(I) Did the Prime Minister, in his policy speech on 30 September 1980, promise to establish crisis accommodation for families in distress.
(2) If so, when was this promise implemented and in what form.
(3) What was the Commonwealth's appropriation and spending on this program in (a) 1980-81 and (b) 1981-82.
(4) How many families were assisted in each year.
Mr Wilson-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Yes.
(2) On 24 February 1982 in a joint news release with the then Minister for Housing and Construction. the Hon. D. T. McVeigh, I announced the new Crisis Accommodation for Families in Distress Program. The press release indicated that the funds allocated could be used by the States, the Northern Territory and Aboriginal Hostels Ltd for the construction, purchase. or leasing of suitable dwellings for crisis accommodation for families.
(3) (a) 1980-81 AppropriationNil, Expenditure Nil; (b) 1981-82 Appropriation--$2m, Expenditure $2m.
In addition, I announced on I October 1982 the distribution of the $4m allocated for crisis accommodation by the Budget for 1982-83.
(4) Information on the number of families assisted is not available. However in 1981-82 the States, Northern Territory and Aboriginal Hostels Ltd used the $2m to: purchase 30 dwellings; construct I dwelling; lease about 180 dwellings; and provide accommodation for 13 additional beds in an Aboriginal Hostel.

Answers to Questions

Accounting Standards Review Board
(Question No. 4705)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:
(I) Further to my repeated questions regarding the urgent need to set up an Accounting Standards Review Board. can the Attorney-General intimate whether the submissions to the National Companies and Securities Commission relating to the Board have been completed.
(2) Has the Commission completed its findings; if so, has a report been made; if not, when will a report be made.
(3) Will the Commission's report be tabled in the Parliament.
(4) Is the National Companies and Securities Commission seeking information by way of submissions regarding the content of the revamped Schedule 9, that is, a re-listed old Schedule 7; if so, for what purpose.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Ministerial Council for Companies and Securities, at its meeting on 20 November 1981, directed the National Companies and Securities Commission (NCSC) to publicly expose until 31 March 1982 its proposals in relation to an Accounting Standards Review Board. The NCSC subsequently issued a media release on 26 November 1981 setting out its proposals for the establishment of such a Board. Further background on these proposals is contained in the answer given in this House by the then Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs on 29 October 1981 to Question No. 2694 from the honourable member (Hansard, page 2798).
(2) The NCSC has advised that it expects its report to be available for consideration by the Ministerial Council for Companies and Securities at its next meeting.
(3) It would be undesirable to make a decision on tabling the report until the Ministerial Council has had an opportunity to consider the issues raised in the report.
(4) The NCSC, on 12 May 1982. sought submissions by 16 August 1982 from a number of interested persons on the form and content of Schedule 7 to the Companies Regulations. In seeking submissions, the NCSC indicated that it wished to develop proposals for:
(i) making information contained in the published financial statements of companies more accessible and comprehensible;
(ii) removing inconsistencies, imprecision or ambiguity in the requirements;
(iii) amending requirements which may be inconsistent with recognised accounting standards; and
(iv) establishing a process for the continuous future scrutiny and, where necessary, revision of the requirements.
Any amendments to the regulations will be made following consideration by the Ministerial Council for Companies and Securities.
Aborigines at Robinvale, Victoria
(Question No. 4715)
Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:

(I) Has the Human Rights Commission, or any other body under his control, made any investigation of allegations of racism directed towards Aborigines in the town of Robinvale, Victoria, as reported on the ABC TV program Nationwide, on 15 March 1982.
(2) If so, (a) who made the investigation, (b) has the Government been furnished with any report of that investigation and, if so, when and (c) what recommendations have been made to the Government following that investigation.
(3) Can the Attorney-General now state what further action the Government proposes to take in respect of this matter.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) In response to requests from the Aboriginal community in Robinvale to the Commissioner for Community relations, two officers of the Human Rights Commission visited Robinvale, Victoria, in the course of conciliation field trips made to various centres in Victoria and Southern New South Wales during the period 4-16 May 1982 and met with a number of people in Robinvale including some who had been involved in the issues which had been the subject of the Nationwide program.
The Human Rights Commission has informed me that it appeared that there were no complaints which needed to be pursued under the Racial Discrimination Act but the team did what it could to assist in restoring good community relations in the town.
Further visits were made during the period 13-16 June 1982 by the team during which it was informed by the Aboriginal community that the situation was satisfactory.
(3) No further action is at present considered by the Human Rights Commission to be required but contact is, I understand, being maintained with members of the community in Robinvale as is the practice in relation to centres which have made complaints to the Commissioner.
Children Illegally Taken out of Australia
(Question No. 4763)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) How many children have been illegally taken out of Australia in each of the past 5 years.
(2) Is the Attorney-General able to give the figures on the basis of country of origin of the abducting parent.
(3) How many claims for legal aid have been (a) lodged and (b) granted in connection with these cases.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am advised that with regard to parts (I) and (2) of the honourable member's question, no details are kept of the number of children illegally taken out of Australia. I am therefore unable to give the figures requested nor am I able to give figures in relation to the country of origin of the abducting parent.
With regard however to part (3) of the question which concerns the number of claims for legal aid lodged and granted in connection with children being taken illegally from the country, I am advised that during the period 1977-1979 there were 9 applications for aid, 3 of which received act of grace payments recommended by the Attorney-General and approved by the Minister for Finance.

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3141

In August 1979 the Government introduced the Overseas Custody (Child Removal) Scheme to provide assistance to parents whose children have been removed from Australia illegally. Since then 73 applications for assistance under the Scheme have been lodged of which 18 have been granted. Of the remaining 55, 5 applications have been refused, 38 were not proceeded with and 12 are still being considered.

Uranium Plant in Queensland: Approaches on Possible Establishment
(Question No. 4861)
Mrs Darling asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:
(I) Has his Government been approached by either the Queensland Government, French industrialists or the French Atomic Energy Commission concerning the possible establishment of a uranium plant in Queensland.
(2) If so, what was the nature of these approaches and the Government's response.
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Government has not been approached by either the Queensland Government, French industralists or the French Atomic Energy Commission concerning the possible establishment of a uranium enrichment plant in Queensland.
The Government has been approached by French industrialists concerning the Ben Lomond uranium deposit near Townsville in regard to the development of a uranium mine and processing plant.
(2) Minatome Australia Pty Ltd. the French company associated with the Ben Lomond project, has been informed of the Commonwealth Government's environmental and foreign investment policy requirements which would need to be met before development approval could be considered.

Department of Business and Consumer Affairs: Assistance to Firms in Division of Lilley
(Question No. 4879)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 24 August 1982:
What firms and/or companies in the Electoral Divisio'n of Lilley have applied for financial assistance from his Department and/or the Department of Business and Consumer Affairs since November 1980; for which grants have they applied, and with what success.
Mr Peacock-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Government's bounty and subsidy schemes provide financial assistance to industry. The schemes were administered by the former Department of Business and Consumer Affairs and are now administered by the Department of Industry and Commerce.
The various bounty and subsidy Acts provide for the Parliament to be furnished with an annual return showing the amount of bounty or subsidy paid to claimants. The 1981-82 returns were tabled recently.
These returns will provide the honourable member with the information she is seeking and I have arranged for copies relating to 1980-81 and 1981-82 to be forwarded to her.
North West Cape Joint Communication Station
(Question No. 4883)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice. on 19 August 1982:
What additions and alterations to equipment and facilities at the North West Cape joint communications installations are proposed for (a) 1982-83 (b) 198384and(c) 198485.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The details set out below reflect current US and Australian planning intentions for the North West Cape naval communication station. They are subject to change due to budgetary and other considerations. For security reasons details of matters such as changes to cryptographic equipment are not included.
(a) 1982-83 (I) Replacement of an AN /TSC-54 satellite terminal
with an AN/GSC-39 terminal.
(2) Installation of a wide band secure voice terminal compatible with the AN /GSC-39 terminal.
(3) Installation of an additional teletype in the Australian national room.
(4) Replacement of land lines to Very Low Frequency (VLF) site.
(5) Replacement of Rhombic Antennae with a second Loop Rosette Antenna at the High Frequency (HF) receive site.
(b) 1983-84 (I) Installation of new station timekeeping system.
(2) Installation of narrow band secure voice equipment in the Australian national room.
(3) Replacement of voice frequency carrier telegraph (VFCT) equipment at the technical control and HF transmit sites.
(4) Construction of an addition to the maintenance shop.
(5) Construction of a vehicle maintenance shop.

Telephone Tapping: Airport Calls
(Question No. 4929)
Mr Scott asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 24 August 1982:
(I) Are any telephone calls made to, or from, any of Australia's airports tapped.
(2) If so, which airports are affected, and to what extent.
(3) Under whose authorisation and in what manner is this surveillance made.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I), (2) and (3) The interception of a telephone communication is illegal unless done pursuant to a warrant issued under the Telecommunications (Interception) Act 1979 to the Australian Security Intelligence Organization or the Australian Federal Police.
That Act provides that, in certain circumstances related to security, the Attorney-General may authorise the Australian Security Intelligence Organization to intercept telecommunications. it also provides that a judge (as defined) may authorise the Australian Federal Police to intercept telecommunications in certain circumstances relating to the investigation of narcotic offences.
It is not the practice of the Government to provide information concerning telephone interceptions.
Accountancy Profession
(Question No. 4981 )
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 26 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the paper entitled 'The Accountancy Profession: Supply, Demand, Pricing and Manpower Planning' written by J. Nieuwenhysen and M. Williams Wynn for the Committee for Economic Development of Australia, published in December 1981.
(2) Does the registration and membership of Australia's 2 accounting bodies give them a monopoly control over the profession.
(3) Is he able to say whet her the report is causing concern about the efficiency of Australia's labour markets in general and professional services in particular.
(4) Has he caused any investigations into (a) the accounting profession, (b) fees for services, (c) demand for accountancy services and (d) the supply of accountants.
(5) What control can the Government exercise in the accountancy profession.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) to (5) Control of the accounting profession is a matter for the States. The Commonwealth has an involvement through the Ministerial Council for Companies and Securities

in relation to the registration of auditors and liquidators under the Companies Act 1981 and associated State companies codes.

National Takeovers Code: Resolution of Disputes
(Question No. 5020)
Mr Jacobi asked the Acting Attorney-General, upon notice, on 8 September 1982:

3144 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

(I) Is the normal judicial process inappropriate for the authoritative and expeditious resolution of disputes which arise under the National Takeovers Code.
(2) Is it a fact that parties to a takeover litigation may have to wait for more than 6 months for the final resolution of a dispute by the High Court.
(3) Will the Attorney-General initiate urgent discussions within the Ministerial Council for Companies and Securities with a view to setting up an expert tribunal to deal effectively and expeditiously with all disputes arising under the Takeovers Code.
(4) Is it a fact that, because of the lengthy delays involved, the final appellate court, or for that matter any appellate court, might find it impracticable to grant the relief sought by a plaintiff in takeover litigation even though the plaintiff's legal arguments are accepted.
(5) Can the Attorney-General say whether parties to takeover litigation with very meritorious cases have been forced to abandon appeals from dubious single judge decisions because delays within the appellate court system appear to make it extraordinarily difficult for an appellate court to satisfactorily unscramble a takeover.
Mr N. A. BrownThe answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) to (5) While legislation governing takeovers of companies needs to take account of considerations such as those mentioned by the honourable member and to minimise delays which are avoidable, it has to be borne in mind that takeovers involve the rights of individuals and that they give rise to questions as to whether those rights have been breached. The resolution of such questions is properly a matter for the courts and the judicial process. I am not prepared to propose to the Ministerial Council for Companies and Securities that such questions be determined by an administrative body.
Speaker of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly: Issue of Writs
(Question No. 5064)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 15 September 1982:
(I) Why does he retain the right to issue a writ for the election of a new member to the ACT House of Assembly when a vacancy occurs.
(2) Why has the Speaker of the Assembly not been authorised to issue the writ in these circumstances.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Any change to the form of election to the House of Assembly would have implications for the future form of government of the ACT. As stated on numerous occasions, no change to the present form of government will be contemplated pending receipt of the report of the Grants Commission into the finances of the ACT.
(2) See my answer to (I) above.

Speaker of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly: Priority of Legislation for
Appointment
(Question No. 5068)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 15 September 1982:
Did the amendment to the ACT House of Assembly Ordinance, relating to the creation of the position of Speaker, take precedence over other legislation relating to the ACT; if so, why and what were the titles of the legislation.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The question of precedence did not arise. The amendments to the House of Assembly Ordinance creating the office of Speaker were relatively simple and straight forward, and so could be dealt with in the drafting process without any appreciable impact on the total drafting program. Therefore, there has been no change in priority in the drafting of other Ordinances for the Australian Capital Territory, and no issue of precedence had to be considered. When drafted, the Ordinance was made in the usual way, again not affecting progress of any other.

Australan Capital Territory Fire Brigade
(Question No. 5075)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 15 September 1982:
(I) What are the duties of the following officers of the ACT Fire Brigade (a) Fire Commissioner, (b) Deputy Fire Commissioner, (c) Superintendent and (d) District Officers.
(2) In the case of the Deputy Fire Commissioner, what duties were formerly performed by the (a) Commissioner and (b) Superintendent.
(3) What is the estimated cost of creating the additional position of Deputy Fire Commissioner.
(4) Was this position made necessary (a) by a Government decision to provide an additional political appointee to overcome the need for a career officer to act as Commissioner on occasions or (b) because the work load of the Commissioner and the Superintendent were excessive.
(5) Have requests for lifting of staff ceilings been received.
(6) Why is the salary of the Deputy Commissioner lower than that of the Superintendent.
(7) Did (a) the Commissioner or (b) the Superintendent request relief from an excessive work load.
(8) What staff will be assigned to the Deputy Commissioner.
(9) Will these personnel be in addition to the staff of the (a) Commissioner and (b) Superintendent.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The duties of the members are:
(a) Fire Commissioner: general control and management of the Fire Brigade.
(b) Deputy Commissioner: assist the Commissioner with the general control and management of the Fire Brigade.
(c) Superintendent: the duties of the Superintendent will be:
I. Oversight the work of the Fire Prevention, Firefighting and Service Maintenance Sections.
2. Evaluate new equipment and submit for ratification to Commissioner findings based on- (a) practicability, (b) cost analysis, (c) interchange with existing equipment and (d) involvement connected with training, stowage, etc.
3. Process returns relating to personnel that is Long Service Leave, Sick Leave, Compassionate Leave, etc.
4. Oversight station and roster changes for personnel.
5. As directed, liaise with various organisations on the implementation of policy established by the Commissioner.
6. Attend and assume command of fires and emergent happenings involving life risk and any other incident deemed necessary until the arrival of a more senior officer.
7. Oversight examinations.
8. Responsible to Commissioner for the welfare of personnel.
9. Prepare, subject to direction. reviews of projected needs for the Brigade.
10. If required, represent the Commissioner at conferences, seminars, functions, etc.

II. Determine, prior to final submittal to the Commissioner, the gravity of all discipline charges associated with all personnel employed by the Brigade.
12. Keep Commissioner fully informed of all fires, offences against discipline and the general function of the day to day operations of the Brigade.
(d) District Officer: while on duty shall:
I. Have charge of the District or Section which has been assigned.
2. Attend fires and take control until the arrival of a more senior officer.
3. Check and pass reports and requisitions for repairs and supplies.
4. Be responsible for the proper working of the assigned District or Section and the maintenance of strict discipline.
5. Perform all other duties required as a District Officer or which may be expressly assigned.
(2) The duties of the Commissioner are unchanged as above. The Superintendent's duties as outlined in I (c) are substantially the same.
The Deputy Commissioner will assist the Commissioner over the full range of the Commissioner's functions and it is expected the Deputy Commissioner will deputise for the Commissioner at more important conferences and meetings and assist with industrial relations matters.
(3) The cost of the position when staffed would be in the vicinity of $65,000 (includes salary and oncost charges). Other costs associated with the establishing of the additional position are insignificant.
(4) (a) No. (b) The position was necessary because of the workload of the position of Commissioner.
(5) No.
(6) In anticipation of the creation of the position of Deputy Commissioner, the Remuneration Tribunal determined a salary as at 7 June 1982. The Superintendent's salary was increased by the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission in a variation to the Fire Brigade Employees (ACT) Award 1975 handed down on I July 1982.
(7) The Commissioner requested a position of Deputy Commissioner because of the top management workload.
(8) No personal staff as such will be assigned to the Deputy Commissioner. However, as the Deputy of the Commissioner, the full resources of the Brigade will be available.
(9) Not applicable.

1\Iational Health Promotion Program
(Question No. 5100)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 16 September 1982:
(I) Is it a fact that no funds were allocated to the National Health Promotion Program in the 1982-83 Budget.
(2) Is it also a fact that only $140,000 was spent out of the $500.000 appropriated for the Program in 1981-82.
(3) If so. does this mean that the Government is abandoning its role in the field of health promotion despite the promise of the Prime Minister in his policy speech of 30 September 1980 and his speech to the ANU Public Affairs conference on Health Policy in which he referred to the promotion of individual health as the front line of health care; if not, what is the future Commonwealth role in this field.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) No. Funds for the National Health Promotion Program were frozen in February 1982 as part of a general restraint of expenditure across a wide range of Government programs and activities.
The Government is concerned about promoting the health of individuals and encouraging Australians to value good health as an asset which is worth achieving and maintaining. Just as health services are mainly provided by States so too health promotion is primarily the responsibility of States. This will not, however, preclude the Commonwealth from taking further initiatives in this most important side of health care as and when the appropriate time and need arises.

Australian Capital Territory Electricity Authority: Connection of Solar Hot Water
Systems to Off-Peak Supply
(Question No. 5115)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 21 September 1982:
(I) Has he received representations protesting against the ACT Electricity Authority's ACTEA decision to connect solar hot water systems compulsorily only to Off-Peak supply.
(2) Has he examined the report of the ACT House of Assembly Standing Committee on City Management in which the committee criticises this decision and draws attention to the large number of units that have not been tested by the ACTEA.
(3) Why was the decision to restrict solar hot water services only to Off-Peak supply made.
(4) Why does this restriction not apply to other systems connected to Off-Peak supply.
(5) Has he made any response to the findings of the committee; if so. what was the reponse.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows.
(I) Yes.
(2) The report by the ACT House of Assembly's Standing Committee on City Management, which contains a number of recommendations, was presented in the Assembly on 25 October 1982 and has now been referred to me.
I am currently studying the contents of the report.
(3) This decision was made in the interest of a fair distribution of costs across the whole spectrum of electricity consumers.

3148 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

The Authority is billed for bulk supply of electricity on the basis of both the energy consumed and the highest demand recorded during the month. In addition, the Authority must provide plant capacity equal to or greater than the highest consumer demand likely to occur within the ACT on the coldest day.
Except for a small number of very large customers who are billed for both energy and demand, the average consumer is billed only for the energy used. Therefore the tariff charged must be structured so that costs associated with the demand generated and the plant installed are recovered as well as the energy consumed.
Charges for electricity are constructed on the basis of a reasonable expectation that electricity will be consumed both during and outside peak demand times. While non-solar hot water heaters add significantly to peak demand, they operate ouside peak hours often enough for the Authority to recover costs.
Solar Water Heaters, on the other hand, while adding to the peak demand, do not require electric boosting during daylight hours. This, as a consequence, denies the Authority a significant amount of potential revenue and requires the shortfall to be subsidised by all other classes of consumers in the ACT. The Authority, therefore, considers that connection to Off-Peak supply is the fairest method of ensuring that Solar Systems 'pay their way'.
The Authority believes very strongly in energy conservation and applauds the Solar Heating Industry for its resourcefulness and dedication. However, the cost of providing standby boosting for Solar Water Heaters should not be borne by those not fortunate enough to afford the capital cost of their own Solar System.
(4) The restriction does apply to other systems connected to off-peak supply. The ACTEA Schedule of Charges provides that with Off-Peak Water Heating Charge 'booster elements are not permitted'.
(5) See my answer to question (2).
Appropriation for Marine and General Services
(Question No. 5125)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport and Construction, upon notice, on 22 September 1982:
In relation to item 972:4:03 of Appropriation Bill (No.2) 1982-83 concerning marine and general services, (a) what estimate can be provided of the real change in the provision made for this item in 1982-83 compared to 1981-82 and (b) what are the reasons for this change.
Mr Hunt~ The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) and (b) The expenditure during 1981-82 was $4,847,000 and the estimated expenditure for 1982-83 is $4,740,000. This means that there is an estimated marginal decrease in expenditure for 1982-83. Assuming that there will be an element of inflation during 1982-83, the estimated marginal decrease represents a decrease in real terms. It is not possible however to provide an estimate of the real change in the provision made in 1982-83 compared to 1981-82, mainly because this capital expenditure largely results from ongoing programmes with cash flows varying from year to year depending on manufacturing schedules amongst other things. For example the installation of light vessels in Bass Strait will continue in 1982-83 whereas bulk cash payments against this project were made in 1981-82.

Withdrawal of Australian Battalion from Singapore in 1974
(Question No. 5162)
Mr Morrison asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 23 September 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the address by the Prime Minister to the National RSL Congress on 15 September 1982 in which he said that the decision to withdraw the Australian battalion from Singapore in 1974 was one of the great mistakes in Australian defence decisions.
(2) Why has this alleged mistake not been rectified previously.
(3) Does he propose to deploy a similar sized unit to Singapore. if not. why not.
(4) Has he had discussions with the governments of either Malaysia or Singapore for a deployment of this kind; if so. what has been their response.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The Government while in opposition made clear its intention to discuss with its Five Power partners the question of regional defence co-operation. In view of the lapse of time between the withdrawal of the main body of the battalion at the end of 1973 and the return to office of the present Government in 1976. and the substantial re-arrangements and accommodations that were forced on the Singapore Government and our other partners by the withdrawal of the battalion. the Government has sought other practical means to demonstrate our willingness to co-operate with neighbouring countries to enhance the security of the region. Examples include:
A revitalisation of the Five Power Defence Arrangements.
An Expansion of Defence Co-operation programs.
An increase in combined exercises with our South East Asian neighbours.
Provision of access by Singapore to training areas in Australia.
(3) and (4) See (2) above.

Trade Practices Commission: Consumer Protection Inyestigations
(Question No. 5432)
Mr Hayden asked the Acting Attorney General, upon notice, on 20 October 1982:
(I) Did the Trade Practices Commission. in its 1981-82 Annual Report. advise that one effect of the Ministerial Direction of 25 May 1981 has been that the Commission is unable to take the first initiative in investigations in relation to consumer protection.
(2) Did the Commission also advise that the very tight control of investigations has meant that some matters have necessarily come to the Commission inadequately developed and with too little information.

(3) Has the Government provided the Commission with any additional resources to enable it to (a) undertake investigations at its own initiative and (b) ensure that investigations are fully and adequately prepared for the Commission.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The Trade Practices Commission advised that until recently the tight control, by which staff needed the Commission's approval for each investigation, and also the need to institute cases within twelve months, meant that some matters could not be taken up with the companies concerned so that they came to the Commission inadequately developed and with too little information. The TPC has now taken administrative steps to improve this situation by delegating to senior staff the power to seek initial comments from the company complained of.
(3) The Government keeps under notice both the impact of the Direction and the level of staffing in the Commission to ensure that it is able to carry out its responsibilities under the Trade Practices Act. Additional resources have not been required because:
(a) The Ministerial Direction of 25 May 1981 required the Trade Practices Commission to confine its consumer protection investigations to matters raising issues of importance at the national level and where such matters are brought to its attention by another person. This Direction accords with the Government's decisions announced by the Prime Minister on the Review of Commonwealth Functions.
(b) As noted above, the Commission has now taken administrative steps to speed up the development of possible cases.
Royal Australian Nayy: Recruitment of British Officers
(Question No. 5438)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 20 October 1982:
(I) Has the Royal Australian Navy sent a recruiting team to Britain to seek Royal Navy officers for the RAN.
(2) If so, how many officers are being sought.
(3) What technical or special requirements are needed that cannot be found amongst Australian naval personnel.
(4) Why are non-commissioned ranks in the RAN denied the opportunity for promotion to fill these positions.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Two RAN officers have been sent to the United Kingdom to expedite and streamline procedures for the induction into the RAN of former Royal Navy officers who have already applied to transfer to the RAN. These two officers are not a recruiting team and they have been directed not to undertake any active recruiting. The requirement for their visit to the UK arose because of the inability of the Australian High Commission to process ex-RN officer entrants to the RAN following recent staffing reductions.
(2) The RAN is not actively seeking RN or ex-RN officer entrants. There are at present 43 RN or ex-RN officers who have been given conditional approval to enter the RAN and unsolicited applications continue to be received.
(3) RN or ex-RN officers being inducted into the RAN are principally engineers or maritime warfare specialists of the requisite rank and experience required for unfilled billets in

3154 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

the RAN. There is a world wide shortage of engineers and this is in part responsible for the RAN shortages. Appropriately trained and experienced naval specialists are not available from within the RAN's own ranks.
(4) Non commissioned ranks in the RAN are not being denied any opportunity for promotion because of these measures. Initial offers of service to ex-RN officers guarantee neither permanency of employment nor promotion and with very few exceptions, these ex-RN officers serve in the RAN for limited periods and without career prospects. The RAN is continually striving to enhance the career prospects for both eligible non commissioned ranks and its junior officers but such is the complexity of modern maritime warfare that it is not possible to by-pass either essential training or experience during career progression if Australia's navy is to remain a credible fighting force with its officers demonstrating professional competence.

Royal Australian Navy Fleet Air Arm
(Question No, 5442)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 20 October 1982:
(I) Have personnel in the RAN Fleet Air Arm been notified that their services will not be necessary after the completion of their present period of service.
(2) Where personnel have not qualified for Defence Force Retirement and Death Benefits retirement what will be the financial terms applied to their retrenchments.
(3) Why are these officers not capable of filling vacancies occurring in other sections of the RAN.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Personnel in the Fleet Air Arm have not been notified that their services will not be necessary after their present

period of service. Re-engagements for the Fleet Air Arm sailors are still being processed and approved. However, until the future of the Fleet Air Arm has been resolved, most Fleet Air Arm sailors are being offered re-engagements of two years ata time.
(2) There is no plan to retrench members of the Fleet Air Arm and therefore no financial policy for retrenchment.
(3) Many Fleet Air Arm officers are capable of tilling officer vacancies in non aviation sections of the RAN but, until the future of fixed wing aviation in the Fleet Air Arm is resolved, the vast majority remain posted to aviation billets. Officer posting plans to cover all contingencies are nearing completion and will be finalised after the Government's decision on a replacement carrier to Melbourne has been taken.
Western Australia Community Law Centres
(Question No. 5448)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 21 October 1982:
(I) Are any community law centres to receive Commonwealth funding in Western Australia; if so, from what source will the funds be received.
(2) Which centres are to receive funds.
(3) On what criteria will the funding be based.
(4) What initiatives have been undertaken by the Government to encourge the Western Australian State Government to provide funding for community law centres.
(5) What steps has the Attorney-General taken to ensure that community law centres are represented on the Commonwealth Legal Aid Council.
(6) Has the Attorney-Genera!'s attention been drawn to the fact that despite extensive patronage the Sussex Street Community Law Service in East Victoria Park, WA, has not received State or Federal assistance to date.
(7) Will the Attorney-General give consideration to providing financial assistance to this service.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) It is expected that some community law centres in Western Australia will receive funds from the Commonwealth in 1982-83. The funds will be provided under the Commonwealth's ongoing program of provision of grants to supplement voluntary and community legal aid schemes.
(2) The provision of grants to particular centres is the subject of correspondence with the Legal Aid Commission of Western Australia.
(3) The evaluation of the Commission's recommendations will include consideration of the criteria upon which the recommendations are based. It is the Commission's role to propose criteria in the first instance.
(4) The Commonwealth will be advising the State Government of Western Australia concerning the Commonwealth allocation of funds to community law centres. It is hoped that the State will also fund community law centres.
(5) The Attorney-General has appointed a person familiar with the work of community law centres as one of his representatives on the Commonwealth Legal Aid Council.
(6) Yes.
(7) This Service will be considered along with other applicant bodies in Western Australia for the provision of financial assistance.

Polychlorinated Biphenyls: Use by Department of the Capital Territory
(Question No. 5547)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 27 October 1982:
(I) What materials and equipment currently in use in the Minister's Department or bodies under the Minister's control are known to contain polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs).
(2) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the dangers associated with the use of PCBs.
(3) What (a) statutory and (b) advisory conditions exist for the purpose of monitoring all introduction, use, transport and disposal of PCBs, and what arrangements are currently in existence for policing statutory conditions.
(4) What methods of storage and disposal are used for materials and equipment containing PCBs when those items become redundant or useless.
(5) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to any contamination of the environment by PCBs which may be wholly or partly due to the use, etc., of PCBs by the Minister's Department or bodies under the Minister's control; if so, what are the details.
(6) Has the Minister taken any steps to ensure that all employees under the Minister's control are fully aware of the dangers of PCBs; if so, what are the details; if not. will the Minister now do so; if the Minister will not do so, what is the reason.
Mr Hodgman-The answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:
(I) I am informed that no PCBs are in use or storage in the Department of the Capital Territory, nor is there any equipment containing PCBs.
(2) Yes.
(3) None exist for either (a) or (b).
(4) Not applicable see (I) above.
(5) No, see (I) above.
(6) No, it is not considered necessary. See (I) above.
Polychlorinated Biphenyls: Use by Department of Veterans' Affairs
(Question No. 5548)
Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 27 October 1982:
(I) What materials and equipment currently in use in the Minister's Department or bodies under the Minister's control are known to contain polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs).
(2) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the dangers associated with the use of PCBs.
(3) What (a) statutory and (b) advisory conditions exist for the purpose of monitoring all introduction, use, transport and disposal of PCBs, and what arrangements are currently in existence for policing statutory conditions.
(4) What methods of storage and disposal are used for materials and equipment containing PCBs when those items become redundant or useless.
(5) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to any contamination of the environment by PCBs which may be wholly or partly due to the use, etc., of PCBs by the Minister's Department or bodies under the Minister's control; if so, what are the details.

(6) Has the Minister taken any steps to ensure that all employees under the Minister's control are fully aware of the dangers of PCBs; if so, what are the details; if not, will the Minister now do so; if the Minister will not do so, what is the reason.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The only equipment in use within the Department known to contain PCBs are two transformers installed at Repatriation General Hospital, Hobart, which are maintained by the Department of Transport and Construction.
(2) Yes.
(3) Customs (Prohibited Imports) Regulations prohibit the importation of polychlorinated biphenyls, terphenyls and other polyphenyls unless the permission of the Minister for Industry and Commerce has been granted. This regulation has been in force since 1973. Health implications of the use of PCBs in Australia have been kept under surveillance by the Commonwealth Department of Health and the National Health and Medical Research Council for many years. It is also relevant that, in connection with its involvement with the Australian Environment Council, the Department of Home Affairs and Environment has reviewed the use of PCBs in Australia and has liaised with industry to encourage the phasing out of PCB use. Regular inspections are carried out by the Department of Transport and Construction as a precautionary measure against possible leaks from the transformers.
(4) There are no immediate plans to replace PCBs due to problems of disposal within Australia.
(5) No.
(6) All staff who are required to work in the area where PCBs are used have been informed of the hazards and correct methods of safely handling PCBs.

Beyerley Uranium Project
(Question No. 5584)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 28 October 1982:
(I) Is it a fact that his Department considers that the Draft Environmental Impact Statement (EIS) for the Beverley uranium project prepared by the South Australian Uranium Corporation fails to meet the requirements of the administrative procedures of the Environment Protection (Impact of Proposals) Act and is unacceptable.
(2) If so, in what respects does his Department consider the Draft EIS to be of an unacceptable standard.
(3) In particular, does his Department consider that the Draft EIS (a) provides inadequate technical detail (b) contains inconsistencies, particularly with respect to groundwater movement data, (c) contains highly questionable interpretation of the geology of the orebody and its surrounds and (d) betrays a lack of expertise in regard to the proposed in-situ leaching method and the geohydrology of the area.
(4) Will he reject the Draft EIS; if not, what action will he take.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No.
(2) See (I) above.
(3) (a) to (d) Comments received on the Beverley draft EIS from the public and from State and Commonwealth authorities identified a number of deficiencies in the draft ElS, including inconsistencies in the geology. geohydrology and groundwater movement section. The proponent company is currently preparing a final ElS for submission to the Department of Home Affairs and Environment and the South Australian Government. The Administrative Procedures under the Environment Protection (Impact of Proposals) Act require the proponent to take into account comment received during public review in preparing a final ElS.
(4) No. The Department of Home Affairs and Environment will examine the final EIS, as part of the Department's assessment of this proposal, to ensure that appropriate responses have been made to public comment and that deficiencies have been corrected.
