Payments to Aboriginal Students
(Question No. 4442)
Dr EYeringham asked the Minister representing the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 22 April 1982:
Is the Minister able to provide details of (a) the average payment to the student and parents by (i) the Commonwealth and (ii) each State and Territory Government in respect of each Aboriginal student over the past 10 years for (A) preschool, (B) primary, (C) secondary, and (D) tertiary students and (b) how these averages compare with the a,;erage payment in respect of other students in Australia.
Mr Fife-The Minister for Education has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The only available information concerns Commonwealth schemes of assistance to students, including schemes designed specifically for Aboriginals. The following tables provide in relation to each assistance scheme (i) its availability; (ii) the main benefits applicable for 1982 (these will be substantially higher in 1983 as a result of the decisions taken in relation to levels of student assistance in the 1982-83 Budget), (iii) numbers of students assisted and expenditure for each year since 1972 (or the inception of the scheme whichever is the later). Some Aboriginal students may be included in the figures for those schemes which are not specifically intended for Aboriginals-this certainly would be so in the case of the Assistance for Isolated Children Scheme (AlC).
The tables do not show average benefits per student. For each of the Commonwealth schemes, an average figure obtained by applying student numbers to overall expenditure would not serve as a basis for valid comparsion because of the variety of schemes involved and considerations such as discontinued study, provision for part-time study (for example, under the Aboriginal Study Grants Scheme) and other factors.

Allowances also provided for dependent spouse and children, as well as an incidentals allowance and assistance with travel.

Education: Discipline
(Question No. 4681)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister representing the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the 1982 direction of the Government of Eire, which followed the example of most European Governments, that, in National Schools, (a) teachers should have a lively regard for the improvement of their pupils, treat them with kindness combined with firmness and should aim at governing them through their affections and reason, and not by harshness and severity, (b) ridicule, sarcasm or remarks likely to undermine a pupil's selfconfidence should not be used in any circumstances, (c) the use of corporal punshiment should be forbidden, and (d) any teacher who contravened these rules would be regarded as guilty of conduct unbefitting a teacher and subject to severe disciplinary action.
(2) Has the Minister's attention also been drawn to the reports of spectacular success in the use of self-discipline by teachers in overcoming problems of difficult pupils in Education for Tomorrow's World. by Henden, Forland and Fraenki, published in 1970 by Grosvenor Books, London.
(3) Will Australia follow Ireland's example; if so, when.
Mr Fife~The Minister for Education has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(J) Yes.
(2) Yes.

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3159

(3) As my predecessor indicated in his reply to Question No. 3954 from the honourable member earlier this year, the Commonwealth's direct involvement in the administration of schools is limited to the Australian Capital Territory. The ACT Schools Authority has urged all ACT government schools to work towards the abolition of physical punishment.
ACT Government schools each have a school board which determines educational policy and philosophy for the school, operating within the general directions set by the ACT Schools Authority.
Schools in the ACT generally wish to be known for their caring relationships, where the teachers are aware of children's needs and guide and promote the whole development of each child, where the teacher's respect for the child as a person cultivates in him or her the desire to learn, and where caring and respect is mutual between all members of the school community.
As far as government schools in the States and nongovernment schools are concerned these are matters for the respective State and non-government school authorities to determine. However, I believe that in all schools, teachers who take a professional approach to their duties would adhere to humanitarian principles.

(Question No, 4697)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:

Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (i) Baulkham Hills-- Urban, 4.1293 Hectares; Rural, Nil.
(ii) Blacktown --Urban, 12.0355 Hectares; Rural, 3,192.7283 Hectares.
(iii) Fairfield--Urban, 22.0880 Hectares; Rural, Nil.
(iv) Hawkesbury--Urban, 9.1068 Hectares; Rural, 105.4812 Hectares.
(v) HolroydUrban, 3.2278 Hectares; Rural, 62.9518 Hectares.
(vi) Liverpool--Urban, 621.0338 Hectares; Rural, 7,608.9901 Hectares.
(vii) Penrith-Urban, 114.5177 Hectares; Rural, 2,195.0285 Hectares.
The above mentioned does not include properties owned by authorities exempt from the Lands Acquisition Act; this information is not retained by my Department.
(2) Section 114 of the Constitution exempts the Commonwealth from the payment of rates on land used for Commonwealth purposes, or on vacant land held by the Commonwealth. However, payments in lieu of rates are made to State local authorities where circumstances exist such as the revenue leasing of property, or its exclusive use for domestic purposes.

Independent Audit: Department of Transport and Construction
(Question No. 4784)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister for Transport and Construction, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
As a condition of payments in the nature of grants/contributions from my Department, non-government organisations are required to submit periodic financial statements certified by independent auditors who are members of the recognised accountancy bodies.

$150,000
COMMITTEE FOR THE ALLOCAnON OF LOAN FUNDS TO REFUGEES IN CENTRES (CALFRIC)
Under the CALFRIC loan scheme, funds from my Department's appropriation are allocated to a fund operated by the Committee for the Allocation of Loan Funds to Refugees in Centres (CALFRIC). This Committee makes small, interestfree loans (up to $600) to refugees, to assist them with the expenses of moving out of migrant centres into the community. Money from repayments on loans is recycled via the CALFRIC fund and lent to other refugees. The Committee comprises representatives of the following voluntary agencies:
The Salvation Army Austcare Indo China Refugee Association (Australia) The Saint Vincent de Paul Society Australian Council of Churches Australian Council of Social Services;
as well as of Commonwealth Accommodation and Catering Services and of my Department.
In 1981-82, the Government allocated a further $200,000 to the CALFRIC loan fund bringing to $1.213m the total Commonwealth contribution.
My Department also makes payments via the Clothing Reimbursement Scheme to reimburse voluntary agencies for the cost of providing essential clothing and personal requisites to refugees who lack these items when they arrive in Australia. A detailed breakdown of all accounts submitted for payment under this scheme in 1981-82 would require an examination of every single claim, and would make demands of staffing resources which I am not prepared to authorise. The major agencies participating in the scheme were:
Saint Vincent de Paul Society The Salvation Army Red Cross The Smith Family Seventh Day Adventist Church Hobart City Mission.
In addition to those agencies, however, small community groups participating in the Community Refugee Settlement Scheme are eligible to claim reimbursement for clothing provided to refugees who bypass migrant centres and go straight into the community under that scheme; details of those groups are not readily available.
A total of $199,850 was paid to voluntary agencies under this scheme in 1981-82.

Answers to Questions

Immigration: Business Migration Category
(Question No. 4840)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to an article on page 21 of the Sydney Morning Herald of 23 June 1982 concerning Tan Sri Khoo Teck Puat.
(2) How many applications for permanent residence under the Business Migration category have been approved each year since the inception of this scheme.
(3) Was Tan Sri Khoo Teck Puat granted entry to Australia under this scheme.
(4) Can foreign nationals purchase permanent residence under this program by purchasing Australian companies or businesses.
(5) Has his attention been drawn to the inequity of such a scheme.
(6) How many permanent resident visa holders travelled overseas and returned to Australia last year.
(7) What is the minimum period of residence required in Australia each year to return with permanent residence status.
(8) Are some permanent residence visa holders only holidaying in Australia each year to retain their permanent resident status while conducting their normal lives and business in other countries.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The Business Migration Program commenced on 19 April 1982. It superseded the previous entrepreneur migration category which had been in operation since November 1976. Arrivals of entrepreneurial migrants and business migrants in the last three financial years are as follows:
1979-80-78 1980-81-- 106 198182-263
(3) Yes. Tan Sri Khoo Teck Puat was approved under the entrepreneur migration category in 1981.
(4) and (5) No. People selected under the former entrepreneurial category and the Business Migration Program are expected to settle in Australia and to satisfy normal migration requirements. The business proposal submitted by the applicant and the likely benefits of the proposal to Australia form important parts of consideration of the application.
(6) During 1981, 221,473 persons with resident status departed. Of these 10,719 were long term residents (i.e. one year or more) and the remaining 212,754 were short term residents (i.e. less than one year).
For the same period, 212,765 persons with resident status returned to Australia. This figure comprised 15,303 long term residents and 197,460 short term residents.
It is not possible to extract from these figures the number of persons who may have departed and returned during the course of the year. The totals would also include persons who may have departed and returned more than once.
Separate statistics are not maintained on resident businessmen who travel in and out of Australia.
(7) Person~ with resident status in Australia under the Migration Program may remain overseas for periods up t9, but not normally exceeding:

three years where the person has lived in Australia for at least 12 months.
one year where the person has lived in Australia for less than 12 months.
(8) Business migrants are expected to settle permanently in Australia. However. some businessmen need to visit other countries in connection with their business affairs. In general, reasons for leaving Australia must be consistent with the principle that there is a genuine intention to settle in Australia.
Review of Migrant Services and Programs
(Question No. 4930)
Mr Young asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 25 August 1982:
(I) Were all 89 recommendations and 55 propositions of the recent evaluation of the Review of Migrant Services and Programs (Galbally Report) by the Australian Institute of Multicultural Affairs accepted by the Government.
(2) Did the Government reject recommendation 68 relating to an aspect of the Criminal Investigation Bill 1981; if so. Why.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No. The Government's response to each recommendation and to the selected proposals which required Government endorsement is contained in a Summary of Government decisions document which has been widely distributed to ethnic organisations, State and Federal bodies, relevant trade unions and all members of this Parliament.
(2) The Criminal Investigation Bill 1981 properly falls within the jurisdiction of the Attorney-General. I am advised that recommendation 68 was considered by the Attorney General as part of an extensive review of the Criminal Investigation Bill 1981 undertaken in the light of a considerable number of submissions on the Bill which he has received. The outcome of this review will be made known as soon as possible.
Adult Migrant Education Service: Employment Conditions of Casual Teachers
(Question No. 4992)
Mr Morrison asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 26 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a statement by the Industrial Commissioner of NSW, issued on 18 August 1982, strongly critical of the conditions under which casual full-time teachers in the Adult Migrant Education Service are employed compared to the 77 permanent teachers in the service.
(2) Has his attention also been drawn to a statement by Mr Justice Bauer that (a) the evidence is that the casual fUll-time teachers do the same work and participate in teaching education preparation and developments in the various centres and for all intents and purposes do the same work as if they were permanent employees and (b) it might reasonably be said that the conditions of employment result in the casual, fUll-time teachers subsidising the Adult Migrant Education Scheme.
(3) If so will he respond to the views expressed in the Industrial Commission by releasing the funds necessary to make these casual teaching positions permanent.

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3163

Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I), (2) and (3) I have read the statement of Mr Justice Bauer of the NSW Industrial Commission which refers to the conditions of casual teachers employed for 20 hours per week in the NSW Adult Migrant Education Service.
I have noted that the transcript of the hearing cites instances of casual teachers undertaking activities which extend the time that they spend at the Adult Migrant Education Centre beyond 20 hours per week. The responsibilities of these casual teachers has been compared by Mr Justice Bauer with the permanent teachers who are on duty for 30 hours per week.
To ensure an adequate degree of flexibility in the size and distribution of the Adult Migrant Education Service to match the changing composition and needs of the non-English speaking migrant intake, it is desirable to have a balance of perma. nent and casual teachers. As at 15 September 1982, there were 115 permanent teacher positions in the NSW Adult Migrant Education Service. In response to approaches from the NSW Minister for Education, I have agreed to provide funding for the conversion of an additional 30 positions from casual to permanent.
Within the current appropriations further funds cannot be provided to convert casual positions to permanent ones without reducing the total number of hours of English language instruction available to migrants, or other important services. I have proposed adjustments to the teaching arrangements to make it possible to convert further positions from casual to permanent.
At the same time. I have undertaken to examine closely the basis for future funding of permanent teacher positions with

particular attention to the appropriate proportion of permanent teachers, given the need for a mix of permanent and casual staff. This is a matter which has national implications and I have indicated to the NSW Minister for Education that the Commonwealth will need to take account of these national implications in responding further to approaches from NSW.

Appropriation for Free or Concessional Railway Fares and Freight
(Question No. 5128)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport and Construction, upon notice, on 22 September 1982:
In relation to item 644:3:04 of Appropriation Bill (No. I) 1982-83 concerning free or concessional railway fares or freight, what estimate can be provided of the real change in the provision made for this item in 1982-83 compared to 1981-82 and what are the reasons for the reduction in the real allocation.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
$2.3m was appropriated for free or concessional railway fares and freight for 1981-82 including a once only carry over amount of $0.22m from 1980-81. This carry over was brought about by a change in Australian National Railway Commission's accounting methods since July 1981, from retrospective to current claims.
The actual expenditure for 1981-82 was $2.08m. The allocation for 1982-83 is $2.2m. After applying the Gross Domestic Product Implicit Price Deflator of 10.3 per cent, there is only a 4.1 per cent decrease in real terms from the 1981-82 appropriation. In the light of forecast economic circumstances it was felt that likely travel demand would not be high and that the funds provided would meet the requirement.

Residents of the Electoral Diyision of Lowe: Applications For Citizenship
(Question No. 5208)
Mr Maher asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 22 September 1982:
Why are some residents of the Electoral Division of Lowe, when applying for citizenship, referred to the departmental office at Parramatta, while other residents are referred to the office in Chifley Square, Sydney.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Applications for Australian citizenship from residents of the Electoral Division of Lowe are normally dealt with at the office of the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs in Chifley Square, Sydney. However, if the applicant requests that the application be dealt with by the Parramatta office because it is more convenient to his/her workplace, then this is arranged.

Median Family Income: Increases
(Question No. 5229)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 12 October 1982:
By what percentage did median family income increase in Australia (a) between 1950 and 1959, (b) between 1960 and 1969, (c) between 1970 and 1979, and (d) from 1980 to date.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Estimates of median family income in Australia are available only in respect of the financial years 1968-69, 1973-74 and 1978-79.
The 1973-74 estimate of median family income was 76 per cent higher than in 1968-69 and the median family income for 1978-79 was 90 per cent higher than in 1973-74.

Housing Assistance
(Question No, 5360)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 19 October 1982:
(I) Was $20m promised by the Government in its March housing assistance package to assist rent and mortgage payers in genuine distress.
(2) Was this decision announced again in the August Budget statement by the Treasurer as the Mortgage and Rent Relief Program; if so, why.
(3) Is it a fact that, at the time this question was submitted, there has been a 7 month delay in implementing the program despite it originally being intended to help people in distress in March 1982.
(4) Is the program dependent on State Government cooperation; if so, what correspondence did the Government enter into with the States immediately after the March announcement to secure co-operation.
(5) What are the details of the correspondence between the Commonwealth and the Queensland Government.
(6) What assurance can he give that mortgage and rent payers in genuine distress in March 1982 have not been forgotten.

3170 REPRESENTATlVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

Mr Wilson--The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes, for the purpose of appropriation. Appropriation Bill No.2 shows the sum of $20m as grants by the Commonwealth to the States and Territories for the Mortgage and Rent Relief Scheme.
(3) On 18 March 1982, it was announced that discussions would be held with the States, Northern Territory and ACT on introduction from 1982-83 of a joint Commonwealth and State Mortgage and Rent Relief Scheme. These discussions commenced at the Commonwealth and State Housing Ministers' Conference in Melbourne on 26 March. Commonwealth and State officials then met to discuss the development of the program.
As a result of these discussions a formal proposal for the scheme was developed and this formed the basis of the formal offer made to the States on 27 August 1982.
The States and Territories are responsible for the administration of the scheme, including the form of assistance to be provided and the date of commencement. The scheme is now operating in most States/Territories.
(4) Yes, the former Minister for Housing and Construction, the Hon. D. T. McVeigh, wrote to each State/Territory Housing Minister on 22 March 1982. This letter proposed the commencement of discussions between the Commonwealth and States and Territories at the Commonwealth and State Housing Ministers' meeting in Melbourne on 26 March.
(5) As indicated, the former Minister for Housing and Construction wrote to the Queensland Minister for Works and Housing, the Hon. C. Wharton, MLA, on 22 March 1982 and I wrote to him making a formal offer on the scheme on 27 August 1982.
In his response to the offer on 8 September, Mr Wharton advised that it would be necessary for him to refer the matter to the Queensland Cabinet for consideration before he could formally respond. At the time of preparation of this answer a formal reply had not been received.
(6) As noted above, a formal olTer to the States and Territories has been made, and the scheme is already operating in most States and Territories.

Polychlorinated Biphenyls: Use by Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs
(Question No. 5552)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 27 October 1982:
(I) What materials and equipment currently in use in the Minister's Department or bodies under the Minister's control are known to contain polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs).
(2) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the dangers associated with the use of PCBs.
(3) What (a) statutory and (b) advisory conditions exist for the purpose of monitoring all introduction, use, transport and disposal of PCBs, and what arrangements are currently in existence for policing statutory conditions.
(4) What methods of storage and disposal are used for materials and equipment containing PCBs when those items become redundant or useless.
(5) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to any contamination of the environment by PCBs which may be wholly or partly due to the use, etc., of PCBs by the Minister's Department or bodies under the Minister's control; if so, what are the details.
(6) Has the Minister taken any steps to ensure that all employees under the Minister's control are fully aware of the dangers of PCBs; if so, what are the details; if not, will the Minister now do so; if the Minister will not do so, what is the reason.

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3171

Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) As stated in the reply to question No. 1997 (Senate Hansard 12 October 1982, page 1308) a check of Departmental equipment and materials has not revealed any items containing PCB's.
I am unable to state that electrical components in general office equipment such as copiers do not contain PCB's due to the lack of identification of the constitutent nature of machine components. However I understand that if PCB's are present in this situation the components are sealed and pose little threat to humans or the environment.
(2) Yes.
(3) (a) None (b) Officers involved in purchasing have been made aware of the dangers of PCB's. The Department does not purchase equipment known to contain PCB's.
(4) Not applicable.
(5) No.
(6) No formal notification as my Department, to its knowledge, does not use PCB's. However a general notice has been included in the Staff Bulletin for information.
Immigration Status
(Question No. 5565)
Mr Coleman asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 28 October 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the article by Mr Phillip Adams in the Bulletin of 21 September, 1982 concerning the immigration status of Mr Michael Friedrich and his family?
(2) If so, have the allegations made in the article been investigated.
(3) What action has been taken on Mr Friedrich's application for permanent resident status?
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) I asked my Department for a detailed report so that I could thoroughly review Mr Friedrich's case.
The facts concerning Mr Friedrich are as follows: Mr Friedrich and his family arrived as visitors on 2 October 1981. Four days later in direct contravention of the undertakings they signed when they applied for their visitors visas, the Friedrich's sought permanent residence.
Mr Friedrich argued that because he had helped people escape from East Germany his life was in danger in his home country of West Germany.
In response to this my Department contacted the Australian Embassy in West Germany; the West German authorities confirmed that Mr Friedrich was an "escape helper' but that he was in no danger while he remained in West Germany.
The fact that Mr Friedrich returned to West Berlin after he had arrived in Australia confirms this.
At the time. I reviewed the case Mr Friedrich had not applied for refugee status in Australia. He subsequently did so through his solicitors. I am mindful of Australia's obligations under the Refugee Convention and the proud record this country has of accepting genuine refugees over past decades. I have asked the Determination of Refugee Status (DORS) Committee to examine his claim to be a refugee and report to me.

Mr Friedrich has been assessed in terms of Section 6A of the Migration Act (refugee status apart) and found not to be eligible for the grant of permanent residence under this provision.
I am satisfied that apart from the question of refugee status, which is soon to be decided. there is no justification for the Friedrich family to remain in Australia. I will advise the honourable member of my final decision once I have the report of the DORS Committee.
United Nations: Kampuchean Credentials
(Question No. 5567)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 28 October 1982:
In the vote on the credentials of the Kampuchean delegation to the current UN General Assembly is he able to give details of the motion and how each country voted.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
On 6 October 1982 the Credentials Committee of the Thirty-Seventh Session of the United Nations General Assembly reported that it had found the credentials of the delegation of Democratic Kampuchea, amongst other countries. in order, and recommended that the General Assembly adopt the following draft resolution:
The General Assembly Approves the first report of the Credentials Committee.
On 22 October the Lao People's Democratic Republic, together with seven other co-sponsors (Congo, Cuba, Democratic Yemen, Ethiopia, Grenada, India, Vietnam) introduced an amendment which would have added to the end of the operative paragraph of the draft resolution the phase "Except with regard to the credentials of Democratic Kampuchea'.
On 25 October the Lao amendment was defeated in the General Assembly by a vote of 29-90-26.

Missing Swedish Diplomat, Mr R. Wallenberg
(Question No. 5598)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 28 October 1982:
(I) Is he able to say whether Raoul Wallenberg was First Secretary of the Swedish Embassy in Budapest from July 1944 to approximately February 1945 and was described as the 'hero of the holocaust' for his work in saving Hungarian Jews during the Nazi occupation.
(2) Is it a fact that he was last seen in Hungary in or about February 1945 when he set off for an appointment with officers of the Red Army.
(3) Is it a fact that reports have been received in Sweden that he was being held in a Soviet prison since that time, that he was last sighted in the late 1970s and that, at the age of 70 years, he may still be alive.
(4) What requests for information has the Government made to the Soviet Government about Wallenberg's fate, and on what dates.
(5) What replies, if any, have been received, and on what dates.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes. Mr Wallenberg's heroism in saving the lives of large numbers of potential victims of Nazi persecutions in Hungary during World War II is well-known and has been widely acknowledged. It is correct that he performed these deeds while serving at the Swedish Embassy in Budapest.
(2) Yes.
(3) Yes. The Government is aware of reports that Mr Wallenberg is alive and that he has been seen in Soviet prisons. Sweden continues to pursue the Wallenberg case with the Soviet authorities, who so far have done no more than repeat their claim that Mr Wallenberg died in prison in 1947.
(4) and (5) The Australian Government fully supports the efforts of Sweden to have the case re-examined by the Soviet authorities. Because the Australian Government has no direct standing in the matter, however, there has been little opportunity for us to make an effective intervention. The matter is basically the concern of the Swedish and Soviet Governments and for the time being it is felt that Sweden is best placed to press for a more satisfactory response from the Soviet authorities. The Government is keeping in touch with developments, nonetheless, and remains willing to take whatever opportunities may arise, in multilateral forums particularly, to support Sweden's efforts.

Proprietary Companies
Mr N. A. Brown-On 23 September 1982 (Hansard, page 178) Mr Moore asked me, as the Acting Attorney-General, the following question without notice:
1 address my qu~stion to the Acting Attorney-General. He is no doubt aware that an enormous amount of commerce in Australia is carried out in proprietary companies. He would be aware also that to form a proprietary company it is necessary to have $2, two directors and two shareholders. In view of the wide-ranging use of proprietary companies, would he and the Government give consideration to ensuring that the capitalisation of these companies is such as to bear their responsibilities and that to do that may require some gearing within the companies' structure? Also, having determined that, would he like to pursue that matter with the ministerial council, where I am sure he would find some welcoming ears?
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Ministerial Council for Companies and Securities, at a meeting held in Sydney on 12 February 1982, considered a proposal that the Companies Act 1981 should require all companies to raise and maintain a prescribed minimum nominal and paid-up capital. At that meeting the Ministerial Council agreed that this proposal should be considered in the context of the proposed Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Bill 1984. I expect that this Bill will be made available for public comment in the second half of 1983.

Mr Holding asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 21 April 1982:

Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) The decision to undertake an independent review of the functions, structure and operation of the National Health and Medical Research Council (NH and MRC) was made by the Government in July 1981.
(3) and (5) The decision was taken in the context of the Government's budgetary considerations of operating costs of the NH and MRC. As such, it is inappropriate for the document to be tabled in Parliament.
(6) No. However, as the ASTEC Report on Medical Research in Australia would have been an important source document, the implementation of the review was deferred until the report was available.
(7) See (I) to (5) above.
(8) Dr B. J. Shea, OBE. (a) 22 January 1982: (b) 8 March 1982. Dr. J. P. Coghlan (a) 12 January 1982: (b) 3 March 1982.
(9) No.
(10) Dr Shea left Australia on 18 April 1982 and returned On 5 May 1982. This commitment was known prior to his appointment to the Review Committee, and did not affect the preparation and presentation of the Review Committee's report.

Sodium in Drinking Water
(Question No, 4717)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:

Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) Professor Trefor O. Morgan, Senior Specialist-inCharge (Medicine), Repatriation General Hospital, Heidelberg, Victoria (Chairman). Miss Maxine Hosking, DeputyChief Dietitian, Royal Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney. Dr Bruce A. Scoggins, Principal Research Fellow, Howard Florey Institute of Experimental Physiology and Medicine, Melbourne. Professor A. Stewart Truswell, Boden Professor of Human Nutrition, University of Sydney. Mrs Maureen Cornish, Nutritionist, Commonwealth Department of Health (Secretary /Convener).

(c) and (d) The Working Party has completed its report. As yet, the report has not been submitted to the secretariat of the National Health and Medical Research Council, but is with the Council's Public Health Advisory Committee (PHAC). (3) Yes. Reasons for these specific variations have not been identified. However the levels of dissolved solids and sodium can vary with the sources of supply and with local weather patterns, and changes over a 3, year period (the interval between the answers) are not unexpected. By way of example, in the A.CT. since early 1979, water from the Googong Dam has been used to augment supplies from dams on the Cotter River. Because water from the Googong Dam has levels of total dissolved solids and sodium marginally higher than water from the dams in the Colter catchment, A.CT. water now has a range of values (see answer to Question No. 4584).
Questions seeking additional information on particular supplies are best directed to the appropriate water authority.
(4) and (5) I am advised that the aspects mentioned were considered. However, all malters in the report must be considered by all the other relevant expert committees and their reports, in turn, examined by the Council before any recommendations are made by the NH and MRC
Major Airport Needs of Sydney Inquiry
(Question No. 4733)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Aviation, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) What financial contribution has been made by the Commonwealth Government to the inquiry into the Major Airport Needs of Sydney in each year since its establishment.
(2) Is he able to say whether any similar inquiries have been conducted in any other State during the last 10 years.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Major Airport Needs of Sydney (MANS) Study has involved work by a large number of bodies, both Commonwealth and State. Because of the shared nature of this work it is difficult to estimate the financial contribution made by the Commonwealth Government, but I can assure the honourable member that it has been significant both in terms of manpower resources and costs. Indeed, the Commonwealth has also employed a number of Consultants to undertake specialist tasks during the Study.
(2) There have been several inquiries concluded by joint Commonwealth-State committees in other States including the Port Phillip Airport Needs Study, the Joint Government Advisory Committee on Adelaide's Airline Airport Requirements and the Commonwealth/State Advisory Committee to study Western Australia's Airport Requirements. I should also point out that the new Brisbane Airport is the outcome of findings from a joint Commonwealth/State Advisory Committee set up in 1969 to advise on Brisbane's Airport needs.

United States Planes and Vessels Carrying Nuclear Weapons: B52 Aircraft Using Darwin
(Question No. 4880)
Mr Beazley asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to remarks by United States Deputy Secretary of State. Walter Stoessel, at a press conference after the ANZUS Council meeting in which he said (a) on the question of United States planes and vessels carrying nuclear weapons he recalled that the basic policy on information concerning nuclear weapons, whether in ships or aeroplanes, is neither to confirm nOr deny their presence and (b) on the question of consulting the Prime Minister On the question of nuclear weapons on ships that the US. would be prepared to consult with him on a very frank and very confidential basis, as appropriate between allies.
(2) Did he inform the House of 12 March 1981 that if the surveillance mission of B52 aircraft using Darwin were to be changed he or the Minister for Defence would advise the House at the time.
(3) Has he the guarantee of the US. administration that the very confidential basis of the information provided him on whether or not visiting warships carry nuclear weapons is not the basis on which he will be advised of changes in B52 missions.
(4) Is he to be informed of B52 mission changes on a very confidential basis; if so, is his pledge of 12 March 1981 to inform Parliament still operable.
Mr Malcolm Fraser~ The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) As the Minister for Foreign Affairs said on 12 March 1981 (Hansard, page 704) the Australian Government has the assurance of the United States Administration that the B52 aircraft will be unarmed and carry no bombs. No changes in B52 missions have taken place since that time, nor have any discussions between the Australian and United States Governments been held on this matter.

(4) See (3) above. As I informed Parliament on 12 March 1981 (Hansard, page 703), lor the relevant Minister would advise the House if the Government's agreement were sought and given for any category of operations additional to sea surveillance and navigation training.

Report on Australian Defence Procurement: Inter-departmental Committee
(Question No. 4963)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 25 August 1982:
(I) When was the inter-departmental Committee, referred to in the Government's response to the report of the Joint

3178 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

Standing Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence on Australian Defence Procurement, set up.
(2) What is the composition of the Committee.
(3) Who (a) are the members of the Committee at present and (b) have been members of the Committee since its inception.
(4) On what dates, and for what periods, has the Committee met since its inception.
(5) What is the brief, or terms of reference, of the Committee.
(6) Has the brief, or terms of reference, been varied since the inception of the Committee; if so, what are the details.
(7) When is it intended that the Committee will complete its task.
(8) Will the report of the Committee be released when it is complete.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The inter-departmental Committee was established in 1978.
(2) The inter-departmental Committee is made up of representatives from the following departments: Administrative Services; Defence; Finance; Communications; Transport and Construction; Aviation; Defence Support.
(3) (a) As (2); (b) Administrative Services; Defence; Finance.
(4) The Committee met for a preliminary discussion on 14.11.1978 after which work was advanced by an informal working party. The Committee met again formally on 16.11.1979 and met thereafter on the following occasions: 25.1.1980, 30.5.1980, 16.9.1980, 14.10.1981, 2.11.1981, 16.11.1981, 19.11.1981, 11.3.1982, 25.3.1982, 21.4.1982, 6.5.1982, 20.5.1982, 28.5.1982, 24.6.1982, 21.7.1982, 15.10.1982,29. 10.1982, 5.11 .1982.
(5) The Terms of Reference of the Committee are to identify the circumstances in which selective tendering would be an appropriate approach to Commonwealth procurement and to determine what changes if any would be required to Finance Regulations to accommodate selective tendering procedures.

The Committee has had under particular consideration the following matters:
the feasibility of undertaking large scale projects (such as the design and construction in Australia of Naval combatant ships) or of acquiring specialist equipments of a technologically advanced or innovative character under the present (Finance) Regulations and any necessary changes to facilitate such activity;
the effects of present Procurement Regulations on the maintenance or development of defence support capabilities and the desirability of adopting a selective approach to the procurement of specialist defence equipment;
the extent to which the present Finance Regulations provide for selective tendering under a Certificate of Exemption, and the extent to which the issue of Certificates of Exemption is based on uniform principles across the Service;
the question whether a system of direct invitation of tenders (based on maintenance of a registered tenderers list) is more efficient for modern procurement conditions than the invitation of tenders by press advertisement.
(6) No.
(7) Early 1983.
(8) This will be a matter for Government decision.

Dumping of High Level Nuclear Waste in the Pacific
(Question No. 4979)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 26 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to any proposals by the United States of America Environmental Protection Agency and Department of State to commence experimental dumping of high level nuclear waste in the Pacific Ocean in 1984; if so, can he say (a) what is the nature of high level wastes to be dumped, (b) what is their origin and (c) what is the proposed dumping site.
(2) Can he say whether the proposed dumping site is the same as that designated by the Japanese Government for the dumping of low level waste.
(3) What attitude has the Australian Government expressed to the United States Government concerning any such nuclear waste dumping proposals.
(4) Can he guarantee that Australian uranium exported to the United States will not be dumped in the Pacific Ocean as nuclear wastes derived from spent nuclear fuel.
Mr Street~ The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) I am advised that no consideration is being given by any regulatory agency in the United States of America for dumping high-level waste in the Pacific Ocean. Sea-dumping of high-level waste is prohibited by the United States Marine Protection Research and Sanctuaries Act of 1972. In addition, the Convention on the Prevention of Marine Pollution by Dumping of Wastes and Other Matter of 1972, to which the United States is a signatory, declares highlevel radioactive wastes as unsuitable for dumping at sea.
(3) The Australian Government has drawn to the attention of the United States resolutions of the South Pacific Forum, of which Australia is a member, expressing opposition to the dumping of nuclear wastes in the Pacific Ocean. These resolutions reflect the deep concern felt by the South Pacific countries about the dumping of radioactive waste in the Pacific.

(4) The question of the disposal of their nuclear waste is a domestic responsibility of individual countries as a part of the management of their nuclear power industries. However, in the light of the answers given to (I), (2) and (3) above Australia has no expectation that the United States will dump high-level nuclear wastes or other nuclear wastes unsuitable for dumping at sea in the Pacific Ocean, irrespective of whether or not such wastes are derived from Australian origin uranium or other nuclear materials.
Relationship between Ministers for Defence and Defence Support
(Question No. 5031)
Mr Scholes asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 9 September 1982:
(I) Did the interim report of the Defence Review Committee recommend (a) the issue of a Ministerial Directive to clarify the relationship between the Ministers for Defence and Defence Support and their Departments, and (b) variations in the Administrative Arrangements Order to cover the changes proposed in the report.
(2) Has such a Ministerial Directive been issued; if so, does it depart in any way from the form recommended in Attachment A of the interim report; and (a) what are the details and (b) reasons for the variation.
(3) Has the Administrative Arrangements Order been amended in the terms recommended in Attachment B of the interim report; if not, (a) what terms have been amended and (b) what are the reasons for the variation.
Mr Malcolm Fraser~The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) (a) Although the Ministerial Directive as issued incorporates a considerable number of editorial and stylistic changes, there are no substantive differences between it and that proposed in Attachment A to the interim report. For ease of reference, the Ministerial Directive as issued is here reproduced in full:

3180 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

DIRECTIVE FROM THE PRIME MINISTER IN RESPECT OF THE MINISTER AND DEPARTMENT
OF DEFENCE SUPPORT
Roles
I. The Minister for Defence has overall responsibility for the nation's defence policies. This is to include responsibility for policy development in respect of defence-related activities administered by the Minister for Defence Support. The Minister for Defence is to keep the Minister for Defence Support informed of policy developments affecting the defence-related activities administered by the latter and is to consult with him as appropriate.
2. The Minister for Defence Support is responsible for administering the following defence-related activities within the defence policies approved by the Minister for Defence:
the naval dockyards but not including fleet base activities at Garden Island Dockyard
the defence production factories
the defence research and development establishments
that part of defence purchasing that up until now has been under the control of the Minister for Administrative Services
defence industry development
marketing activities associated with the above together with related administrative functions.
3. In keeping with the intention that the Minister for Defence and the Minister for Defence Support should work closely together, the Minister for Defence Support is also to occupy the position of Minister Assisting the Minister for Defence.
4. The Department of Defence Support is to participate in the policy development and the financial planning and programming processes of the Department of Defence in so far as they affect activities administered by the Minister for Defence Support.
Funding
5. The funding of the Department of Defence Support is to be decided as part of the Defence Outlay. Proposals for expenditure for the Department of Defence Support are to be subject to the same processes and to the same degree of scrutiny within the Department of Defence as other elements of defence expenditure. For this purpose the Department of Defence Support will make available to the Department of Defence all relevant information and documentation and the Department of Defence Support is to participate fully in the financial planning, programming and budget control processes of the Department of Defence. The form and content of the Appropriations for the Department of Defence Support shall be determined by the Departments of Defence, Defence Support and Finance.
Committees
6. The Minister for Defence Support and the Secretary to the Department of Defence Support are to be members of the Council of Defence.
7. The Secretary to the Department of Defence Support is to be a co-opted member of the Defence Committee when matters related to the activities administered by the Minister for Defence Support are to be considered.
8. In keeping with the requirements set out above concerning the relationship of the Department of Defence and the Department of Defence Support in respect of policy development processes and financial planning, programming and budget control processes, each department is to participate in the deliberations of the boards and committees of the other to

an extent, and at a level to be agreed by the Minister for Defence and the Minister for Defence Support.
Interdepartmental Access
9. The Department of Defence Support is to be responsive to appropriately endorsed requirements of the Defence Force. There is to be free access between the Department of Defence Support and the Service Offices in matters of a purely single Service nature. Involvement of Service personnel in activities administered by the Department of Defence Support is to be facilitated in the same way as it is in the Department of Defence.
10. In order effectively to discharge his responsibilities as set out in the Directive from the Minister of Defence to the Secretary to the Department of Defence regarding the position of the Chief Defence Scientist, the Chief Defence Scientist is to have free access to the research and development Laboratories which form part of the Department of Defence Support.
Annual Review
II. In keeping with the intention that the Secretary to the Department of Defence and the Secretary to the Department of Defence Support should work closely together, the Secretaries, together with the Chief of Defence Force Staff, are to meet regularly to review matters of common concern. In addition, in order to assess the effectiveness of the Defence Support organisation, a group of senior officers representing the Secretary to the Department of Defence, the Chief of Defence Force Staff and the Secretary to the Department of Defence Support, is to review annually the activities of the Department of Defence Support in respect of:
(a) defence production and dockyard support
(b) defence research and development
(c) defence purchasing
(d) defence industry development
(e) marketing of defence equipment and services.
12. The results of the annual review are to be presented by the Secretaries to the Departments of Defence and Defence Support to the Ministers for Defence and Defence Support and are to be included in the Annual Defence Report which is tabled in Parliament.
(2) (b) The various changes were introduced to improve clarity, to rectify minor omissions, to delete unnecessary repetition, to reflect present administrative practice more accurately and to allow for flexibility should administrative arrangements change in the future.
(3) (a) and (b) The Administrative Arrangements Order of 7 May 1982 for the Department of Defence Support is substantially the same as that proposed in Attachment B to the interim report. Some editorial changes have been made to the 'matters dealt with' column. The 'enactments administered' column includes additional legislation related to the Minister's functional responsibilities.
Eligibility for Inclusion on the Vietnam Roll of Honour
(Question No. 5105)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 16 September 1982:
(I) How many names were on the Australian War Memorial Roll of Honour for Vietnam service for each of the three Services on (a) 30 August 1972, (b) 30 August 1977 and (c) 30 August 1982.

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3181

(2) What are the criteria for determining the eligibility of servicemen or servicewomen for inclusion on the Roll of Honour.
(3) What is the period of time covered by the Vietnam Roll of Honour.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) There were no names on the Roll of Honour relating to Vietnam on 30 August 1972; by 30 August 1977, the Roll had been erected containing 517 names (8 RAN, 15 RAAF and 494 Army); on 30 August 1982 the figure was still 517 names.
(2) The broad criteria used in determining the eligibility of servicemen and servicewomen for inclusion on the Roll of Honour are that they must have been attested members of Australia's armed forces, their deaths must have been designated by the repatriation authorities as 'due to war service', and their deaths must have occurred within a specified period.
(3) The time period covered by the Vietnam part of the Roll of Honour is 3 August 1962 to I January 1973.
Selection of Conductors for ABC Orchestras
(Question No. 5118)
Mr Free asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 21 September:
(I) What are the procedures followed in the selection of conductors for ABC orchestras.
(2) What person or group is responsible for interviewing and auditioning applicants.
(3) What procedures exist for reviewing selection decisions.
(4) How many people currently hold positions as conductors of ABC orchestras.
(5) How many are Australian-born.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question, based on information supplied by the Australian Broadcasting Commission, is as follows:
(I) Conductors are selected by various methods.
(i) Chief or Principal conductors of the six symphony orchestras are selected by the Artists' Tours Committee (ATC), after extensive discussion and evaluation of their work. Any musician offered a Chief or Principal conductorship has normally undertaken at least one successful ABC concert tour, comprising concerts with several orchestras.
(ii) A certain number of longtime resident conductors, with proven ability, may be offered several concert engagements each year, after agreement by the Director of Music and the Federal Director of Concerts.
(iii) Individuals including visiting composer-conductors from overseas and returning Australian conductors often approach Music or Concert Department officers seeking engagements. Such conductors may be offered opportunities in line with their experience and particular areas of expertise.
(iv) Resident musicians with previous conducting experience outside the ABC may be invited to undertake an audition with one of the orchestras before a committee comprising principal players of the orchestra, the Chief or Principal conductor or a suitable alternative musician, and representatives of the Federal and State Music Departments. Full reports are provided after

each audition. Successful auditionees are offered studio recording work and minor concerts to evaluate their abilities.
(2) The Artists' Tours Committee comprising senior officers of the ABC's Music and Concert Departments and chaired by the Assistant General Manager (Radio), or the Director of Music and Federal Director of Concerts.
(3) Visiting conductors who do not meet ABC, audience or critical expectations are not invited to undertake further tours.
Resident conductors who do not maintain their performance standards, who are unable to develop orchestra standards or whose appeal to audiences is perceived to be insufficiently positive are gradually phased out and replaced by younger or alternative residents. These decisions are made by the selection committee.
(4) Eight-- the ABC administers six symphony orchestras, a training orchestra for young musicians (ABC Sinfonia) and employs a full-time Conductor-in-Residence.
(5) Three.
Australian National Railways Commission: Potential Public Service Obligations
(Question No. 5124)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport and Construction, upon notice, on 22 September 1982:
(I) How many potential public service obligations has the Australian National Railways Commission identified for the Government for possible funding by revenue supplements under section 44 of the Australian National Railways Act.
(2) How many of the obligations have been accepted by the Government and for wbat reasons.
(3) What steps has the Government taken to accelerate the Commission's review of potential obligations.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Australian National Railways Commission has currently identified one potential public service obligation for possible funding by a revenue supplement under Section 44 of the Australian National Railways Act.
(2) There has only been one specific request by the Commission under Section 44 of the Australian National Railways Act and this was accepted as a public service obligation while the future of tbe service was determined by arbitration.
(3) The Government considers that explicit funding of public service obligations is desirable in principle. Indeed, the new Australian National Railways Commission Bill makes specific provision for reimbursement of costs of complying with Government directions. It must be recognised, however, that the benefits of separate funding of PSOs are highly dependent upon the availability of accurate cost data.
The Government notes that the Commission is in the process of implementing its new computerised cost information sytstem and believes it would be more appropriate to defer explicit funding of public service obligations until that system is properly operational, and decisions can then be based on accurate cost data.

Department of Aboriginal Affairs: Estimated Expenditure on Uranium Mining
(Question No. 5217)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 12 October 1982:
What is the estimated total expenditure in 1982-83 for the activities of his Department associated with uranium mining in the Northern Territory, including salaries, allowances, travel, administrative and other expenses.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
A number of officers in the Department's Central Office and the Northern Territory spend varying proportions of their time handling matters associated with uranium mining in the Northern Territory. The estimated expenditure in 1982-83 on salaries and allowances for officers involved with uranium mining activities in the Northern Territory is $126,000. The expenditure On travel, administrative and other items in 1982-83 cannot be separately estimated because this expenditure is included in the overall Departmental expenditure on these items and is not readily quantifiable.
The above figure does not include payments to the Northern Land Council resulting from the conclusion of an agreement between it and the Commonwealth in respect of the Ranger uranium mine at Jabiru or payments to the Aboriginals Benefit Trust Account pursuant to Section 63 of the Aboriginal Land Rights (NT) Act in respect of the Ranger and Nabarlek uranium mines in the Northern Territory.
Minister for Ayiation: Attention to Chit Ayiation Matters
(Question No. 5220)
Mr Morris asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 12 October 1982:
Is it a fact that the Minister for Aviation is also Minister Assisting the Prime Minister in Federal Affairs and Public Service Matters and Deputy Leader of the House, and is currently Acting Minister for Industry and Commerce; if so, how does he propose to ensure that the Minister gives his undivided ministerial attention to civil aviation matters as the Prime Minister stated.
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The practice of asking a Minister in charge of one portfolio to assist another Minister with his duties is more than 40 years old and has been followed by both Liberal/National Party Governments and Labor Governments. It is a sensible arrangement which relieves Ministers of some of the pressures cf their work load and helps government administration generally.
The Minister for Aviation ceased acting as Minister for Industry and Commerce on II October 1982.
