National Library: Funds for Purchase of Modern Sound Recordings
(Question No. 5234)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 12 October 1982:
(I) What sum does the National Library of Australia spend on the purchase of (a) books and (b) sound recordings.
(2) Are insufficient funds provided for the purchase of modern sound recordings.
(3) Is it a fact that the Library receives Australian books by legal deposit but that no legal compulsion exists for the deposit of sound recordings; if so, why.
(4) Is half of the Library's collection of 500000 sound recordings stored at the old Government Printing Office building at Kingston, A.C.T., which has no temperature or humidity control and is a breeding ground for vermin; if so, are the extremes of dampness and heat at this location contributing to the physical deterioration of these fragile historical documents.
(5) Are any of the national collection of discs, cylinders and tapes catalogued; if not, why not in view of the demand from all sections of the community for access to this archive.
(6) Is this collection the only section of the Library that does not have the services of a librarian; if so, why.
(7) Are any listening or copying services provided in the Sound Recording Collection and Services Section of the Library.
(8) Are there currently only 4 full-time officers in this section, and can he say whether this number is less than the staff of an average suburban record shop.
(9) Is there a need for permanent subject specialists in this section of the Library; if so, when will the Library ipcrease the staffing of this section to enable it to provide an effective copying and listening service.
(10) Is it a fact that some of the Library's most significant sound recordings, in terms of social and historic importance, are acetate recordings which fall to pieces in the same manner as old nitrate films; if so, is an important part of the history of Australia being lost as a result of the disintegration of these fragile tapes, because the Library has not provided funds for a scheme to copy these unique sounds to archival tape.
(II) Is the Library responsible for the only major public source of audio information in Australia, whereas users of print information are serviced by a long-established and well funded library network.
(12) Has the Library downgraded the priority of sound archives: if so, why.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) and (b) In 1981-82 the National Library spent $2,492,000 on the purchase of books, $24,600 on the purchase of sound recordings and $18,600 on the oral history recording program. In 1982-83 it plans to spend $3,012,000 on the book collection, $46,000 on the purchase of sound recordings and $25,000 on the oral history program.
(2) The Library is not able to acquire the full range of any type of library material as defined in the Library's published Selection Policy. In spending the sound recordings budget, which is a responsibility of the Sound Archivist, priority has been given to the purchase of retrospective materials, since these may come on the market only once.
(3) The legal deposit provisions of the Copyright Act 1968, under which books and other printed materials are deposited

in the Library, do not extend to sound recordings or other non-print materials. However in May this year the members
of the Australian Record Industry Association (ARIA) agreed to deposit voluntarily in the Library one pressing of all
records manufactured in Australia by them from the beginning of 1982. The Attorney-Genera!'s Department is
presently reviewing audio-visual copyright law. In a submission to that Department, the Library has recommended
the extension of the deposit provisions to include sound recordings and other materials.

(4) About 200,000 recordings, approximately half of the

Library's recording collection, are stored at the old Govern-

ment Printing Office at Kingston A.C.T. While this accom-

modation is not considered to provide satisfactory conditions

of storage, considerable resources have been devoted during

1982 to improving this environment. A recent Library conser-

vation condition report states that "the provision of sisalation

which covers all windows prevents light from coming into the

building

the floor is clean, while the building is not

dust free, dust is now controlled

there is no evi-

dence of vermin

in short the storage condition of

records has dramatically improved. All 12 inch records have

been sleeved and the sleeving of IO inch records is well under-

way." The risk of damage to those parts of the Library's col-

lections which cannot be stored in the Library building, was

recognised by the Government when it authorised the con-

struction of a 7,000 sq. m. extension to the building in the

1982-83 Budget.

(5) The sound recording collection is not yet adequately documented or catalogued. Because of the rapid growth of the collection since its establishment in 1971 to 416,000 records at
30 June 1982, priority has been given to organising it prior to cataloguing. Considerable progress has been made in the or-
ganisation of the collection.

Since January 1981 a task force, originally of 3 and now of 6 part-time staff, has been engaged in the organisation of the recordings. In that time more than 60,000 broadcast discs
have been organised so that any identified discs can be
retrieved immediately. More than 40,000 LP discs have also been organised to allow retrieval of an identified disc and organisation of the considerable 78 collection is well underway.

(6) At present no librarian works in the Sound Recording Section. This is one of the specialist areas of the Library where administrative, rather than Librarian designations, have been
adopted at least for the present. The Head of the Section, the Sound Archivist, is recognised as one of the leaders of his profession in Australia. It is planned to allocate a Librarian to the
Section in 1982-83 to proceed with a cataloguing program.

(7) One full-time member of staff is engaged in copying sound recordings. Generally these recordings have been lent to the Library for the purpose of obtaining copies for the
sound recordings collection. From time to time the Sound Archivist also allocates resources to the copying of material
for broadcast. In the past limited facilities existed for listening. It has been necessary to curtail the listening service in 1982 due to limited resources.

(8) There are presently 4 full-time members of staff, in ad-
dition to 6 part-time officers who each work 30 hours per week in sorting and organising the recordings. Between December 1981 and September 1982 the Section had the additional services of a Technical Assistant, dedicated exclus-
ively to copying sound material. A replacement for this officer will shortly be appointed. No relevant comment can be offered about the staff of an average suburban record shop.

(9) All special collections in the Library would benefit from
the injection of subject specialists. However, in determining the resources it should allocate in meeting its obligation to preserve and develop the national collections and to make them available for use, the Library must take into account the

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3185

need to respond to demands for the development of all services. Since 1973 the permanent establishment of the Sound Recording Section has been increased from 2 to 4 with appropriate upward reclassification. In addition, since 1981 a total of 6 part-time positions have been assigned to the Section. This increase in staff has taken place over a period (since 1979) in which the National Library's overall ceiling has actually fallen by 9 per cent.
(10) The Sound Recording Section, as elsewhere within the Library, must determine an order of priorities in utilising its resources. Because of more pressing commitments, only a very small proportion of acetate material has been copied. However, such material is stored in environmentally controlled conditions which inhibit further deterioration.
( II) The establishment of the sound recording collection and services was a pioneering initiative by the Library. The Library is currently the major public source of audio information, both through its sound recording collection and services and oral history unit.
(12) No. The Library has enhanced the resources available for its sound archives since January 1981.
Dried Vine Fruits
(Question No. 5243)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 12 October 1982:
(I) For how many years has the Australian Dried Fruits Corporation, and its direct predecessors, been the only statutory organisation selling dried vine fruits on international markets.
(2) Are there now 2 Australian growers' organisations selling on international markets.
(3) Which of the following packers sells through the Australian Dried Fruits Corporation (a) Mildura Fruit Cooperative, (b) Irymple Packing Company, (c) Robinvale Cooperative and (d) other packing organisations in South Australia.
(4) What percentage of the crop is packed by each of the organisations referred to in part (3).
(5) If there are 2 growers' organisations selling on international markets, what percentage of the market does each hold and in what markets do they sell.
(6) What evidence is there of the 2 organisations competing in the same market and thereby reducing net returns to producers.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Neither the Australian Dried Fruits Corporation nor its predecessor has sold dried vine fruits on international markets. The Corporation's powers are generally to promote and control the export of dried vine fruit from Australia and its powers to trade in dried fruit placed under its control have not been used to date. A consortium of packing houses formed a private company for marketing purposes during the I920s. Today this company is known as Australian Dried Fruits Sales Pty Ltd (ADFS) and the honourable member may have confused this company with the statutory organisation.
(2) The meaning of the term "growers' organisations" is not clear. There are 57 persons, organisations or companies that have been granted licences to export dried vine fruit in 1982 but only four are significant in volume of sales on international markets. The main industry organisation for example, known as Australian Dried Fruits Association (ADFA), is composed of packers and marketing firms as well as growers but it does not engage in the selling of fruit.

Members of the ADFA control about 95 per cent of Australian dried vine fruit production; the remaining 5 per cent is packed by two independent companies in South Australia. For several years all fruit produced by ADFA members was marketed through ADFS. In late 1981, however, the Robinvale Producers Co-operative Co. Ltd, the Barmera Cooperative Packing Co. Ltd, and Berri Co-operative Packing Union Ltd-all members of ADFA--appointed G. Wood Son and Co. Pty Ltd as their marketing agent for the domestic and export markets.
(3) None. See (I) above. However, I understand that the Mildura Co-operative Fruit Company Ltd and Irymple Packing Pty Ltd sell fruit through ADFS. The two South Australian independent packers arrange their own sales.
(4) The approximate percentages of the dried vine fruit crop packed by the various groups of packers are:
Mildura Co-operative Fruit Company Ltd, approximately 39 per cent.
Irymple Packing Pty Ltd, Aurora Packing Pty Ltd, Coomealla Packing Pty Ltd, Sarnia Packing Pty Ltd, approximately 42 per cent.
Rivergrowers Co-operative Ltd, Waikerie Co-operative Producers Ltd, Anganston Fruit growers Co-operative Ltd, Swan Settlers Co-operative Association. Ltd, approximately 4 per cent (sold through ADFS).
Robinvale Producers Co-operative Co. Ltd, Barmera Cooperative Packing Co. Ltd, BeTTi Co-operative Packing Union Ltd, approximately 10 per cent.
Angas Park Fruit Company Pty Ltd, Robern (Overseas) Pty Ltd, approximately 5 per cent
(5) There are at present two dried fruit sellers exporting on behalf of their packer principals. The two South Australian independent packers conduct their own export operations. The quantities exported are those permitted by the corporation to be exported generally or to specific markets. The Corporation endeavours to ensure, as far as it is practicable to so do, that each exporter's share of each export market or region is proportionate to his annual handling of dried vine fruits.
(6) The United Kingdon is the only major international market affected by the recent changes in Australian selling arrangements. All four main Australian sellers export to the UK. In recent years there has been no competition between them in that market, as they have been required to sell through the one agent approved by the Corporation to sell Australian dried vine fruit there. The Corporation also lays down minimum prices, and certain terms and conditions, for sales to export markets.
In 1982, after representations by a significant sector of the Australian industry, the Corporation approved three agents in the UK market as part of an investigation to help it decide on future agency arrangements. Each seller now conducts his trade with the UK through a particular one of the three agents. No evidence is yet available to indicate that the new agency arrangements in 1982 have reduced returns to producers. The main competition in that market in 1982 has been from cheap Turkish fruits.
Export Marketing of Dried Vine Fruits
(Question No. 5244)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 12 October 1982:
(1) Have the members of the Australian Dried Fruits Corporation recently been appointed; ifso, who are the members.
(2) Did Mr C. Lloyd-Bostock replace Mr M. Lucey as a member of the Corporation; if so, what were the reasons for this.

3186 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

(3) Is Mr Lucey the General Manager of Mildura Fruit Cooperative, the largest packer of dried fruit in Australia.
(4) Can he say whether the attitude of the Corporation to multiple agencies for export marketing changed with the appointment of the new members.
(5) Have dual agencies in export markets resulted in increased administrative costs.
(6) Is the dual agency decision opposed by the majority of dried vine fruit producers.
(7) What consultation did he have with the new Chairman of the Corporation in relation to the Chairman's decision to exercise his casting vote to introduce dual export marketing.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Apart from the Chairman, Mr E. W. Barr, who was appointed in February 1982, all current members of the Australian Dried Fruits Corporation were appointed in January 1982. Their names are:
Mr V. L. Byrnes, Mr A. R. Mead, Mr H. M. Tankard, Mr W. M. Whiting, Mr E L. Jenkins, Mr J. C. Harrison, OBE, DFC, and Mr C. Lloyd-Bostock.
(2) Mr M. MeN. Lucey was one of the members of the Corporation whose term expired on 31 December 1981. He had been one of the two members appointed to the Corporation in 1979 as persons specially qualified by reason of marketing or selling dried vine fruits, or having other experience in commerce, finance, economics, science or industrial matters. Mr C. Lloyd-Bostock is one of the three new members and was appointed as having such qualifications.
(3) Mr Lucey is the General Manager of the largest cooperative packing house, Mildura Co-operative Fruit Co Ltd, but the Irymple Group of proprietary companies packs a larger volume of dried fruit.
(4) The Australian Dried Fruits Corporation has power to approve arrangements necessary or convenient to control export of dried fruits from Australia, and to control the sale and distribution after export, of Australian dried fruits. This power includes requiring that a purchaser, agent or representative to or through whom the dried fruits are shipped be approved by the Corporation. The arrangements approved, which vary from market to market, are reviewed from time to time in the light of changing circumstances in Australia and overseas. Since the appointment of Corporation members early in 1982, two additional import agencies have been approved for the United Kingdom market.
(5) No significant increase has occurred in the Australian Dried Fruits Corporation's administrative costs following appointment of the two additional agents.
(6)There is no definitive evidence whether the dual agency arrangements in the UK are favoured or opposed by a majority of dried vine fruit producers. The Corporation of course has power to act without seeking such ~vidence.
(7) The Corporation has full authority to vary agency arrangements. The Chairman was not required to consult me as to the exercise of his voting rights, nor did he.
Seychelles
(Question No, 5246)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 13 October 1982:

(I) Has his attention been drawn to reports that a proportion of the militia of President Rene of the Seychelles mutinied in August 1982.
(2) Has the Government received any information to verify these reports and associated reports that some 70 Seychellois were slain in action involving Tanzanian soldiers; if so, what are the details.
Mr Street-The answers to the honourable member's question are as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The Government has received reports confirming that some members of the Seychelles army mutinied in August. The rebels complained of mistreatment and demanded the dismissal of several senior officers. The mutiny collapsed after 36 hours when troops supporting the Rene Administration captured key installations. The press reported that five mutineers, one loyalist soldier and two civilians were killed and that Tanzanian troops were involved in an unsuccessful attempt to retake the radio station. Other reports suggest that a large number of Seychellois were killed, but the Government is not able to verify this. The Government has not seen any reports connecting the deaths of Seychellois with action by Tanzanian troops.
Australian National Gallery: Admission Fee
(Question No. 5271)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 14 October 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the fact that the British Government does not charge the public an entrance fee to visit the British National Collection in London contained in the National Gallery, the National Portrait Gallery, the Tate Gallery, the British Museum, the London Museum, the National Maritime Museum, the Victoria and Albert Museum and the Natural History Museum.
(2) Has his attention also been drawn to the fact that the United States Government does not charge the public an entrance fee to visit the United States National Collection in Washington contained in the National Art Gallery, the Smithsonian Institute, the Museum of History and Technology, the National Air and Space Museum and the Natural History Museum.
(3) Has his attention been further drawn to the fact that the Canadian Government does not charge an entrance fee to visit the Canadian National Collection in Ottawa contained in the National Gallery of Canada, the National Museum of Man, the National Museum of Natural Sciences and the National Museum of Science and Technology.
(4) Is it a fact the the Australian National Collection is contained in several buildings including the Australian War Memorial, the National Library, Parliament House, the High Court and others, as well as in the new National Gallery; if so, will entrance fees also be charged for viewing the National Collection contained in these other buildings.
(5) Will he insist on departing from established practice on the national capitals of Australia's closest allies amongst the Western democracies and proceed with restricting access to the Australian National Gallery only to those who can afford to pay.
(6) Who has paid for the Australian National Collection and the National Gallery.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3187

(I), (2) and (3) Yes. However I understand that throughout Europe many major galleries and art museums such as the Louvre, charge for admission.
(4) Yes. There are works of art and other items of importance to the nation's heritage which are displayed in Commonwealth institutions and buildings as mentioned by the honourable member. These works do not, however, come within the meaning of National Collection as defined in the National Gallery Act 1975 as being all works of art that are owned by the Gallery from time to time. Charges are levied for entry to the Australian National Gallery only.
(5) An extensive range of exemptions applies in relation to charges for admission to the Australian National Gallery as follows:
children under 15;
full-time students;
persons in receipt of pensions, benefits and allowances under the Social Security Act 1947 and the Repatriation Act 1920 and their dependants; and
members of the Australian National Gallery Association.
It is anticipated that over 50 per cent of visitors to the gallery will be admitted free in this way.
The honourable member may be aware that the Gallery recently announced that, by arrangement with ESSO Australia Ltd, the Gallery will be open to the public free of charge, for a three hour period from 5.00pm to 8.00pm on Thursday evenings during the daylight saving period to 10 March 1983.
(6) Works of art and other material of importance to the nation's heritage which are displayed in Commonwealth institutions and buildings, including the collection owned by the National Gallery, and the Gallery building itself, have been funded principally from annual appropriations made by Parliament. From time to time these collections have been supplemented by specific donations from individuals. Some are also enhanced by surplus material from Government departments and authorities. For example, some Government aircraft have become part of the collection of the Australian War Memorial in this way.

Port Augusta, South Australia: Diversion of Freight Trains
(Question No. 5370)
Mr Wallis asked the Minister for Transport and Construction, upon notice, on 19 October 1982:
(1) Were approaches made to him by the City of Port Augusta Corporation, South Australia, seeking to have Australian National freight trains diverted around Port Augusta instead of the present practice of taking these trains through Port Augusta.
(2) Was concern expressed that many of the freight wagons carry highly inflammable and dangerous materials.
(3) Has he given any consideration to the approaches from the Port Augusta Corporation; if so, when will a decision be taken.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) 1 replied to the Port Augusta Corporation on 7 September 1982 in the following terms:
The level of rail traffic in flammable liquids through Port Augusta is not considered a significant safety hazard. The quantities of petrol and LPG carried by rail through Port Augusta are 180 tonnes per week and 20 tonnes per week, respectively. These quantities are relatively small and much less than that carried every day through many Australian Communities. It is generally acknowledged that rail transport of these goods is considerably safer than road

transport. Within the town limits, safe operating procedures ensure that the risk of accidents with these fuels is minimised.
The conditions for transporting of flammable liquids by the Australian National Railways Commission are regulated by the provisions of the Australian Code for the Transport of Dangerous Goods by Road and Rail, which ensures the safest possible conditions of carriage.
The Commission has, in the interests of effective town planning, indicated that it sees a long term need for a by-pass route around Port Augusta. Current indications suggest that the bypass will not be required for quite some time.

Defence Force: Overseas Recruitment
(Question No. 5436)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 20 October 1982:
How many (a) RAN, (b) RAAF and (c) Australian Regular Army officers currently serving were recruited from outside Australia.
Mr SincIair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The information requested is not readily available within the Services. Collection of the information would involve a time-consuming manual search of several thousand files by staff who would have to be diverted from other priority tasks.
Defence Force: Recruitment
(Question No. 5437)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 20 October 1982:
(I) How many teams or recruiting officers have been sent overseas by the (a) Army, (b) RAAF and (c) RAN in the last 5 years.
(2) What was the cost of this exercise in each case.
(3) How many (a) officers and (b) other ranks were recruited in each case.
(4) Why was recruitment within Australia not successful.
Mr SincIair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No teams or recruiting officers have been sent overseas by the Defence Force Recruiting Organisation in the last five years. However, in June 1977, a team comprising two Naval Police Warrant Officers and a Naval Police Constable visited the United Kingdom on behalf of Navy Office. The team was tasked to recruit fifty ex-Royal Navy jRoyal Marine non-commissioned personnel for service as members of the Naval Police Branch of the RAN.
(2) The cost is not known. However, the team travelled by Service transport as part of a RAN fleet visit to UK at that time.
(3) (a) No officers were recruited, (b) Forty-eight non-commissioned personnel were entered.
(4) Naval Police are recruited entirely from serving and exserving sailors and the normal recruiting efforts within Australia failed to attract sufficient numbers of applications at that time.

(8) What is calculated to be the remaining duration of voluntary electoral enrolment for Aborigines, which in October
1961 the Select Committee on Voting Rights of Aborigines
considered a temporary provision (Committee Report, paragraph 80).

Mr Newman--The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) On 6 June 1979 the then Chairman of the Council for Aboriginal Development, Mr W. F. Bird, wrote to the then Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, Senator Chaney, conveying the Council's decision to recommend the 'removal of the optional electoral enrolment provision in respect of Aborigi-
nals'. He also set out the Council's rider to that recommendation-

The Council further decided that

the amend-

ment should be delayed until after a program of electoral

education had been undertaken and completed. This delay

is intended to allow a program of electoral education to run

for some time so that Aboriginals currently unenrolled may

be given the opportunity of learning about elections, enrol-

ment and voting before they are required by law to enrol. A

delay of a period of between 2-4 years for this purpose is

envisaged by Council'.

(2) The Aboriginal Electoral Education Program Teams commenced in Western Australia and South Australia in June
1979. A third team began operating in January 1981 when the program was extended to the Northern Territory.

(3) There is no proposal to extend the program to other States during 1982-83. The program will be extended as and when financial and staff resources permit.

(4) No, not at this stage. However, any extension of the program to Queensland or other States would, of course, be made in consultation with the State Governments.

(5) Subsequent to March 1980 the Queensland Govern-
ment decided, for what I understand to be reasons of State electoral administration, not to enter into joint roll arrange-
ments with the Commonwealth. It must be remembered, however, that the conduct of State elections and the maintenance of electoral rolls for State purposes are matters for the State Governments concerned. Discussions are proceeding on the
possible introduction of dual Commonwealth-State electoral claim cards.

(6) As at II November 1982, enrolment for both Common-
wealth and State purposes in New South Wales was identical; and, in Victoria 243 more electors were enrolled for Commonwealth purposes than for State purposes. This difference in Victoria results from the different residential requirements of Commonwealth and State Legislation-the Commonwealth

legislation requires one months residence in a Subdivision before an enrolment entitlement is established, whereas the Victorian legislation requires three months residence.
In New South Wales and Victoria the State electoral legislation does not differentiate between Aboriginals, naturalborn or naturalized subjects and therefore there are no separate details regarding the enrolment of Aboriginals.
(7) As was stated in the answer to Question No. 5628 part 3 (Hansard, 31 March 1990, page 1492), in those States where it is compulsory for Aboriginals to enrol, the enforcement of the compulsory provisions is the responsibility of State authorities.
(8) The Government has made a commitment to introduce compulsory enrolment for Aboriginals. That commitment still stands. The timing of the necessary amending legislation is a matter for the Government.
National Highway System: Sealing of Roads in South Australia and Northern Territory
(Question No. 5487)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport and Construction, upon notice, on 26 October 1982:
(I) What are the anticipated completion dates for the sealing of those sections of the national highway system from (a) Adelaide to the Northern Territory border and (b) the South Australian border to Darwin.
(2) What is the estimated (a) total cost of the works (b) costs already expended and (c) additional costs still to be incurred to completion.
(3) What works in relation to these sections have (a) already been performed and (b) are yet to be performed.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) The Commonwealth Government and the Government of South Australia have a joint commitment to complete the reconstruction and sealing of the Stuart Highway in South Australia between Port Augusta and the Northern Territory border by 1987.
Because of the size of this project, and the difficulties of constructing roads in harsh, remote locations, it is not possible to predict accurately at this stage when it will be completed. However, at the present construction rate, it is anticipated the highway will be sealed to the border by late 1986 ahead of the 1987 commitment.
Sealing of the road between Port Augusta and Pimba was completed in March 1981 when reconstruction and sealing of the 50 kilometres between Bookaloo and Mount Gunson was finished. Of the remaining 757 kilometres of the highway between Pimba and the Northern Territory border, it is expected that sealing as far as Gosses will be completed by the end of November this year, bringing to 334 kilometres the total length of sealed road north of Port Augusta.
(b) Sealing of the Stuart Highway from the South Australin border to Darwin was completed in March 1976.
(2) (a) The estimated total cost of reconstructing and sealing the Stuart Highway in South Australia between Pimba and the Northern Territory border is $ 102m in 1982/83 prices.

Answers to Questions

(b) To date, approximately $26m has been spent on construction works on the Stuart Highway in South Australia, including some $4.7 million to complete the 50 km Bookaloo to Mount Gunson section between Port Augusta and Pimba.
(c) On the basis of recent cost estimates, approximately $80m (in 1982/83 prices) remains to be expended to complete the Stuart Highway project in South Australia.
(3) (a) To date, the following sections have been completed:
50 km between Bookaloo/Mount Gunson

Adult Migrant Education Service
(Question No, 5492)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 27 October 1982:
What are the means employed by the Government to make migrants aware of the services of the Adult Migrant Education Service.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Information about the services provided under the Adult Migrant Education Program is disseminated through numerous channels. General information on courses is provided in printed information material distributed to prospective migrants overseas. Such information is also encompassed in group and individual counselling provided before departure for Australia.
Those migrants accommodated in migrant centres are able to attend courses at those centres and receive information about avaiiable courses on their arrival.
For migrants in the community, information is disseminated through radio, newpapers and community information channels. Ethnic community organisations, in particular. play an important part in spreading information about English language learning opportunities.
Following the discussion at the first meeting of the new Joint Commonwealth/State Committee on the Adult Migrant Education Program on 26-27 October, increased promotion of the program is to be undertaken by the State Adult Migrant Education Services to ensure that those needing to improve their English language ability are aware of the available opportunities.

Adult Migrant Education Service: Funding
(Question No. 5495)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 27 October 1982:
Is it a fact that the Commonwealth Government has refused to allocate additional funding to enable the New South Wales Government to rectify the situation in which 150 to 200 teachers are being employed in the Adult Migrant Education Service on a full time casual basis but with none of the benefits of their permanent colleagues; if so, what is the reason.
Mr Hodges--The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Commonwealth Government has allocated additional funding to enable the New South Wales Government to increase the number of permanent teachers by 30 positions in 1982-83. It has also proposed arrangements which would enable the conversion of some 10 further positions from casual to permanent.
I have had discussions with the New South Wales Minister for Education and consultations are continuing. I have indicated to the New South Wales Minister for Education that I need to consider the national implications of an increase in the proportion of permanent teachers within the Adult Migrant Education Service in New South Wales for funding the Adult Migrant Education Program. My Department's review of the arrangements in other States indicates a situation which differs markedly from State to State in terms of remuneration and in terms of leave benefits and tenure for non-permanent teachers.
There are also some issues relating to the structure of the adult migrant education arrangements in New South Wales and the effect on arrangements of the recent Evaluation of

3192 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

Post Arrival Programs and Services which need to be taken into account.
Further consideration of the Teachers Federation claim is dependant on the resolution of these issues by the New South Wales authorities.

Repatriation Claims: Uniformity of Judgments
(Question No. 5648)
Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 10 November 1982:
(I) Can the Minister state what measures have been taken by (a) Repatriation Boards, (b) the Repatriation Commission and (c) Repatriation Review Tribunals to ensure reasonable uniformity of judgments made on claims which are essentially similar either because of circumstances surrounding the claims, or the conditions for which the claims are made, or both.
(2) When were those steps taken and what changes to practice resulted.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) and (2) For some time the Repartiation Commission has been taking steps to improve the uniformity of decision making within the Repatriation determining system, particularly since the handing down of the High Court decision in the case of Mrs Nancy Law.
Since that time regular meetings of Repatriation Board Chairmen and delegates of the Repatriation Commission have

3194 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

been held to discuss decisions of the Courts and the Administrative Appeals Tribunal and other matters of common interest, with a view to ensuring consistency in decision making as far as practicable.
The re-organisation of the top structure of the Department is being implemented. As part of the restructuring of the Department it is proposed to locate in Canberra all Commission delegates who handle appeals against decisions of Repatriation Boards. This shoulf,l enhance the consistency of decision making at this level. In addition, a Legal Services Division has been established. Among other things, it will advise on the functions, structure and operation of the determining system.
One of the priority tasks of the new Legal Services Division will be the development of a system of reference documentation for distribution to all Repatriation Boards and Commission delegates. This documentation will include copies of identified significant decisions of the Courts and the Repatriation Review Tribunal as they become available. The Legal Division is responsible for developing a consistent approach to the consideration of claims and appeals and identifying training requirements for those engaged in the determination system. Some training courses have already been run. This Division is also responsible for monitoring the decisions of the various determining bodies to check that they are consistent and correct in law.
These matters are still in the developing stage. While it is hoped that they will result in more effective decision-making, it is too early to assess any effects.
The Repatriation Review Tribunal has commenced collecting copies of its decisions reasons in a form to make them more readily accessible and in this exercise the Tribunal is seeking to identify more significant decisions.
I also understand that the Tribunal has been convening meetings of Deputy Presidents to discuss the various decisions of the Courts and the Administrative Appeals Tribunal in the Repatriation area.

National Anthem
Mr Newman-On 9 November 1982 (Hansard, page 2854) Mr Ian Cameron asked me the following question, without notice:
Whether the Government will initiate a program throughout Australia to educate us in the words of our new anthem. 'Advance Australia Fair'.
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
'Advance Australia Fair' was chosen as the National Tune by a large majority of voters at a national poll in 1'977. It was particularly noted at the time that the choice was for a tune and not a song and that 'God Save The Queen' would remain the National Anthem.
The original words of 'Advance Australia Fair', composed by Peter Dodds McCormick in 1878. were written for an earlier period in our history and are not officially recognised for use today. Earlier this year the Government considered whether it should take formal steps concerning words for a National Song. It was felt that words that would find general acceptance would evolve in time.
The Government does not therefore propose to make any change at present to existing arrangements.

Defence Force Personnel: Duties at Nareen, Victoria
(Question No. 5061)
Mr Scholes asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 14 September 1982:
Are a number of Defence Force personnel being utilised in works and other duties at the Prime Minister's property at Nareen, Victoria; if so, (a) how many persons have been or are allocated to these duties, (b) what are the duties requiring use of Defence personnel, (c) could any of these duties have been carried out by local tradesmen or contractors, (d) what is the cost to the Defence Budget of these services and (e) are these costs recovered from the Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet.
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The honourable member would appreciate the need for a Prime Minister to have communications available at all times, including when at home. He would also understand, in view of the national security classification applying to much of those communications, that it is essential that an appropriate level of security protection is provided.
To that end, Defence equipment is used for communications between Nareen and Canberra, and other centres. Defence Force personnel, having an appropriate level of security clearance, service that equipment.
The equipment servicing generally involves visits to 'Nareen' by maintenance teams of up to four men at approximately three-monthly intervals. Special visits are made if and

when equipment malfunctions require or when major cyclical maintenance is due. Defence Force personnel also service similar equipment installed in my offices in Sydney and Melbourne and in my Department in Canberra.
Defence personnel servicing communication equipment at 'Nareen' also service a Government-owned standby generator which is an alternative power source for communications and security equipment in emergencies. These personnel have no other functions at 'Nareen '.
In 1982-83 cost of Defence personnel servicing equipment at 'Nareen' is expected to be approximately $9,000. Such costs are not recovered from my Department.

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3199

Gold Owned by Australian Government
(Question No. 5230)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 12 October 1982:
(I) What is the total value of gold owned by the Australian Government.
(2) In what countries is this gold held, and what are the values of this gold held in each country.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Reserve Bank of Australia holds Australia's official gold and foreign exchange reserves. At end-October 1982 the value of gold held totalled $3575m. Gold is valued at the average London gold price for the month, converted to Australian dollars at the market rate of exchange applying on the last day of the month.
(2) The bulk of this gold is held in Australia; the remainder is held in the United Kingdom and United States of America. I understand that for reasons of security, it is not the practice of the Reserve Bank to disclose the value of gold it holds at specific locations.
Sanctions Against South Africa and the Soviet Union
(Question No. 5272)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 14 October 1982:
(I) What is Australia's declared attitude towards the United Nations International Year of Mobilisation for Sanctions against South Africa.
(2) Does the UN Charter provide for the Security Council alone to impose sanctions.
(3) Is Australia's approach towards sanctions against the Soviet Union consistent with his answers to parts (I) and (2); if not, why not?
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The International Year of Mobilisation for Sanctions against South Africa was proposed by the International Conference on Sanctions against South Africa, which was held in Paris from 20-27 May 1981. Australia attended the Paris Conference as an observer, but did not participate in its deliberations and does not adhere to the Paris Declaration.
In November 1981 the United Nations General Assembly passed a resolution (36/172 B) which endorsed the report of the Paris Conference and proclaimed the year 1982 International Year of Mobilisation for Sanctions against South Africa. Australia abstained on this resolution.
Australia's approach is based on the Government's belief that the subject of mandatory sanctions is within the exclusive competence of the Security Council. However should the Security Council decide to impose sanctions against South Africa, Australia would abide by its decision.
(2) Yes.
(3) Australia has adopted a number of measures, which could be regarded as sanctions in the broadest sense of the term, against both the Soviet Union and South Africa. These measures are designed to curtail the range of our bilaterial relations with those countries and are an expression of the Government's condemnation of the policies and actions of the governments of those countries.

There is no inconsistency between the measures adopted in the cases of South Africa and the Soviet Union.

Advertising of 'Barclay' Cigarettes
(Question No. 5282)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 14 October 1982:
(I) Is it a fact that, in response to questions asked in Parliament regarding the accuracy of advertising relating to the tar content of the cigarette brand 'Barclay', investigations into the matter were undertaken by his Department.
(2) If so, what progress has been made in these investigations.

3200 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

(3) Will he advise the House of the results of these investigations and indicate what action he will take in regard to the matter.
Mr CarltonThe answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No. I am advised that investigations into the 'Barclay' brand of cigarettes commenced before questions were asked in the Parliament concerning claims about the delivery of tar and nicotine by this brand and possible implications for the testing methodology currently in use. The investigations followed an informal expression of concern by representatives of an Australian cigarette manufacturer in April 1982 regarding the validity and comparability of tar and nicotine yield test results for the 'Barclay' brand of cigarettes. My Department and the Australian Government Analytical Laboratories (AGAL) investigated technical aspects of the type of filter used and of the implications as regards the testing methodology used in accordance with the Voluntary Code in Respect of CPM (,Tar') and Nicotine Labelling on Cigarette Packaging.
(2) and (3) Based on the results of the investigations referred to in (I) and the evidence available to my Department and AGAL, including the results of research conducted overseas, there appears to be no scientific case for any change in the current industry/ AGAL agreed collaborative testing methodology for determining tar and nicotine yields of cigarettes.
Manufacturers and importers of cigarettes in Australia are responsible for ensuring that there are no breaches of the Voluntary Code. The operation of the Code will continue to be monitored by my Department.

Popular Theatre Troupe, Brisbane
(Question No. 5365)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 19 October 1982:
(I) Was the Popular Theatre Troupe of Brisbane given a grant of $75,000 for 1982 by the Theatre Board of the Australia Council.
(2) If so, what were the particular reasons for the Troupe's success in attracting this grant.
(3) Has the Theatre Board decided not to give any grant to the Troupe in 1983.
(4) Is he aware that the Popular Theatre Troupe presents material containing controversial social issues and that some of it consists of political satire aimed at the Queensland Government.

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3201

(5) Is he also aware the Queensland Government recently prevented the Troupe from presenting a political satire to State schools in Queensland.
(6) Has he received any representations from the Queensland Government, either formal or informal, concerning the Popular Theatre Troupe; if so, what was the nature of those representations and were they taken into account when the decision was made to cut off funds for 1983.
(7) What were the particular reasons for the decision not to fund the Troupe in 1983.
(8) Was one of those reasons the insufficient artistic development of the Troupe; if so, what does this mean.
(9) Would the Troupe be better placed to continue receiving Commonwealth funds if it did not present political satires.
(10) Is he able to say whether the Queensland Government subsidises the Troupe; if it is not subsidised in this way, was this taken into account in the decision to cut off Commonwealth funds to the Troupe and in what way did it influence the decision.
(11) What is the usual guideline for deciding funding levels for theatre groups when there is no State Government subsidy.
(12) Will he review the decision to cut off Commonwealth funds to the Troupe in 1983 in view of the important role the Troupe plays in presenting an alternative view on various social issues in a State where such action is actively discouraged by the State Government.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Australia Council has advised me as follows:
(I) Grants made by the Theatre Board to the Popular Theatre Troupe in 1982 were:
General grant towards activities--$75,000 Supplementary grant-$4,000 Challenge grant-$4,000
(2) Theatre Board funding for the Troupe over recent years has reflected the Board's concern to assist the development of a diversity of theatre experiences. The Popular Theatre Troupe has been an innovative force in drama in Queensland in terms of its approach to drama and its commitment to presenting theatre in community venues such as shopping centres, schools and factories.
In approving the grant towards the Troupe's activities in 1982, the Board took into account the Troupe's record of presenting innovative productions and the lack of similar theatre experiences available in Brisbane.
(3) The Theatre Board has decided not to provide financial assistance to the Troupe in 1983.
(4) The material contained in productions of the Popular Theatre Troupe has related to controversial social issues and on some occasions has contained satirical material aimed at the Queensland Government.
(5) Two productions of the Troupe-Crook Shop and The Stale We're In (1982) have not been approved for performance in State high schools. This is a matter for the Queensland Arts Council and the Education Department in Queensland.
(6) No representations concerning the Popular Theatre Troupe have been received by the Australia Council from the Queensland Government.
(7) The Board considered other applicants had stronger claims for assistance from the limited funds at its disposal. The Board's decision also took into account the following factors affecting the Troupe's operations:

the precarious financial position of the company, including its continuing failure to attract significant funding from sources other than the Australia Council;
concern about the artistic development of the company; frequent turnover of actors and staff which has resulted in the discontinuity of the Troupe's activities and a failure to develop professional skills; at a meeting with the Theatre Board in Brisbane on 24 September 1982, members of the Company could not indicate to the Board that they would continue with the Troupe in 1983.
(8) Concern about the artistic development of the Troupe was a factor which influenced the Board's decision to withdraw its support. The term 'artistic development' refers to the ability of the Troupe to maintain its operations on a sound basis and to continue to present stimulating drama productions of high quality. The Board considers the following factors inhibit the Troupe's artistic development:
the Troupe is without an artistic director; there is frequent turnover of members of the Troupe, most members serving for 12 months only; there has been a frequent turnover of administrators for the Troupe;
the Troupe lacks long term artistic plans.
(9) No. Council decisions to fund the Troupe have not been influenced by the content of the Troupe's productions. Nor was the content of the Troupe's productions considered in relation to the Board's decision to withdraw funding in 1983.
(10) The Queensland Government has never funded the Troupe. The Theatre Board has always been aware of the refusal of the Queensland Government to fund the Troupe and it was not a consideration in the Board's decision to withdraw funding.
(II) No fixed guidelines have been developed to cover situations where State funding is not available for theatre groups. The Theatre Board considers applications against its policies as set out in its policy booklet, the artistic merit of projects proposed, and funds available.
(12) The Theatre Board will, on request from the Troupe, review its decision.

Uranium Enrichment Plant: Feasibility Study
(Question No. 5431)
Mr Hayden asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 20 October 1982:
(I) What size of enrichment plant is under investigation in the uranium enrichment feasibility study being conducted by the Uranium Enrichment Group of Australia.
(2) What would be the anticipated workforce during (a) each year of plant construction and (b) the production phase of such a plant.
(3) What would be the estimated cost of a plant of this kind.
(4) If the study referred to in part (I) is also investigating the establishment of a uranium conversion plant, what is the estimated (a) size, (b) workforce and (c) cost of the conversion plant.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy, who has responsibility for the uranium enrichment feasibility study, has provided me with the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The Uranium Enrichment Group of Australia is basing its investigations on a plant size of I million SWU /per annum; however, to be economic, the capacity of the enrichment plant would need to grow from 200,000 SWU /year in the early 1990's to approximately I million SWU /year over a period of five to six years. The UEGA anticipates that the plant would eventually reach a capacity of 3 million SWU /year, twelve to fifteen years after start-up.
2(a) It is not possible to estimate the construction workforce on a yearly basis as construction of gas centrifuge enrichment plants is characterised by the continued addition of production units as demands grows. It is estimated, however, that the construction workforce could amount to some four hundred people on average, who may be able to enjoy semipermanency, possibly for more than 10 years depending on the rate of growth of the enterprise.
(b) It is estimated that the operational labour force would grow from some two hundred people to about five hundred.
(3) The capital cost of the plant when at I million SWU /year capacity would be in the region of $600m in 1982 dollars rising to $1.5 billion at a capacity of 3 million SWU/year.
4(a), (b), (c) The UEGA has not studied in detail the establishment of a uranium conversion plant, however this would be necessary if a full-scale feasibility study is undertaken and if it is apparent that no other organisation is interested in establishing a conversion plant.

A feasibility study of a uranium conversion plant at Pt. Pirie is being conducted by the Uranium Conversion Joint Venture comprising Broken Hill Associated Smelters Pty Ltd, Roxby Management Services Pty Ltd, British Nuclear Fuels Limited, and the South Australian Government.
It is estimated that an Australian uranium conversion plant should ideally have an initial capacity of around 1,000 tonnes U /year, rising to a maximum of 4,500 tonnes U /year by the time enrichment capacity reaches 3 million SWU /year (early in the 2000's).
The capital cost of such a plant would vary with capacity and the technology chosen, in the range of $60m-$120m (in 1982 dollars). Likewise, direct employment in normal operation would range from 100 to 300 people with probably around 500 during construction.
Australian Army: Promotion and Recruitment of Personnel
(Question No. 5440)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 20 October 1982:
(I) How many non-commissioned personnel of the Australian Regular Army were promoted to officer level in each of the last 5 years.
(2) How many Army officers were recruited outside Australia in each of the last 5 years.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) 1977-78-85, 1978-79-103, 1979-80-49, 1980-81-121,1981-82-131.
(2) This information is not kept in readily retrievable form. The Army has not actively recruited overseas during the last five years. National Library of Australia: Anti-apartheid Films
(Question No. 5450)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 21 October 1982:
Further to his answer to question No. 4976 (Hansard, 23 September 1982, page 1894) in which he stated that the National Library also tries to ensure, as far as possible, that mUltiple viewpoints on major issues are represented, what films have been circulated as an alternative viewpoint to films defending apartheid provided by the South African Embassy.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The National Library of Australia has advised me that the National Film Lending Collection includes several films which express views opposing apartheid and/or which attempt to objectively analyse apartheid. These films, which are available for loan, are:
Report on South Africa's Race Policy' (1978, 48 mins) which chronicles the position of blacks in South Africa and which, though it attempts to be objective, leaves the viewer with an understanding of injustice done to the black population.
South Africa: The White Laager (1977, 59 mins) which explains the collective psychology and history of the Afrikaaners and how these have resulted in apartheid. The film analyses their separatist attitude and shows the factors which caused the evolution of racism in South Africa.
Katutjuta (1974, 37 mins) is an African word meaning 'total insecurity'. This film shows what it means to blacks to have no voice in Parliament, no free choice in the work they do or where they live but, nevertheless to be required to contribute to South Africa's economic prosperity.
A further film, Generations of Resistence (1979-80, 52 mins) which documents the three hundred years struggle of native South Africans against the white influence in all aspects of their society, is on order for the Collection.

Mr Anthony~The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

Manufactured Products: Imports
(Question No. 5697)
Mr Kent asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 10 November 1982:
Did he say on 8 November 1982, at the opening of Melbourne's World Trade Centre, that the Australian Government believes it must take measures to liberalise trade, not restrict it; if so, will he assure the House and the tens of thousands of workers in Australian manufacturing industry who are facing unemployment because of the high volume of cheap foreign imports, that the Government does not intend to ease imports of manufactured products from overseas while Australian workers are faced with mass unemployment.

Mr Anthony~ The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
In my address at the official opening of the International Trade Development Centre in Melbourne on 8 November 1982, I said that the Australian Government has put forward a package of proposals for consideration at the GATT Ministerial Meeting being held in late November. I indicated that the proposals covered areas such as protectionism, subsidies, safeguards, agriculture and dispute settlement and, in particular, involved a standstill on all industry assistance measures which have a trade distorting effect, to be followed by a windback of these measures. I pointed out, moreover, that the latter proposal is designed to break the vicious circle of protectionism and develop momentum for sustained economic recovery and growth. I said that such an approach was essential if the present economic malaise is to be overcome and the 'beggar thy neighbour' policies which we saw leading up to the economic chaos of the 1930s are to be avoided. A copy of my address is available from the Parliamentary Library. As has been stated on many occasions, the Australian initiative relating to protectionism concerns international protectionismthat is, a commitment by the major trading nations to reverse the trend of recent years towards increased protectionism. In this context, there has been no suggestion that Australia would act unilaterally. What I said in effect on 8 November was that the Australian Government believes that the major trading nations of the world must take measures to liberalise trade, not restrict it. The Government considers that a meaningful and lasting commitment of this type is necessary to facilitate an early return to sustained growth in world trade coupled with brighter prospects for economic recovery.

Tax Evasion

Mr Howard-On 23 September 1982, (Hansard, page 1778) Mr Bowen asked the
Treasurer the following question, without notice:

I refer to the Supreme Court Action involving McCabe-

Lafranchi and Commercial Securities (Vic.) Pty Ltd before

Mr Justice Jenkinson. A judgment was delivered as far back as

14 June 1979. I refer to the fact that in His Honor's judgment

he said:

. one of the inspectors gave evidence that

the attention of officers of the Income Tax Department had

been directed. at the inspector's suggestion, by an officer of the

Corporate Affairs Office to a very large pile of documents

then standing in an enclosed but uncovered yard

Those documents were collected by officers of the Australian Taxation Office. They related to corporations engaged
in tax evasion. Is it a fact that no investigations were undertaken into these companies and the promoters of tax evasion schemes. even after the Australian Taxation Office had come
into possession of the documents? This occurred three years prior to the publication of the McCabe-Lafranchi report. Is it also a fact that nO investigations have been carried out with re-
spect to these companies and promoters since the publication of the McCabe-Lafranchi report"

The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Commissioner of Taxation has confirmed that a large volume of documents and financial records were obtained by his officers in May 1979 from an open area where the records had been, for some time. subjected to the prevailing weather conditions.

As a result of this exposure to the elements some of the
material was so badly damaged that it had to be destroyed. In addition, examination of the remaining documents and
records indicated that in most instances the documents and records of the companies were incomplete. Taxation officers were able to obtain better information regarding the profits and sales of shares in the companies from other sources. As is
evident from my comments of 27 June 1982 on the McCabeLafranchi report, taxation investigations of stripped companies were conducted on a test case basis, that is. action
in regard to the bulk of companies awaited the outcome of attempted proceedings in representative cases. Legal advice had been sought in respect of such cases as early as 1974.

A special prosecutor has recently been appointed with the specific task of prosecuting persons responsible for stripping companies with pre-tax profits. Charges have already been laid against several persons. Other cases are under
investigation.

It should not be overlooked that the recently-introduced Taxation (Unpaid Company Tax) Assessment Bill will apply to many of the companies for which records are held. The proposed legislation will require the former owners of
companies that were stripped of pre-tax profits and promoters of those arrangements to effect recoupment of the evaded tax.

Importation of Spanish Aircraft
Mr Howard~On 15 September 1982 (Hansard, page 1447) Mr Cunningham asked the Treasurer, without notice:
I asked on 24 August in relation to the importation of a Spanish aircraft by a Melbourne aviation company. At that time. I asked:
Did the Australian Taxation Office give a clearance to the importers to declare the aircraft to be new? Was the Taxation Office aware that the aircraft was declared at Customs as a demonstration aircraft? Is it a fact that the aircraft first flew in 1978? Is it a fact the aircraft was originally an armed forces aircraft in Spain? What criteria did the Taxation Office refer to in declaring the aircraft to be new"
The Treasurer said:
I am not in a position to answer that question today; I hope to be so tomorrow.
Is the Treasurer in a position to answer the question now? If not, why not?
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
As I indicated in answer to the question without notice on 15 September 1982, the Commissioner of Taxation has advised me that the provisions of section 16 of the Income Tax Assessment Act preclude him from divulging information in relation to the particular aviation company.
The Commissioner was able to say, however, that it is a feature of the investment allowance concession that it applies only to new property. The income tax law defines new to mean not having previously been either used by any person or acquired or held by any person for use by that person. Reconditioned or wholly or mainly reconstructed property does not qualify as new property. Whether property that has, before acquisition, been a demonstration model would qualify as new property would depend upon the degree of use to which the property had been put for demonstration purposes.
