Tax AYoidance Schemes
(Question No. 3784)
Mr Robert Brown asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 16 February 1982:
What was the estimated loss to taxation revenue as a result of the successful operation of tax avoidance schemes and arrangements which have been the subject of specific anti-tax avoidance legislation since 1975-76, from the time the schemes commenced to operate or were discovered until the time the legislation took effect.
Mr Howard~ The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
It is not possible to estimate the loss to taxation revenue that may result from the tax avoidance schemes referred to in the honourable member's question. The Commissioner of Taxation does not accept that the vast majority of schemes which were entered into in the period referred to by the honourable member have achieved the result of avoiding tax. However, taxpayers involved in the schemes which the Commissioner contends are ineffective have generally exercised their rights of objection and appeal. The resulting disputes will probably ultimately have to be resolved in the courts. As the Commissioner indicated in the 60th Report to Parliament, the matter of bringing before the courts cases representative of the various schemes is a long drawn out process which is frequently prolonged by unco-operative scheme promoters. This remains true notwithstanding that, as stated in the 6 Ist Report, cases representative of 28 kinds of scheme were heard or awaited hearing during 198 I-82.
Until the efficacy of the various schemes has been determined by the courts, it is not possible to say how much tax has in fact been avoided. Recent decisions of the courts which have upheld the Commissioner's rejection of some schemes indicates that other schemes are also likely to be held to be ineffective. To safeguard the revenue against the possibility of the courts ultimately finding in favour of the taxpayers, action has been taken to outlaw the various schemes as soon as full details have been obtained.
Accordingly, although there was an amount of $I,035m unpaid and further substantial amounts paid against disputed liability in respect of all identified tax avoidance schemes (other than those involving companies that have been stripped of their pre-tax profits) as at 8 October 1982, it is confidently expected that, when the judicial processes available to taxpayers involved in those schemes have been exhausted, the amount of revenue actually lost as a result of

3210 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

the schemes will be substantially less than the figure mentioned. It is also relevant that legislation has been introduced that will increase from 10 per cent per annum to 20 per cent per annum the penalty for late payment of income tax.
Tax Deductibility: Local Government Rates and Water Rates
(Question No. 4168)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on II March 1982:
What would be the cost of introducing full tax deductibility in respect of (a) local government rates. and (b) water rates.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The estimated loss of income tax revenue that would have resulted if full tax deductibility had been allowed in respect of (a) local government rates (i.e. general and sewerage rates and (b) basic water rates (excluding excess water rates) for the 1981-82 income year are $320m and $75m respectively.
In arriving at the above estimates it has been assumed that local government rates and taxes up to $300 would cease to form part of concessional expenditures the excess of which over $1,590 at present entitles a taxpayer to a rebate.
Sales Tax
(Question No. 4666)
Mrs Darling asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
(I) Did the Taxation Review Committee, which consisted of a number of this country's foremost tax experts, state on pages 19 and 20 of its report that the sales tax is a broad based tax.
(2) Did he recently state publicly that sales taxes are not regressive because higher rates of tax are imposed on luxury items than on necessities; if so, is he able to present statistical or other factual evidence to support this claim.
(3) Did he also state publicly that a broad based tax is regressive since the proportion of consumption to income normally falls as income increased.

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No. On page 19 of its Report the Taxation Review Committee stated:
'A distinction must be made here between "narrowbased" taxes falling upon only a few consumption goods and services (even though they be ones that absorb quite a significant proportion of total expenditure) and "broad based" taxes levied on very large ranges of goods and services even if not quite all consumption. The Australian sales and excise taxes are an obvious example of the former category; the British and Continental value-added taxes belong to the latter.'
(2) and (3) I have publicly stated that many commentators in asserting that the Australian sales tax is regressive overlook some significant features of the tax which offset the usually regressive impact of such taxes.
In the first place the rate structure is an important feature often overlooked. There are three rate classes, with rates of 7t, 20 and 32+ per cent. The 32t per cent class includes goods such as cameras, jewellery, television sets and record playing equipment. Items like household furniture and floor coverings, refrigerators, crockery and cutlery are only taxed at 7+ percent.
Even more significant, food, rent and power, on which the major proportion of lower incomes are spent, are not taxed nor has the Government proposed they be taxed. The sales tax is accordingly structured to offset regressive effects.
Taxation incidence studies rely upon family or household expenditure surveys for their basic data. Findings from such studies need to be treated with caution because of shortcomings in the surveys themselves, the absence of up-to-date information-the last household survey in Australia was in 1975-76 -and the need to make a number of broad assumptions (for example about the effect of the tax on consumption patterns) in deriving estimates of taxation incidence.
Subject to this important reservation, I am informed that a Treasury study, based on the 1975-76 ABS Household Expenditure Survey, found that a 2t per cent increase in tax on all goods subject to sales tax in the planned 1981-82 Budget would have had the following effects:

Proportion of gross income paid in tax

Average Weekly Household Income 1975-76

$080 $80-140 $140-200 $200-260 $260340

%

%

%

%

%

1.2

0.9

0.8

0.8

0.8

$340
% 0.7

The apparent regressive effect at the lowest income level has to be heavily qualified because there are statistical problems with the lowest income group. 12.2 per cent of households in this group received income as a result of selfemployment and many of those surveyed in 1975-76 had a significant, though temporary, reduction in income. Consumption for self-employed persons in this group was 3 times their income and probably reflects more closely income expectations or prior experience. If average income over a longer time period were taken into consideration these households would be placed in a higher income group. Mr Neil Warren of the University of NSW in fact stated in his study on incidence, the figures from which are often quoted by the Opposition, that 'it is therefore necessary to be guarded in the conclusions one reaches in respect of incidence estimates for the two lowest income groups'.

If the lowest income group were omitted it could be said that the changes to the sales tax arrangements proposed in the 1981-82 Budget would have had a roughly proportional effect on the bulk of taxpayers.
In a comprehensive incidence study it is not only necessary to examine how the tax was raised but also where the revenue was spent. The Asprey Taxation Review Committee suggested that the method generally used to calculate incidence, including the one used to calculate the above figures, overstate the regressive effect at low incomes. Income in the Household Expenditure Survey includes transfer payments received from the Government sector (for example by way of pension). The Asprey Committee suggested, however, that taxation incidence should be calculated by tax payments less transfer payments as a proportion of original income. This method of presentation reveals a significant redistribution in favour of the lowest income groups.

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3211

A recent OECD study has also indicated that general consumption taxes in member countries (given the way they operate in those countries) are not regressive but tend to be more or less proportional.
Royal Commission into the Builders Labourers' Federation
(Question No. 4692)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:
(I) When was the Royal Commission into the Builders Labourers' Federation established.
(2) What (a) have been the payments to date and (b) are the amounts outstanding to (i) the Royal Commissioner Mr Justice Winneke, Q.c., (ii) counsel assisting, Mr P. O'Callaghan, Q.c. and (iii) other counsel.
(3) What is the total estimated cost of the Royal Commission and related court cases (a) to date and (b) in the foreseeable future.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) 20 August 1981.
(2) The State of Victoria was responsible for the payments referred to, pursuant to arrangements between it and the Commonwealth.
(3) The cost of the Royal Commission was borne by Victoria. Related court cases which involved expenditure by the Commonwealth are the proceedings instituted by the BLF against Mr Winneke, the State of Victoria and the Commonwealth and a number of criminal prosecutions against officials of the BLF for breaches of the Royal Commissions Act 1902. The breaches were substantially in relation to refusals to answer questions at hearings of the Royal Commission in Canberra and Perth. Payments to Counsel in respect of the first mentioned proceedings totalled $20,545. Accounts have not yet been received from Counsel conducting the criminal prosecutions. Total Counsel's fees in respect of all the prosecutions are estimated to be $25,000.
Bankcard Charges
(Question No. 4700)
Mr Jacobi asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:
(I) Is it a fact that Bankcard charges up to 5t per cent on every transaction purchase with Bankcard, and this charge is levied from the retail outlets and others who accept the card for purchases so that, if a cardholder buys something worth $100, the bank takes up to $5.50 and only pays the retailer $94.50.
(2) Is it also a fact that many bankcard holders pay off their debts within a month, or at the most 2 months, of buying the goods or services on their card so that the period of credit actually given to the cardholder in these cases is only I or perhaps 2 months.
(3) If so, is it a fact that (a) the bank can then advance the credit to someone else and again collect up to 5t per cent on the next transaction, (b) the same credit may be turned over up to 10 or 12 times in a year and the corresponding fees, of up to 5t per cent, charged every time or on every transaction, and (c) the true rate of return to Bankcard can therefore be as high as 66 per cent per annum on the $100 committed, that is $5.50 x 12 turnovers.
(4) Will the Government (a) take action against the banks to prevent them making these disguised profits at will and (b)

initiate a thorough investigation into Bankcard and other credit card systems, such as has been conducted in New Zealand recently, to assess the true profits made under these schemes and to evaluate the impact of the credit card revolution on our monetary system.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I am advised that Bankcard banks levy a merchant service fee on the sellers of goods and services where sales have been financed by Bankcard. The fee is a service charge designed to cover, inter alia, the administrative costs incurred by the Bankcard organisation in processing transactions and the administrative and other costs (including provision for bad debts) incurred by participating banks in relation to Bankcard. The size of the charge to individual merchants is a matter for each bank to determine in negotiation with its customers. However, I am advised that the charges commonly fall in the range of It per cent to 5t per cent of the merchant's total Bankcard turnover, depending on his monthly volume of Bankcard sales and the average value of his Bankcard transactions.
(2) I am advised that it is not uncommon for Bankcard users to repay their outstanding balance within their period of charge free credit, which, depending on the juxtaposition of the date of purchase and the monthly billing date, may extend to nearly two months. However, many Bankcard users do not repay as promptly as this; the available data for amounts financed and advances outstanding under Bankcard (including cash advances) suggest that, on average, balances remain outstanding for around five months.
(3) It is possible for the set of transactions hypothesised in the question to occur under certain very restrictive assumptions about dates of purchase, dates of payment and details of the merchant Bankcard transactions. The resulting hypothetical fee income of $66 per annum for each $100 of revolving credit would represent revenue earned.
Profits to the Banks concerned would be substantially less than the revenue figures cited because, as noted in (I), the merchant service fee is a charge for services rendered and the cost of providing those services needs to be taken into account.
(4) The authorities carefully examined the operations of the Bankcard scheme prior to its introduction in 1974. Also, in July 1980 the Trade Practices Commission completed a detailed study of the operation of the Bankcard scheme. As a result, a number of anti-competitive elements in the agreements between member banks in the scheme were amended, including to provide that each bank must take its own decision on charges it makes to merchants and cardholders and on the period of free credit it allows cardholders. Restrictive conditions on the participation to the Bankcard scheme were also removed.

Sports Development Program
(Question No. 4753)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to concern in the community that this country's 6 million registered sports participants are not being provided for in current Federal Government allocations, particularly in respect of the Sports Development Program.
(2) Has the Government considered direct allocations to be made to individual sporting bodies to encourage these bodies to program for the health and fitness of the Australian nation.
(3) Has the Government any tax policy that would allow tax rebates for large organisations that agree to underwrite or assist these programs.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) In the 1982-83 Budget, an amount of $3.7m was provided for Sports Assistance Programs. This Program incorporates, inter alia, what was previously the Sports Development Program. Of the $3.7m, $3.2m is for grants to national sporting organisations. The amount provided for this purpose in 1981-82 was $2.9m.
(2) The question of criteria for allocating grants to particular sporting organisations is the responsibility of my colleague, the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, who is advised on this matter by the Sports Advisory Council.
(3) Section 51 of the Income Tax Assessment Act, the general deduction provision, authorises deductions for outgoings incurred in producing assessable income or necessarily incurred in carrying on a business for the purpose of gaining such income, except to the extent that the outgoings are of a capital, private or domestic nature. Businesses that sponsor or assist sporting programs may, therefore, be able to claim deductions for expenditure so incurred where it can be established, to the Commissioner's satisfaction, that an appropriate nexus (involving for example, publicity or goodwill) exists between the expenditure and the derivation of assessable income.
Independent Audit: Department of Employment and Industrial Relations
(Question No. 4782)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.

Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The following audit procedures apply to grants made by the Department to non-government organisations:
(a) The organisation is required to submit to the Department financial statements either on an annual basis or within a specified period.
(b) The statement must be audited by a qualified public accountant who is not an officer, member or employee of the organisation.
(c) Continued payment of on-going grants is subject to the receipt of vouchers or audited statements for the previous period.
(d) The organisation must certify that the conditions for which the grant has been made have been met.
Recipients of Bureau of Labour Market Research sponsored research grants are required to provide statements of expenditure fully supported by receipts or vouchers in lieu of independent audit requirement at (b).

Sales Tax ExemptioQs: Fermented Fruit Juices
(Question No. 5021)
Dr Everingham asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 8 September 1982:
(I) Did the Minister Assisting the TreasIJrer in March 1977 acknowledge thaI exemption from sale, lax would be reviewed for ferm~nled fruil juices other than grape wine when further sales tax exemptions were being considered as a result of previous requests and the representations of the Member for Wide Bay on behalf of Mr J. Gianduzzo who, since 1972 and trading as Tropical Wines in Bundaberg, Queensland, has annually produced 8,200 gallons of nongrape wines unique in Australia.
(2) Has the Government increased sales tax twice since 1975011 these products, from 15 percent to 20 percent, despite Mr Gianduzzo's appeals each time, while leaving grape wine and 2.pple cider exempt each time.
(3) Did the Government reject these representations in framing the 1978-79 budget for fiscal reasons without giving any other justification for the inequity of the position.
(4) Has Mr Gianduzzo co-operated fully with Australian Taxation Office inquiries as to his coHs, income and lUrnover, and established to their satisfaction that he is providing an attraction to tourists, including those spending foreign currency, and producing a produ,t which conforms with standards required for grape wines including alcohol content.
(5) Is he able to say wh~ther the Gianduzzo family has worked efficiently from 8 am to 7 pm, 7 days a week, 363 days a year for a return well below that of sugar mill, bar. retailing or ot"er award workers so that their enterprise is threatened while they still sell quality products at prices lower before sales tax, but higher after sales tax, than those of large wine enterprises enjoying to~al sales tax exemption.
(6) Did he '"gain promise a review when sales taxes were next reviewed in his repl) to me of 14 January 1982 and acknowledgr the existence of anomalies a~d the need to correct them carefully to avoid creating more unspecified anomalies and to cons:der other unspecified factors.
(7) Has the Government's review on ,~ach occasion since 1977 aggravated the anomalies, th~ existence of which it acknowledges, by ir.creasing sales tax on the products of tropkal wines but not on grape wine and apple cider.
(8) Has the Government exempted from sales tax under item 36 of the First Schedule, Divisior. VI of the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Act: stout; cider; nonaicoho:ic fruit juice bever::.ges; vermcuths and aperitifs; apricot and other fruit juice essence cocktails; manhattan cocktail anct martin. cocktail made by McWilliams Wine Pty Ltd; apricot, banana, cherry, maraschino and pineapple wine cocktails ma<1e by Chateau Rosevale; banana and prunell;; cocktails made by Hamiltolls Ewell Vineyards Pty Ltd; Brandivino, an Australian wine with imported additIves; Norman Golden Glow apricot, cherfY and pineapple cocktails, and Stonyfell apricot 31ld cherry wine cocktails.
(9) Will he (a) give Mr Gianduzzo reasons for this discrimination, (b) define any new anomalies that could possibly arise if a prompt favorable determination was made and (c) respond celevantly to my request of 5 February 1982 for definition of the other factors to be considered.

Mr HowardThe answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes, but the increase in sales tax on fruit wines resulted from general across-the-board increases in rates. In the 1981 and 1982 Budgets all sales tax rates were increased by 2.5 percentage points so that goods previously taxable at 15 percent became taxable at 17.5 percent as from 19 August 1981 and taxable at 20 percent as from 18 August 1982. As fermented fruit juices were amongst those goods affected by the general across-the-board increase in rates it was not practicable to consider their position in isolation from all other goods that were previously taxable at the same rate as fermented fruit juices.
These general increases in rates did not apply to exempt goods such as grape wine and apple cider produced in Australia. However, the rate of tax on imported grape wine and cider increased in line with locally produced fermented fruit juices.
(3) In framing tile 1978-79 Budget the Government gave consideration to a wide range of representations for tax relief or other form of assistance. It was not a question of the Government rejecting these representations including those of Mr Gianduzzo but whether in the context of the overall Budget it was possible to grant some form of tax relief and, if so. what form it was to take.
(4) The Commissioner of Taxation informs me that he is not aware of any lack of co-operation on the part of Mr Gianduzzo.
(5) I am not personally aware of Mr Gianduzzo's work situation but the point has been made to me in representations on the matter.
(6) Yes.
(7) I have explained the Government's position in my answer to question 2.
(8) The goods referred to relate to decisions made by the Commissioner of Taxation under item 36 and are contained in the pubiication titled Sales Tax Exemptions and Classifications.
(9) (a) There is no discrimination in favour of the products listed in question 8 as against those produced by M r Gianduzzo. With the exception of stout and cider, all of the alcoholic products ruled to be exempt are made principally of grape wine and references to apricot, banana, cherry pineapple incicate the kind of flavourings added. These products are noi fruit wines of the kind produced by Mr Gianduzzo. Stout is exempt under item 36 (I), being a beer while cider is specifically exempted by item 36 (2). Wines produced from fruits other than the grape or from other products such as honey (mead) or rice (saki) are taxable.
(b) If fruit wines were to be exempted, the exemption would have to apply to both the imported and local product to avoid infringing the GATT This could have repercussions in relation to the discrimination between local wines and overseas wines and also the existing sales tax discrimination betw~en beer and cider of local and overseas origin. Those of Australian origin were exempted prior to Australia becoming a party to the GATT The exemption of imported products would reduce the element of protection at present enjoyed by Australian beer, wine and cider because oftlleir exemption.
(c) Other factors that need to be tak~n into account by the Government include the overall incidence of taxation including excise uut), customs duty and income tax and the general economic position of the industry. Wine producers, for example, incurred substantial income tax liabilities as a result of a change in the valuation of wine stocks some years ago and are only now recovering from that change.

Australian Atomic Energy Commission: Reprocessing of Aluminium Fuel Elements
(Question No. 5160)
Mr Uren asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 22 September 1982:
(1) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to pages 51 and 52 of the Australian Atomic Energy Commission's annual report for 1965-66 where it is stated that it had been necessary to reprocess several hundred aluminium fuel elements containing a total of one kilogram of uranium 233 used it' physics experiments; that some of these elements had become swollen and defective and all were becoming difficult to handle because of the increasing radiation levels due to daughter products of the contained uranium 233; that, a small laboratoryscale recycle facility was built and operated to produce decontaminated uranium dioxide for refabrication into new fuel elements, and that reprocessing and reconversion had been completed with a loss of approximately 1 per cent of uranium 233.
(2) In view of the Minister's answer to Question No. 4469 (Hansard, 18 August 1982, pages 669-70), what were the physics experiments in which the aluminium fuel elements referred to in part (1) were used.
(3) What volume of material has been reprocessed and reconverted in the recycle facility referred to in part (1). or any other facility, at Lucas Heights, NSW.
(4) What is the volume and nature of any materials (a) extracted and (b) stockpiled as a result of reprocessing and reconversion of this kind.
(5) What is the volume and nature of any waste materials produced and what practice was adopted for the disposal of material of this kind.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) Yes.
(2) The answer to Question No. 4469 referred specifically to spent fuel elements from the HIFAR reactor at Lucas Heights and has no bearing on this question.
The physics experiments referred to in the AAEC's Annual Report for 1965-66 were part of a program initiated in 1963-64 to obtain basic neutron data on the behaviour of various fuel moderator mixtures in high temperature gas cooled reactors. These experiments were also referred to in the AAEC's Annual Reports for 1963-64 (pages 34, 35) and 1964-65 (pages 38 and 40).
For the purpose of these experiments, one kg of U233, containing small amounts of U232 as an impurity, was obtained on lease from the USA and fabricated into fuel elements. Two sets of experiments were carried out, using the AAEC's MOATA reactor. In the first, sub-critical fuel assemblies (simulating conditions in a full, cold, reactor core) provided a means to check nuclear data and test methods of computing reactor core characteristics. In the second, samples representative of reactor core composition and sufficiently large to establish the reactor's nuclear properties were placed within MOAT A to measure neutron reaction rates.
(3) See (2). Initially the one kg of U233 was fabricated into 205 fuel elements, each containing an average of 4.85 grams of U233.
Fission product activity in the U233 was negligible because all experiments were carried out at extremely low level fission rates. However, as the decay of U232 present in the U233 resulted in the growth of daughter products and increasing

levels of gamma radiation, and also because during the experiments a small number of fuel elements had suffered damage, in 1966 all these fuel elements were processed to remove the radioactivity arising from the decay of U232 and the recovered uranium oxide was refabricated. 177 fuel elements were fabricated, averaging 5.05 grams of U233, as well as 20 half plates and 2 quarter plates. In 1967 the entire batch of elements was processed a second time in order to reduce the radioactivity arising from the growth of the decay products from U232, so that the material could be conveniently transported to the USA at the termination of the lease.
There has been no other reprocessing and reconversion of fuel clements at this or any other facility at Lucas Heights. The facility used for processing the U233 has been dismantled.
(4) (a) Overall recovery of uranium present after dissolution was about 98 per cent.
(b) None of the U233 was stockpiled: it was returned to the lessor in 1967.
(5) Solid waste containing 3,45g of U233 was obtained from treatment of processing solutions, and a further 3.34g of U233 are stored in solution. The solid waste has been placed in storage facilities at Lucas Heights. Contaminated solid materials such as gloves, tissues, equipment, aluminium powder and graphite containing 16.06g of U233 are also held in storage facilities at Lucas Heights, whilst contaminated materials containing a further 4.04g of U233 were disposed of in the Little Forest burial ground prior to its closure in 1968.

(4) The rationale varies with operating requirements and equipment available in the various locations. Factors involved include:
Those very senior officers who have a need for guaranteed access to the telephone network at all times;
Where installed equipment is STD barred and officers have a demonstrated need for STD access at times when the switchboard is not staffed;
Officers with a regular requirement for access to the telephone network at times when a manual switchboard is not staffed;
Operational requirements dictate a need (paging beeper for Buildings Services Officer).

(5) (a) $4,646, (b) $19,719, (c; $401

(6) No.

Income Tax Appeals
(Question No. 5198)
Mr Maher asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 23 September 1982:
(I) How many income tax appeals are pending in each State before (a) a Board of Review, or (b) a court.

(2) How many requests for a reference or appeal are awaiting consideration by the Commissioner of Taxation or a Deputy Commissioner.
(3) What is the anticipated delay in having an appeal heard before a Board of Review.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The position in respect of income tax appeals as at 30 June 1982 was as follows:

(a) Boards of ReviewHeard awaiting decision Part heard or adjourned Awaiting hearing
(b) CourtsAwaiting decision Awaiting hearing

(2) There were 25,569 requests for reference to Boards of Review and 1,615 appeals to courts (representing less than 0.4 per cent of annual issues of assessment) on hand in the Australian Taxation Office as at 30 June 1982. Of those cases, 6,612 references and 602 appeals were deferred either at the taxpayer's request or pending the outcome of decisions in other cases or pending the outcome of enquiries or correspondence already in course. Of the remaining cases only a small proportion will need to be referred to the Boards of Review or the Courts. The vast majority will be resolved in discussions between the taxpayers concerned and the Australian TaxationOffice.
(3) The period which can elapse between the receipt by the Taxation Office of a request for reference to a Board of Review and the time when the matter is heard by the Board varies in each case due to a variety of factors. At present there are three Boards of Review. No. I Board is based in Sydney, No.2 in Melbourne and No.3 in Brisbane. Each Board also travels to other capitals and (as the need arises) to provincial cities and towns. Because of this it frequently happens that the delay in hearing cases in the less populous States is less than it is in the 'home' cities of the respective Boards. For these and other reasons it is extremely difficult to make an accurate forecast of the time it may take to have a case heard by a Board of Review. However, based on the historical annual output of the three Boards (and excluding deferred cases) it is estimated that as at 30 June 1982, No. I Board had approximately 18 months work on hand, No.2 Board had about 28 months and No.3 Board about 9 months.
Members oCParliament: Tax Evasion
(Question No. 5359)
Mrs Darling asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 19 October 1982:

Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The conduct of members of parliament in relation to their personal financial affairs is a matter for members and senators themselves.
The Report of the Committee of Inquiry concerning Public Duty and Private Interest recommends a framework of arrangements for dealing with the public duty and private interests of members of parliament.
The Government has announced that it supports the thrust of the Bowen Report. It will shortly be announcing its specific proposals in relation to members of parliament. All members of parliament are aware of the contents of the report.

General Aviation Study was publicly released by then Minister for Transport on 13 March 1981 on the basis that there was no Government commitment to its recommendations pending industry consideration of the report and consultation on its cost recovery recommendation through the Aviation Industry Advisory Council (AVIAC). On 16July 1981, the Minister announced that the Government had decided not to take up the cost recovery recommendations of the Study. The non-cost recovery recommendations, other than those dealing with airport traffic priorities and noise abatement, were subsequently referred to AVIAC for consideration. Those matters are currently under review by the council. On the basis of the basic thrust of the Holcroft recommendations, the Government substantially revised the air fare formula for TAA and Ansett on I April 1981. A study undertaken by Price Waterhouse Associates Pty was commissioned in July 1981 to further address some cost allocation issues not finalised by the Holcroft Inquiry. The Government subsequently established the Independent Air Fares Committee which is now solely responsible for the setting and determination of domestic airfares for all regular public transport passenger operators. The powers of the Minister to approve domestic air fares previously held under Air Navigation Regulation 106 were repealed upon commencement of the Independent Air Fares committee Act 1981.

National Crimes Commission
(Question No. 4073)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 24 February 1982:
(I) Does he recall my repeated questions relating to the urgent need to set up a National Crimes Commission and his repeated rejection of this proposal.
(2) Is it a fact that Commonwealth responsibility for crime is divided between his Department, the Attorney-Genera!'s Department and the Department of Business and Consumer Affairs.
(3) If so, will he take immediate steps to co-ordinate the respective responsibilities under one authority.
(4) Is there a need for combined Federal/State initiatives in crime prevention; if so, will he give urgent consideration to the possibility of special powers being given to a composite Federal/State body to study and investigate organised corporate, white collar and international crime with a view to developing model laws and practices which could lead to a national approach to overcome the problem.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) to (4) The Commonwealth Government has, for some time, recognised the need for combined Federal/State initiatives in the enforcement and prevention of crime.
On 28 April 1982, in answer to a question without notice on this subject, the Prime Minister stated that the Government was considering the possible establishment of a Crimes Commission to tackle the problems posed by organised crime (Hansard page 1927). The issue of a Crimes Commission has also been taken up with the States through the Premiers' Conference, the Australian Police Ministers' Council and the Standing Committee of Attorneys-General.
On 21 October 1982 the Acting Attorney-General introduced, into this House, a Bill to set up the National Crimes Commission. The Bill contains several mechanisms to facilitate joint investigations by the Commonwealth, States and Northern Territory. I am hopeful that the States and Northern Territory will take this opportunity to participate in joint inquiries as appropriate.
Fluoridation of Water
(Question No. 4829)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3221

(I) Has his attention been drawn to the report of a Committee to the Quebec Minister of the Environment on 30 November 1979.
(2) If so, does the report confirm the mutagenicity of fluoridated water, with increased cancer mortality, and recommend suspension of fluoridation and further evaluation of the risks.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) No. The report contains no convincing evidence that fluoridation of water increases mutagenicity and mortality from cancer. It recommends the suspension of a Bill dealing with compulsory fluoridation in Quebec until fluoridation has been subjected to further investigation.
With regard to mutagenicity, the extensive report of the Committee of Inquiry into the Fluoridation of Victorian Water Supplies (1980) summarises reports of studies in Britain in 1958 and 1962, and in the United States of America in 1974 and 1976, which show no association between water fluoridation and the incidence of congenital malformations. Details of a further investigation in England, which also shows no such association, were published in 'Community Medicine': 2.p.190, 1980.
The allegation of a link between fluoridation of water and cancer led to a number of investigations all of which show no association between fluoridation and cancer. These investigations were carried out in the United States of America, United Kingdom, Canada, New Zealand and Australia (New South Wales).
An investigation throughout Canada which was conducted by the Canadian Department of National Health and Welfare was completed in 1978. It was concluded that '. . . the maintenance of the fluoride level of potable water at approximately I mg/L is the most efficient procedure to decrease the incidence of dental caries, and such a level of fluoride poses no risk to the health of Canadians'.
Fluoridation of water in Quebec is continuing though it is no longer mandatory.

United States of America Ports: Access to Nuclear-armed and/or Nuclear-powered Ships
(Question No. 5163)
Mr Milton asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 23 September 1982:
(I) Is he able to say whether any cities in the United States of America deny access to their ports to nudear armed and/or nucl~ar-powered ships; jf so, (a) what cities. and (b) on what grounds is access denied.
(2) Is he also able to say whether any countries involved in the NATO alliance refuse access to their ports to nuclearpowered and/or nuclear-armed ships; if so. (a) which countries and (b) on what grounds is access denied.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) 1 am informed that no cities in the Unit~d States of America deny access to their ports by United States Navy ships in the categories mentioned. However, some ports in the Un:ted Stat~s are not visited by conventional or nuclearpowered warships either because there is no operational requirement to do so or because of particular port characteristics such as channel depth, accessibility, port services, and so on. These requirements are assessed by national. not local authorities. The criteria employed by the United States authorities in deciding visits by United States Navy nuclearpowered ships to United States domestic ports are identical with those employed for vis:ts to foreign ports.
(2) I am informed that no NATO country imposes a blanket ban on visits by nuclear-powered warships. Requests for port entry are handled bilaterally between the governments concerned on a case-by-case basis employing safety and environmental criteria corresponding to those introduced by Australia in 1976. Visits by nuclear-armed ships are permitted by most NATO countries. However. I understand that Denmark and Norway maintain policies of not permitting the stationing of nuclear weapons in their territory in peace time and look to other governments to respect their nuclear policies. The United States. Britain and France follow a policy of neither confirming nor denying the presence of nuclear weapons in particular ships.

Department of Aviation: Re-establishment
(Question No. 5219)
Mr Morris asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 12 October 1982:
What were the reasons for the re-establishment of a Department of Aviation, particularly in the light of his steadfast opposition to the creation of such a portfolio from November 1975 to May 1982.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The previous Department of Civil Aviation was abolished in the Administrative Arrangements Order of 30 November 1973 during the time of the previous administration.
The re-establishment of an Aviation Department has been under consideration for some time and recognises the importance of civil aviation to the economy and to the community at large.
Commonwealth Employees (Employment Provisions) Act: Use of Suspension Provisions
(Question No. 5262)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister Assisting the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 13 October 1982:
(I) On how many occasions, and in what departments, have the suspension provisions of the Commonwealth Employees (Employment Provisions) Act 1977 been implemented since the Act came into operation.
(2) How many persons have been suspended under the Act and for what length of time was each suspended.

3224 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) The Public Service Board, having consulted with the Department of Employment and [ndustrial Relations, has informed me that the suspension provisions of the Commonwealth Employees (Employment Provisions) Act 1977 have been used nine times in Commonwealth employment. The details of the use of the Act are as follows:
(a) Some 120 members of the Australian Telecommunications Employees' Association employed in the Australian Telecommunications Commission, were suspended from duty for the period 13-17 July 1979 for implementing work bans in support of a wage claim.
(b) 1132 Transport Workers' Union members were suspended in the period 21-31 July 1981 in the Departments of Administrative Services, Capital Territory, Defence, Housing and Construction, National Development and Energy, and Transport. The suspensions, which were issued over industrial action taken in support of a pay claim, were subsequently cancelled.
(c) 638 members of the Administrative and Clerical Officers' Association and 343 members of the Australian Public Service Association (Fourth Division Officers) employed in the Australian Telecommunications Commission were suspended from duty for the period 22-26 July 1981 in connection with the implementation of work bans in support of a pay claim.
(d) 480 members of the Licensed Aircraft Engineers' Association employed in Trans-Australia Airlines were suspended from duty for the period 14-17 August 1981 in connection with strike action taken in support of a pay claim.
(e) Approximately 90 broadcasting engineering officers employed in the Australian Broadcasting Commission were suspended from 14 to 17 August 1981 for engaging in strike action over a pay claim. [n addition, some 230 other employees in the Australian Broadcasting Commission were stood down during the course of the dispute because they could not be gainfully employed.
(f) 26 members of the Australian Telecommunications Employees' Association employed in the Australian Telecommunications Commission at the Haymarket Exchange were suspended from 2-14 December 1981 for implementing work bans in connection with a staffing dispute. 13 other staff at the Exchange were stood down during the course of the dispute.
(g) During the period 7-17 December 1981, a total of 451 members of the Administrative and Clerical Officers' Association employed in the Department of Social Security were suspended for implementing work bans in support of staffing claims. Suspensions were progressively lifted from II December and the final 414 were lifted on 17 December 1981.
(h) Some 640 Transport Workers' Union members were suspended from duty in the period 11-24 December 1981 for engaging in industrial action in support of a pay claim. The Departments of the Capital Territory, Administrative Services and Housing and Construction were affected, as were the Australian Capital Territory Electricity Authority and the Capital Territory Health Commission.
(i) Approximately 2240 members of the Australian Capital Territory Teachers' Federation employed in the Commonwealth Teaching Service were suspended from duty during the period 12-17 March 1982 for engaging in industrial action in support of a pay claim.

Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement Act: Voluntary Retirement Provisions
(Question No. 5263)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister Assisting the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 13 October 1982:
What sum has been spent in payments under the voluntary retirement provisions of the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act 1979, to staff affected by the recommendations of the Review of Commonwealth Functions involving the transfer to the private sector of functions previously performed in the Australian Public Service.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Public Service Board has advised me that Section 17 of the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act allows staff of the Australian Public Service to retire voluntarily at any time after they attain 55 years of age. The Board also advised that the Act makes no provision for any payments to such staff although those who are superannuation contributors receive the entitlements due to them under the Superannuation Act.
Visual Display Units: Department of Defence

United Nations Conference on Disarmament: Visas for Delegates
(Question No. 5347)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 19 October 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to problems some Australian delegates had in obtaining visas to attend the recent United Nations conference on Disarmament in New York, USA.
(2) Did he receive any requests for assistance in obtaining visas for delegates.
(3) [f so, what approach was made to the US Government and what was the response.
(4) [s he able to say whether the Australian delegate's applications for visas were treated in the same way as any other application.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3225

(I) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) No approach was made to the United States Government. Under the terms of the Headquarters Agreement signed between the United States and the United Nations, the United States undertook not to impose any impediment on the transit to the United Nations Headquarters district of representatives of non-governmental organisations recognised by the United Nations for the purpose of consultations under Article 71 of the Charter. Those Australians and other individuals refused visas to the United States were not representatives of such organisations.
In these circumstances the issue of visas is a matter for the United States to decide. It would not be appropriate for the Australian Government to suggest to the United States the manner in which they should exercise their responsibilities.
(4) The Government has not reason to believe that applications for visas by Australian citizens were treated any differently from those lodged by citizens of other countries.

Mrs Darling asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 20 October 1982:
What was the total cost of the visit to Australia in September and October 1982 by The Queen and The Duke of Edinburgh to open officially the XII Commonwealth Games in Brisbane and the National Gallery in Canberra.

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
All charges for the visit have not yet been brought to account but direct costs are estimated to be in the order of $778,000. Salaries and travel costs of departmental staff involved with the visit as part of their normal duties and costs incurred by other departments in carrying out their normal functions in relation to the visit are not included. In accordance with long-standing practice, charges related to the use of RAAF 34 Squadron aircraft are brought to account under the specific appropriation for use of special purpose aircraft and have also been excluded.

Budget Appropriations
(Question No, 5601)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister representing the Minister for Finance, upon notice, on 9 November 1982:

Mr Howard-The Minister for Finance has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
For the purpose of this question the term 'Budget appropriations' has been interpreted to mean appropriations contained in the Appropriation Acts (Nos I and 2) 1982-83 and the Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83.
A reconciliation between estimated Budget outlays in 1982-83 as shown on page 64 of Statement No.3 of Budget Paper No. I, 'Budget Statements 1982-83' ($47,067.2m) and appropriations of the Consolidated Revenue Fund (CRF)- dissected to identify amounts to be appropriated by Special Appropriations and Appropriation Acts (Nos I and 2) 1982-83 and the Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83 is detailed in Tables I and 2 of Budget Paper No.5, 'Estimates of Receipts and Summary of Estimated Expenditure For The Year Ending 30 June 1983'.
The Appropriation Acts (Nos I and 2) 1982-83 and the Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83 contain appropriations aggregating $13,986.4m. After adjustments have been made for:
allowance for prospective wage and salary increases (add $320m);
CRF expenditure from Budget appropriations but classified as financing transactions in Budget Statements (subtract $1.2m);
CRF expenditure from Budget appropriations but netted against receipts in Budget Statements (subtract $27.1 m);
Advances to the Minister for Finance and to the Presiding Officers (subtract $225.6m);
estimated expenditure in 1982-83 from Budget appropriations forming part of total outlays amounts to $14,052. 5m.

Estimated expenditure from permanent and special appropriations in 1982-83 by way of appropriation of the CRF ($31,648.4m) and Loan Fund ($2,670.3m) amounts to $34,318.7m. After adjustments have been made for:
allowance for cost escalation in education grants (add $1 lO.4m);
CRF expenditure from special appropriations but netted against receipts in Budget Statements (add $1 ,674.1 m);
CRF expenditure from special appropriations but classified as financing transactions in Budget Statements (subtract $892.9m);
estimated Defence expenditure chargeable to the Loan Fund (subtract $547.4m);
net increases in balance of Trust Accounts through which outlays are channelled (subtract $349.7m);
estimated expenditure from permanent and special appropriations forming part of total outlays amounts to $34,31 3.2m.
The two totals for Budget appropriations and permanent and special appropriations in the above tables do not add to total outlays listed in Statement 3 as account must be taken of CRF receipts offset against outlays ($1,298.7m)-an amount identified in Tables I and 2 of Budget Paper No.5.

Commonwealth Funding for Pre-schools
(Question No. 5634)
Dr Theophanous asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 9 November 1982:
(I) Has the 'block grant' method of Commonwealth funding for pre-schools resulted in an effective shortfall of over $15m in 1981-82.

3228 REPRESENTATIVES 8 December 1982

Answers to Questions

(2) Will the Minister undertake to review the sum currently made available for pre-schooling with a view to increasing it substantially.
(3) Can the Minister also give an assurance that funding for pre-schooling will be adequately indexed in future.
Mr Wilson-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I), (2) and (3) The Commonwealth has accepted the view, put with considerable emphasis by the States, that the basic responsibility for pre-school education lies with the States. The Commonwealth recognises the importance of preschools and provides financial support to supplement the States' own pre-school programs. This financial support, which is additional to the general purpose revenue grants, is provided to the State Governments in the form of a block grant and may be applied by the States according to their own assessment of priorities. The general purpose revenue grants have increased by 82 per cent since 1978-79. The block grant allocation for 1983-84 will be considered in the context of the 1983-84 Budget.

Tax Concessions for Interest on Home Loans
(Question No. 4029)
Mr Uren asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 24 February 1982:
(I) In view of the calls of some State Premiers for the Federal Government to exempt interest paid on home loans from assessable income tax paid by individuals and the Government's investigation of options for relieving the home loan repayment burdens placed on current home buyers as a consequence of high interest rates, has the Government estimated the revenue which would be forgone in 1980-81 by tax exemptions on:
(a) full mortgage interest payments of all home purchasers,
(b) full mortgage interest payments of first home buyers and

(c) one-third of the mortgage interest payments of first home buyers whose assessable income is less than
$20,000.

(2) If so, what are those estimates.

(3) If not, will he have such estimates made and provide
them to the Parliament to facilitate informed debate about the options for relieving home loan repayment burdens.

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) to (3) As the honourable member will be aware the Government introduced legislation earlier this year to assist
home buyers who first occupied a home after I July 1977. The scheme provides a rebate on home loan interest payments in
the first year of occupancy up to a limit of $700 where there is a dependent child or dependent student or $500 where there is
no dependent child or dependent student, the rebate entitlement reducing by $100 per year over the succeeding
four years.

Following on a further decision announced in the Budget, more recent legislation has extended the home loan interest rebate to others in respect of interest that exceeds 10 per cent per annum on up to $60,000 of a loan. The rebate under both schemes is at the standard rate of tax and applies to home interest payments after I July 1982. Where a taxpayer meets the criteria for a rebate under both schemes the scheme which confers the larger rebate will apply.
The full-year cost to income tax revenue of allowing deductions in respect of home loan interest payments is estimated as follows:

Small Arms Factory, Lithgow
(Question No. 4341)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence Support, upon notice, on 21 April, 1982:
(I) In view of the import restrictions that prevent the importation of military-type automatic rifles, why is the Small Arms Factory at Lithgow. N.S.W., engaged in a sales promotion program designed to sell similar weapons to the Australian public.
(2) Were these weapons manufactured for the Department of Defence.
(3) How many weapons is it proposed to sell to Australians.
(4) Does the Government consider the proliferation of this type of weapons in the public interest.
(5) What is the availability of ammunition for this type of weapon.
Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Answers to Questions

8 December 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 3229

(1) The Small Arms Factory at Lithgow is not engaged in a sales promotion program designed to sell military-type automatic rifles to the Australian public. Since 1978 sales of selfloading rifles have been permitted on the request of members of the public only when the buyer has proven authorisation under relevant State or Territorial laws to buy and own such a weapon.
(2) No.
(3) As from 1 September 1982 rifles can only be made available for sale to registered rifle club members. Their future requirements are not known, however I would estimate the quantity to be less than 30 per annum.
(4) Gun control legislation is vested in the States and Territory and public interest is more correctly expressed through State Legislature. The Government does not consider that sales of rifles to registered rifle club members represents a proliferation of this type of weapon.
(5) Ammunition manufactured to military specification in Government factories is made available only to members of the National Rifle Association and to licenced dealers.
Taxation: Deduction for Soil Conservation Expenditure
(Question No, 4704)
Mr Jacobi asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:
Further to his answer to a question without notice (Hansard, 5 May 1982, page 2232) where he stated that the Government had legislated to allow expenditure undertaken by an individual farmer on soil conservation to be deducted in the year of expenditure instead of over 10 years, does the tax deduction referred to also apply to laser land forming in view of the fact that it is an important soil and water conservation measure in certain irrigation areas; if not, why not.
Mr Howard-The answer to the' honourable member's question is as follows:
The question of income tax deductions allowable for expenditure incurred by a primary producer on soil conservation is governed by section 750 of the Income Tax Assessment Act. Section 750 authorises an immediate deduction for the cost which a farmer incurs in constructing surface or sub-surface drainage works for the purpose of controlling salinity or assisting in drainage control on farmland, except where the work consists of the drainage of a swamp or low-lying land.
Land forming or levelling in irrigation areas to improve drainage of irrigation water, whether or not such work is carried out using laser equipment, is generally regarded as being for the purpose of controlling or combating salinity in the land or a potential salinity problem and, therefore, eligible for immediate deduction under section 750.

Queensland: Expected Population Growth
(Question No. 4927)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 24 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a statement by the Australian Conservation Foundation and Queensland Conservation Council that estimates of south-east Queensland's expected population growth were significantly underestimated.
(2) What estimate of growth does his Department use in planning the future demand for Telecom Australia services.
(3) Have several exchanges in south-east Queensland installed extra exchange equipment with a 1,000 line capacity to cope with demand pressures; if so, how long is this new equipment expected to meet the demand.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question, based on advice from the Australian Telecommunications Commission, is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The future demand for Telecom services is monitored for individual exchange areas. In South-east Queensland the percentage rate of growth varies from 8 per cent in areas such as Toowoomba to 14 per cent in areas such as the Nambour District.
(3) In South-east Queensland 41 exchanges were increased by 1,000 or more line capacity during 1981-82. Typical examples are Mount Gravatt, Surfers Paradise, Noosa Heads and Bundaberg. This new equipment is expected to meet demand for an average period of two years.

They are then submitted through my Private Secretary for my approval. Similar arrangements apply to answers prepared in the Australian Taxation Office and the Australian Bureau of Statistics.

Housing Funds
(Question No. 5007)
Mr Uren asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 7 September 1982:
(I) Which institutions lending for housing will gain access to additional funds as a result of the 1982-83 Budget decision to release I per cent from Statutory Reserve Deposits.
(2) What will be the cost of these funds to these institutions.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) I refer the Honourable member to my statement of 9 November 1982 in which I announced that the Government has decided to defer for the time being its request to the Reserve Bank to effect an SRD release of I per cent or some $300m.

Nurse Education and Training
(Question No. 5119)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister representing the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 21 September 1982:
(I) How many students are enrolled in courses in basic nurse training at colleges of advanced education by (a) college and (b) year of course.
(2) Has the Government abandoned the target of 2200 students taking these courses by 1985, as recommended by the 1978 report of the Committee of Inquiry to the Tertiary Education Commission into nurse education and training.
(3) Has there been any evaluation of such courses; if so, what are the major conclusions.
Mr Fife-The Minister for Education has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1)-
BASIC COLLEGE NURSING ENROLMENTS, 1982

Information concerning students enrolled by year of course is not collected by the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission.
(2) In considering the Report of the Committee of Inquiry into Nurse Education and Training, the Commonwealth Government agreed that the existing pilot courses in basic nursing should be maintained for evaluation purposes. It subsequently announced that it would support a further 350 college places for basic nursing; these additional places allowed the establishment of two new college-based courses in Queensland and Tasmania.
The Commonwealth Government did not accept the Committee of Inquiry's target figure of 2,200 college basic nursing places by 1985.

(3) Several evaluations of basic college nursing courses have been, and are being, conducted under the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission's Evaluations and Investigations Program. The completed studies are:
Lindsay D. Mackay, et aI, An Evaluation of a CollegeBased Basic Course in Nursing, August 1981;
John McArthur, et aI., The First Working Year in Nursing: An Evaluation of a Group of College Graduates, November 1981.
A third study of Victorian college graduates is due for completion in 1982-83. This study uses the methodology developed for the original study, but examines a much larger sample.
A comparative evaluation of the nursing performance of graduates fo the Western Australian Institute of Technology is nearing completion, and the final report of the study is expected later this year.
A major longitudinal study of college-trained nurses in New South Wales is being conducted, and is due for completion in 1986.
The findings of the small studies completed to date in Victoria appear to indicate only minor differences in the performances of college-trained and hospital-trained nurses:
(a) college-trained nurses feel some lack of practical skills, but these skills are quickly acquired;
(b) there is some evidence to suggest that college-trained nurses perform better in interpersonal areas of nursing;
(c) the nursing performance of college graduates tends to compare favourably with the performance of hospitaltrained nurses practising outside their training hospitals.

Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program
(Question No, 5232)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport and Construction, upon notice, on 12 October 1982:
(I) What information can be provided on the distribution of funds in the Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program in 1982-83 and 1983-84 by (a) each State and Territory, (b) each road category of assistance and (c) any other category of assistance.
(2) By what means is it proposed to provide funding for improvements to urban public transport services.
(3) What types of projects will be eligible for assistance under the urban public transport program.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) and (b) Allocation of funds to the States and the Northern Territory to be made under the Australian Bicentennial Road Development (ABRD) Program has not yet been completely finalised. National roads funds will be apportioned between the States and the Northern Territory in accordance with Commonwealth priorities, having regard to the works programs submitted for Commonwealth approval. Funds for road categories other than national roads will be distributed between the States and the Northern Territory in accordance with the proportional distribution of funds for the respective categories provided in the Roads Grants Act 1981.
An indicative distribution of ABRD funds between the States, the Northern Territory and road categories for the years 1982-83 and 1983-84 is set out in the table below.
(I) (c), (2) and (3) The enabling legislation for the ABRD Program also provides that moneys allocated for the construction of urban arterial roads may be used for the funding of urban public transport capital projects if a State or the Northern Territory so chooses. Urban public transport capital projects will be eligible for assistance under the ABRD Program provided the Minister is satisfied that the project would result in the reduction of traffic on or wear and tear affecting the State's urban arterial roads and provided that a reasonable balance is maintained in meeting the needs of urban roads and urban public transport.
Taxation: Australian Residents Employed by Foreign Companies in Australia
(Question No. 5245)
Mr Beazley asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 13 October 1982:

(I) What steps are taken to ensure that Australian residents employed by foreign companies operating in Australia are paid their wages and salaries in Australia and thereby become subject to Australian taxation requirements.
(2) Is the Commissioner of Taxation investigating any operations in Australia where Australian citizens or residents have been offered the option of having their wages and salaries paid into bank accounts in countries with a low rate of income tax and taxation agreements with Australia to prevent double taxation on income.
(3) Has the Commissioner of Taxation expressed any concern in this regard with foreign-owned fishing companies who have an operational base in Australia.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) Generally speaking there is no legal requirement that Australian residents employed in Australia by foreign companies operating in Australia be paid their salaries or wages in Australia. The Commissioner of Taxation has advised, however, that the view is taken that salaries and wages of Australian residents employed in Australia by a foreign company have their source in Australia and are therefore assessable to Australian tax whether or not they are paid in Australia. In such cases the employer is required to make tax instalment deductions at the prescribed rates. Inspections are made of employers' records to ensure that they are complying with the PAYE provisions of the income tax law.
(3) The Commissioner has not expressed any general concern. He is, of course, obliged by section 16 of the Income Tax Assessment Act to preserve confidentiality in respect of specific cases.

Vegetable Production in Tasmania
(Question No. 5354)
Mr Burr asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 19 October 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a statement by Mr Les Richardson, Chairman of the Vegetable Council of the Tasmanian Farmers and Graziers Association, claiming that there has been a substantial cut-back in the plantings of green beans, peas, cauliflower and broccoli in Tasmania; if so, can he say whether the cut-backs are associated with negotiations with the New Zealand Government for closer economic relations.
(2) Can he say whether General Jones Ltd or Gordon Edgell Pty Ltd or any of their affiliated companies have vegetable processing operations in New Zealand.
(3) Can he say whether the reduced vegetable production in Tasmania n,ight be taken up by increased imports from New Zealand.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (3) The Department of Primary Industry has made enquiries into reports of some reduction in the area of vegetables contracted for processing purposes in Tasmania. The Department is satisfied that there is no link between the changes in the area contracted for vegetables in Tasmania and the arrangements for closer economic relations between Australia and New Zealand.
Most vegetable products of importance for Tasmania are already free of duty or subject only to the minimal tariffs if imported from New Zealand. In these circumstances the agreement achieved with New Zealand for an accelerated removal of its export subsidy arrangements for peas, beans, sweet corn and potato products and for a phasing out of export subsidies on all other products from 1985 to 1987, should result in an improvement in the relative competitiveness of Tasmania's vegetable growing and processing industries vis-a-vis New Zealand.
(2) I am advised that the only affiliation between Australian and New Zealand vegetable processors is the operation of a single frozen vegetable processing plant by Petersville Australia Limited in New Zealand through its international branch, Petersville International Limited. The plant is situated in Blenheim in the South Island and I understand is geared mainly to supplying the New Zealand market and export markets other than Australia.
Population Estimates
(Question No. 5411)
Dr Klugman asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 20 October 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to 'Population estimates and projections of local government areas in Western Sydney: an analysis of the issues' by Donald Perlgut of the Western Sydney Regional Organisation of Councils.

(2) Will he ask the Australian Bureau of Statistics to commenton it.
(3) Will he ask the Bureau to undertake a study of underenumeration in census data associated with non-English speaking residents, especially in the Fairfield City Council area.
(4) Is there any substance to the claim on page 16 of the paper that Fairfield City Council has forgone $135,000 of Commonwealth revenue funds during the last 5 years because of under-estimation of population.
(5) If the claim is correct can an adjustment be made by way of an ex-gratia payment or an adjustment in the next allocation.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The Australian Bureau of Statistics (ABS) is currently investigating some of the matters raised in the paper, and will provide comments directly to the Western Sydney Regional Organisation of Councils in the near future.
(3) No. I am informed that the ABS conducted an Australia-wide evaluation survey soon after the 1981 Census for the purpose of measuring under-enumeration in the Census. An analysis of the data from this survey is understood to have shown that, overall, there is no significant difference in census under-enumeration between Australian-born and overseas-born persons.
I am also informed that special efforts were made in the 1981 Census - in the Fairfield City Council area as well as other areas with large numbers of migrants--to produce an accurate enumeration of persons born overseas. These efforts are considered to have been successful as the results of the evaluation survey show that only a very small percentage of persons was missed in the Census.
(4) As far as the Commonwealth's local government tax sharing funds are concerned, the allocation bet ween local authorities within a State is related, among other things, to the population of each authority. Since estimates of the population in the Fairfield City Council area would need to be compared with estimates of the population derived on the same basis in other local government jurisdictions in New South Wales, it is not possible to comment on the substance of the claim concerning $135,000 foregone by the Fairfield City Council.
(5) Under the local government tax sharing arrangemens, the Commonwealth provides funds to each State government on the basis of a set formula. Each State is then responsible for the allocation of the funds between the local authorities within its borders. The question of any additional payments to individual local authorities is therefore a matter for consideration by the State government concerned.

Employment Statistics
(Question No. 5561)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 27 October'1982:
(I) When will the figures for employment derived from the Commonwealth Census of 30 June 1981 be published.
(2) In the event that the figures are published before an answer is provided to part (I) of this question what (a) are the numbers of persons employed in manufacturing in each Commonwealth Electoral Division and (b) percentage of the total labour force in each Electoral Division does this figure constitute.
(3) What are the (a) national, (b) state and (c) territorial statistics for employment in manufacturing expressed (i) in figures and (ii) as a percentage of the total workforce in each case.

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Employment figures from the 1981 Census have been available, on microfiche, since July 1982.
(2) Statistics on the numbers of persons employed in manufacturing industry in each Commonwealth Electoral Division (CEO) at 30 June 1981 are included in microfiche batch 81.208, 'Small Area Data-Commonwealth Electoral Divisions', a copy of which is available in the Parliamentary Library. These fiche also contain information on the total labour force in each CEO. The percentage of the total labour force in each CEO, employed in manufacturing, can be derived from these figures.
(3) Statistics on the numbers of persons employed in manufacturing industry at the national, State and Territory levels are included in microfiche batch 81.303, 'Small Area Data-States and Australia'. These fiche also contain information on the total labour force at the national, State and Territory levels. The percentage of the total labour force in the States. Territories and Australia employed in manufacturing can be derived from these figures.
Unemployment Rates in Ethnic Communities
(Question No. 5588)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 28 October 1982:
What surveys have been carried out by the Australian Bureau of Statistics, or what other statistical material is available to the Bureau, which compares unemployment rates in various ethnic communities in Australia, for example (a) Yugoslav, (b) Greek, (c) Italian, (d) Maltese, (e) Polish, (f) German, (g) Dutch, (h) Jewish, (i) Lebanese, (k) Vietnamese and (I) Chinese.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Statistics of unemployment rates classified by country of birth are compiled from the Bureau's monthly labour force survey and published in 'The Labour Force, Australia' (Catalogue No. 6203.0). Unemployment rates for persons born in Yugoslavia, Greece, Italy, Malta, Poland, Germany, the Netherlands and Lebanon are shown in this publication. Estimates for persons born in Vietnam or China are not available, nor is information available from the labour force survey on the Jewish community.
Informa'ion on unemployment rates by country of birth is also available from the 1981 Census of Population and Housing; see table 7 in microfiche batch 81.501, a copy of which is available in the Parliamentary Library. As regards the Jewish community, information that may be of interest will be a cross-classificatory table of religion and labour force status, expected to become available for the whole of Australia towards the end of 1983.

Concatenated Order of Hoo-Hoo
(Question No. 5717)
